KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
PART I. SECTOR BACKGROUND, ISSUES, AND STRATEGIES
A. Economic and Sectoral Background
1.1   The Kyrgyz Republic is a small landlocked country in Central Asia with a population of 4.5
million people. It is located in a mountainous area and has common borders with Kazakstan, China,
Tajikistan and Uzbekistan. The country became an independent state in 1991 as a result of the
disintegration of the Soviet Union. Since then it has been developing as a secular state and attempting to
implement market reforms. Price liberalization has, to a large extent, been completed and external trade
has become almost free of major restrictions. Similarly, the foreign exchange regime and interest rates
have been fully liberalized. Privatization and enterprise restructuring are underway, with the state
reducing direct involvement in economic activity, domestic production and distribution.
1.2   The Kyrgyz Republic is among the countries most adversely affected by the transition, which has
had a significant negative impact on the health status of the population. Poverty is a prevalent and
persistent problem. Even before the disintegration of the Soviet Union in 1991, about one third of the
population had a "socially unacceptable standard of living". Much of the existing poverty in the country
is, therefore, inherited and structural. According to the 1993 Kyrgyz Living Standards Survey, 40 percent
of households then lived in poverty with 25 percent classified as very poor. There were significant
disparities in the extent of poverty among the populations of different regions; farnilies headed by people
of different age groups; rural and urban populations; families of entrepreneurs and families of non-
entrepreneurs; employed and unemployed; families with income generated by employment and families
with income based on the provisions of social safety net; as well as among families with many children
and families with only a few children in the rural areas.
1.3   The level of financing of broad social programs inherited from the Soviet system was at an
unsustainable level. Following independence, in order to stabilize the economy the Kyrgyz Government
allowed social expenditures to fall from nearly 17 percent of GDP in 1990 to 9 percent in 1993, although
by 1994, the figure had increased to 14 percent. The health expenditures followed the same pattern,
dropping from 3.7 percent of GDP to 2.4, and then increased again to 3.6. Social assistance expenditures
were most affected, falling from 6.1 percent of GDP in 1991 to 3.4 percent in 1994. The enterprises,
which used to be major providers of subsidized social services, have encountered severe financial
difficulties and are now forced to divest their social assets.
1.4    World Bank studies show that while in 1994 local governments covered 84 percent of the health
expenditures and 74 percent of the expenditures on education, the current intergovernmental system has
several features that could jeopardize the social safety net including: (i) revenue sharing arrangements
which give subnational governments limited incentive to increase their tax efforts; (ii) a transfer system
which provides soft budget constraints for subnational governments and reduces incentives to cut
expenditures and/or increase the revenue collection efforts; (iii) local tax administrations which might be
more loyal to the local level with a potential non-compliance; (iv) limited correspondence of expenditures
and revenues, which forces local governments to accumulate large payment arrears; and (v) full
expenditure autonomy at the local level, which leads to ad hoc expenditure cuts or large arrears for social
expenditures, especially in the poorer regions.



2
1.5    Although redefining the role of the state has been successful in the Kyrgyz Republic with respect
to its withdrawal from micro-management and liberalizing the economy, the Government now has an
even greater challenge to create new institutions which will effectively provide for instances where market
forces alone cannot cope. The social sectors, and the health system, in particular, require a careful
institutional restructuring while the provision of services cannot be interrupted. The sensitivity and
complexity of issues involved in health reform in the Kyrgyz Republic under conditions of severe
economic crisis present a strong case for the Bank's participation.
1.6    The Kyrgyz health care financing and delivery system is a vestige of the centrally planned
national health service system of the Former Soviet Union (FSU). Such systems performed well in termis
of guaranteeing access to health care and promoting a relatively equitable distribution of health resources.
These systems were successful in promoting certain types of preventive services such as immunizations
and providing access to care in rural areas, and, until recently, resulted in relatively good health outcomes
given the amounts of money spent. However, with the epidemiological transition and the resulting
changing composition of illness from infectious diseases to chronic conditions, system performance has
been disappointing and inefficiencies, both clinical and economic, along with the dismal economic
situation in all FSU countries have resulted in very limited gains, and, indeed in some areas, in declines
in the health status of the population when compared to either western or other middle income countries.
1.7    The proposed Health Sector Reform Project in the Kyrgyz Republic is designed to assist the
Kyrgyz Government in the reform of its health care financing and delivery system. The Project takes
as given the underlying economic situation of the country, the basic epidemiological status of the
population, and the inherent strengths and weaknesses in the current health care financing and delivery
system. A set of health reformn goals have been set forth in the Government's MANAS Health Care
Reform Program', and this proposed IDA funded multi-component project targets the major causes of
death and disability as well as the basic underlying economic and clinical inefficiencies in the current
health care financing and delivery system. Explicit in the Project's design is the introduction of modem
clinical practice methods and provider payment reforms separating purchase and payment of health
services from delivery of care. These changes would improve clinical practice, target the country's major
health problems, and result in a reconfiguration of the service delivery system which will promote
efficient use of scarce health resources. Concomitantly, the Project is designed to preserve and strengthen
the major positive features of the previous centrally planned system, in particular universal access to care
and a relatively equitable distribution of health resources.
1.8    The components of the Health Sector Reform Project are developed on the basis of these reform
goals, weaknesses in the current system, the underlying epidemiological patterns, the current
configuration of the service delivery system, economic characteristics of the country, and the
Government's health priorities.
B. Epidemiological Trends
1.9    The Kyrgyz Republic's epidemiological profile and underlying health status compares quite
favorably to many middle income countries. Much of this positive performance can be attributed to the
strengths of the previous system in terms of widespread availability of basic services, such as children's
vaccinations, and universal access to care. However, over the last several years deaths from infectious
diseases tend to be higher, while several indicators of health status have been stagnant or declining.
1/     The MANAS Health Care Reform Program was a 1994/95 collaboration between the Government, WHO
and other donors which examined the health status of the Kyrgyz Republic and recommended future
reforms and priorities.



3
Overall, cardiovascular diseases are the most common cause of death in both sexes. For males, accidents
and intoxications, respiratory diseases, cancer, and infectious and parasitic diseases are the major causes
of death. For females, after cardiovascular disease, the most common causes of death are respiratory
diseases, cancer, accidents and intoxications, and infectious and parasitic diseases. The system has been
particularly unsuccessful in dealing with chronic conditions and the underlying epidemiological factors
affecting adults. Moreover, there are also major weaknesses in the women's reproductive (including the
matemal) health system, and the system's outdated medical practice patterns are wasting resources and
appear to be unable to cope with major increases in the incidence of highly infectious diseases such as
tuberculosis (TB) and sexually transmitted diseases (STDs).
Mortality
1.10   While the crude death rate dropped substantially over the past 15 years, it has increased since
1991 from 6.9 deaths per thousand population to 8.3 in 1994, comparable to the 9.4 rate in the westem
industrialized Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) countries. The crude
birth rate has declined over the past 15 years from 30 births per thousand population in 1978 to 24.3 in
1994, but is still almost double the industrialized country rate. The rapid drop in the birth rate between
1992 and 1994 (28.5 to 24.3) likely reflects the current socio-economic upheaval (Table 1.1).

al 1992
1.11    The infant mortality rate declined from 43 deaths per thousand live births in 1978 to 29.4 in
1994, still a rate three times higher than that in the industrialized countries. Almost half of all infant
deaths are due to acute respiratory infections (ARI), 26 percent are due to perinatal causes, and another
10 percent are due to severe diarrhea. In all, 60 percent of infant deaths are preventable. Since the
percentages of low birthweight and premature births are comparable to those found in industrialized
countries, it is apparent that poor maternal care is a major causal factor for these high rates of preventable
deaths. Over 60 percent of the deaths of children from 1 to 5 years are due to the same causes.
Accidents are the major cause of death for children aged 5-14, although ARI still accounts for almost 15
percent of deaths in this age group.
1.12   Life expectancy at birth has been relatively stable over the last several reported years (e.g. 1990-
1992). Unfortunately, the last year with accurate data is 1992, since funds were not available to do the



4
computations in 1993 (although computations will be done for 1994). In 1992 the Kyrgyz Republic's life
expectancy at birth for males of 64 years was 9 years below the OECD average and the 72 year figure
for women was 7 years below the OECD average (Table 1.1). The 1994 figures should provide some
indication of whether the current economic crisis has significantly affected the longevity of the population
as is the case in Russia.
1.13   The main causes of death of young adults 15-24 years of age are motor vehicle accidents,
suicides, and homicides. However, chronic respiratory diseases are the leading cause of death for women
15-24, and ischemic heart disease is the second most important cause of death for women. Ischemic heart
disease is the leading cause of death for both men and women at age 35 and above. Various cancers are
the second leading cause of death in women 35-54, while accidents, homicides and alcohol intoxication
are the other major causes of death. Chronic respiratory diseases are also an important cause of death
for both men and women in the 35-54 age range. For both males and females age 55 and over, after
ischemic heart disease, chronic respiratory infections and cerebrovascular diseases are the main causes
of death.
Morbidity
1.14   Specific diseases, many of which result in illness not death but nevertheless place significant
burdens on the health system, cause significant losses of productivity, and result in major health risks
being imposed on the rest of the population. Analyses of morbidity data are difficult due to the lack of
comprehensive information and the questionable reliability of reported data. Available data indicate two
areas which show large increases in incidence of morbidity and pose major health threats to the
population at large: TB and STDs.
1.15   Sexually transmitted diseases not only pose major public health problems, but also have
detrimental effects on women's reproductive health. Reported cases of syphilis were 22 per one hundred
thousand population in 1994, reflecting a ten-fold increase over the 1992 rate of 2.3. While reported
gonorrhea cases dropped from 44 per hundred thousand population in 1992 to 37.9 in 1993, there is
strong evidence that detection rates are dropping as people have stopped coming to Government facilities
for treatment. While there are no officially documented AIDS cases, the current socio-economic situation
increases the risk of a significant spread of this disease in a health system that is ill prepared from a
public health or financial perspective to deal with such an epidemic.
1.16   The tuberculosis situation in the Kyrgyz Republic is also grave. Even during the Soviet period
tuberculosis case notification and death rates were among the highest of the entire Soviet Union. Since
1989, case rates have increased annually at an average rate of three percent, and mortality has increased
annually by eight percent. Taking into account under-reporting, the estimated incidence could be as high
as 70 cases per hundred thousand and that of sputum smear positive cases as high as 35 per one hundred
thousand. The largest proportion of cases still occur in the young adult age groups: the most productive
segment of the population. In 1994, over two-thirds of all cases occurred in persons under 45 years of
age. The country's TB mortality rate has doubled from 6.7 deaths per hundred thousand population in
1989 to 13.6 in 1994. In addition to the serious epidemiological situation, drug resistance levels,
particularly to the most important anti-tuberculosis drugs, are reported to be increasing from the already
high levels recorded in the mid-1980s. Moreover, treatment patterns are out of date with a heavy reliance
on inpatient treatment as opposed to newer outpatient approaches.
1.17   Utilizing available morbidity and mortality data, a burden of disease analysis was done for the
Kyrgyz Republic using a variant of the DALY (Disability Adjusted Life Years) methodology developed
for the 1993 World Development Report. The data on DALYs presented in Table 1.2 indicates the



5
priority diseases accounting for the highest loss of DALYs. It is to be noted that the DALYs to be gained
from reduction in some highly infectious conditions, such as TB (or STDs affecting women's reproductive
health), can be as much as three or four times higher than levels indicated here because of dynamic
transmission (i.e. the infection of additional persons by an infected person).

1.18   The burden of disease analysis is combined with cost-effectiveness analysis (see Part VI) to
identify those areas where the health status of the population can be promoted in the most cost-effective
way possible (i.e. the possibility of gaining the greatest number of DALYs for the lowest cost).
C. Health Care Delivery System and Resource Utilization
1.19   The Kyrgyz health system is characterized by heavy reliance on inpatient and tertiary hospital-
based care. The emphasis of the health system on costly inpatient care is evident in the large numbers
of hospital beds and physicians, and high use rates, as compared to western industrialized countries (Table
1.3). Moreover, norms on facility structural standards, on medical practice, and on staffing and salaries
perpetuate outmoded medical treatments and economic inefficiency. The number of beds per 1,000
population has declined from 12 in 1981 to 9.5 in 1994, but is still some 15 percent above the average
for OECD countries. Similarly, while hospital admissions rates have declined from 25 percent of the
population being admitted in 1987 to 18 percent in 1994, they are still 20 percent above the average in
westem industrialized countries. While the average length of hospital stay has declined by about one day
over the past 15 years to 15.6 days in 1994, slightly above the 14 day OECD average, it has increased
from a low of 15 days in 1988. The average number of inpatient days of care per year per person has
declined from 3.6 days in 1978 to 2.8 in 1994, but is still some 15 percent above the OECD average.
The hospital occupancy has declined from 90 percent in 1978 to 81 percent in 1994, clearly indicating
significant excess capacity. The Kyrgyz Republic has significantly more beds and far higher use rates
than most western industrialized countries.

1.20    Although there is an extensive network of facilities capable of rendering primary and secondary
levels of services, the bulk of health resources flow to costly inpatient care rather than to outpatient
primary care settings. The pattern of expenditures for local health budgets, for example, are heavily
skewed towards hospitals, which primarily provide inpatient care (Table 1.4).
Table 1.4: Allocation of Health Budget by Type of Facility, 1994
Type of facility          56 of Consolidated
Health Budget
Hospitals                        75.3
Polyclinics                      8.2
FAPs                             1.5
Other                            15.0
Source: Ministry of Finance
1.21    As a result, there is virtually no diagnostic capacity and equipment at lower, less costly levels
of the system. Instead of being able to treat patients at these less expensive levels, these lower levels
have typically referred patients upwards for treatment at the tertiary level. The major reductions in health
spending described below are exacerbating the problems of inappropriate referrals to higher levels of the
system as the already under-resourced lower levels are reported to be suffering from particular shortages
of consumables, especially pharmaceuticals. As a consequence, the lower facilities have greater incentive
to refer patients up the chain to the relatively less under-resourced tertiary care levels.
1.22    The number of physicians per 1,000 population has increased by some 30 percent over the past
15 years to reach 3.1 in 1994 (of which 0.45 are dentists), somewhat above the OECD average of 2.2.
More troublesome is the large number of specialists relative to generalists: some 35 percent are



7
generalists (therapists and pediatricians), whereas the ratio in many OECD countries (excluding the U.S.)
is closer to 50-50. The number of physician contacts per year is comparable to the high utilization rates
in the OECD countries. As in the case of inpatient beds, the Kyrgyz Republic has large numbers of
physicians and high utilization of physician services.
1.23   The current system of health service provision is highly specialized and fragmented. The
population of just over 4.5 million people is serviced by specialized research institutes (Infectious
Diseases, Cardiology, Oncology, Tuberculosis, Maternal and Child Health, etc.) at the tertiary level;
specialized tuberculosis and dermatovenereal hospitals and AIDS clinics at the oblast level; and a
tuberculosis dispensary at the raion level. For example, a woman is not able to go to just one primary
health facility in order to receive health care for her children and for herself. For gynecological
problems she must visit a women's consultation clinic; for contraception, a marriage and family clinic;
for venereological problems, a dermatovenereal clinic; and for AIDS testing, an AIDS clinic. While the
system is better integrated at the Feldsher/Accoucheur Posts (FAP) and ambulatory level in the rural
areas, this generally fragmented system is expensive for the country to run and inconvenient to users.
It wastes scarce resources which could be better channeled into fewer and more integrated programs.
D. Financing Health Care
Budgeting System
1.24   Health financing regulated by budgeting norms based on physical capacity (e.g. numbers of beds
or numbers of doctors) perpetuates inefficient resource use in the health sector by encouraging
overstaffing, overbedding, and increased utilization of inpatient services. Combined with the rigidly
enforced Soviet 18-line budgeting system used to pay medical care providers (hospitals and polyclinics),
there are no incentives for primary care providers to use cost-effective ambulatory treatment protocols
or to perform a "gate-keeping" function of screening inappropriate referrals. The reliance on capacity-
based budgeting and payment norms promotes inefficiency and waste. Although the recent financial crisis
in the country has weakened the application of capacity norms as the basis of budgeting, a more rational
system for reimbursing providers that encourages efficiency, as well as improved internal management,
would need to be clearly defined and instituted.
Need for National Health Accounts
1.25   Effective health policy can only be mnade on the basis of understanding the total amount of
resources being devoted to the health sector: recurrent and capital spending, spending from all public
sources including Ministries other than the Ministry of Health, enterprises, donors, and private spending
both legal and informal. National Health Accounts (NHA) provide a frarnework for classifying all
expenditures by source of funding (e.g. public and private) and by type of service. They provide the
basis for analyzing changes over time of both the total and the individual components. Ideally spending
at the oblast and local levels would be analyzed in the same manner. Such accounts are a necessary
condition for effective policymaking and are an inherent feature of the health systems of all OECD
countries. Unfortunately, the health accounting systems in FSU countries often do not lend themselves
to functional classifications or analyses. Moreover, the increasing fragmentation of the financing systems
in terms of health insurance-based models and private payments has added to the number of flows of
funds and further obfuscated the spending picture.
Levels of Public Funding
1.26   The health system in the Kyrgyz Republic, as elsewhere in the FSU, is predominantly publicly
financed.   Public funds from  general revenues are allocated through a complex system  of



8
intergovermnental tax-sharing and transfers to finance facilities through federal (Republican) and local
(oblast and raion) level health budgets. Additionally, public enterprises have financed some social
services, including health facilities.
1.27   Irrespective of the measures employed, public health spending in the Kyrgyz Republic has fallen
dramatically since 1990. As shown in Table 1.5, the decline was especially pronounced between 1990-
1993, with a slight improvement in 1994. Nevertheless, health spending in 1994 is still substantially
below the 1990 level for all measures. The ratio of public health expenditures to GDP declined from 4.2
percent of GDP in 1990 to 2.6 percent in 1993 with an increase to 3.6 percent in 1994 (the 1994 public
health number reflects only the consolidated budget, so if extra-budgetary funds and enterprise spending
were included, the ratio would be higher). The health to GDP ratio in OECD countries exceeds 8 percent
on average (Table 1.6).
1.28   Although health spending as a percentage of GDP is seen to have fallen moderately, the real
decline in public health funding is far more severe than is indicated by these figures. In real inflation
adjusted terms, GDP in 1994 was only 50 percent of the 1990 level. Similarly, 1994 real (GDP deflator
adjusted) health expenditures were only 46 percent of the 1990 level, up from 40 percent of the 1990
level in 1993. In short, there has been a 50 percent reduction in real output in the Kyrgyz Republic since
1990, accompanied by a 54 percent reduction in real public health spending. Major reductions in real
output and their associated tax revenues have caused equivalent reductions in real public health spending.
1.29   The picture is even more dismal when per person spending is converted into U.S. dollars using
purchasing power parities (i.e. exchange rates which adjust for cost of living differences across
countries). In 1990, per capita public health spending in the Kyrgyz Republic was US$156. Spending
declined to US$133 in 1991, US$74 in 1992, and US$37 in 1993, only one-fourth the 1990 level
compared to a 1992 OECD average of almost US$1,400 and spending in Turkey of US$156 (Table 1.6).
Allocation of Financial Resources
1.30   The Kyrgyz Republic has not opted for generating earmarked revenues for health through
mandated insurance, unlike some of the other FSU countries. An experiment in this regard is underway
in one of the oblasts (Issyk-kul), the provider payment reform elements of which are being expanded
under the proposed IDA Credit. Changes in medical care provider payments are needed to create
financial incentives for all providers of health services to manage care given to patients more efficiently
and cost-effectively while improving the quality of health services. Decision-making on the allocation of
resources should be decentralized so that both providers of health services and the population have more
responsibility and accountability for the efficient provision of high quality health services.
1.31   Further, the concepts of paying providers in accordance with the number of persons covered and
the types of services rendered would establish a clear relationship between the quantity and quality of the
services dispensed and the financial reimbursement received for those services, and would encourage
outpatient treatments, and reductions in the length of hospital stays and unnecessary referrals to higher
levels of care.
1.32   The advantages of applying this new method of paying health care providers include:
(i)    Ability to give more health services with current funding levels due to more efficient
allocation of existing health sector resources;



9
(ii)     Increased confidence in the health sector by businesses and the population leading to
greater willingness to invest in health;
(iii)    Increased responsibility and accountability of health providers for the outcome of health
services;
(iv)     Increased consumer participation in decisions about their health care;
(v)      Improvements in the quality of health services;
(vi)    Increased access to health services for the population; and
(vii)    Greater satisfaction and prestige for medical professionals, including  attracting  and
keeping quality professionals.

1.33    Levels of private funding.  Data from  Goskomstat based on its non-random  Family Budget
studies of 2,000 families, provide information on the out-of-pocket spending by family members on health
care. As shown in Table 1.7, data from 1989-1994 indicate that between 0.7 and 1.4 (in 1994) percent
of per family member expenditures were for health services. Taking the absolute amounts in Kyrgyz
soms and dividing by total public spending indicates that these out-of-pocket payments are equivalent to
10-15 percent of public health spending. Since infornal payments may not be correctly reported as health
spending, it is probable that the private share is somewhat higher. However, even if total (public and
private) health spending is 20 to 30 percent higher than public spending alone, spending levels are still
quite low. While informal payments may to some extent be offsetting the reductions in public health
spending, it is unlikely that they could offset the enormous decline. Likewise, aid from donors is also
offsetting these declines in health spending, but the magnitude of such assistance is quite small relative
to total health spending, perhaps on the order of three percent in 1994.

1.34    Widespread user-charges have not been introduced. The MANAS group is expected to identify
a basic package of cost-effective services that will be financed publicly with no or minimal user fees.
Beyond the package, an exemption schedule for the poor and for sufferers of diseases with catastrophic
expenses would need to be developed, as well as an administrative mechanism for collecting fees. Given
widespread poverty, already existing formal and informal private outlays and proposed changes towards
cost-efficient behavior targeting medical providers (rather than users), developing a program of user fees
is not deemed to be a priority for the health sector at the moment, nor is it likely that significant revenue
would be generated if at the same time access of the poorest groups to medical care were to be protected.



11
Impact of Funding Crisis
1.35   The current funding crisis has precipitated a series of changes that impact on all levels of the
health system. A few of these changes have been beneficial in reforming a chronically overstaffed and
overutilized health system. Others have actually reduced the quality of the service provided and seek to
undermine both the health workers' and general public's confidence in the system. It has been noted that
the population's health seeking behavior has changed in the last few years with people visiting health
facilities less often, later in the illness, or not at all. The main reasons for this change in behavior have
been the lack of drugs in the health facilities, transport problems, mass unemployment reducing demand
for sick notes, and lack of money to pay the fees for service.
1.36   The health system is severely constrained by the reduced availability of drugs, equipment,
spareparts and consumables in all health facilities, particularly at the primary health care level. Health
facilities maintain a stock of drugs for emergencies and heavy reliance is being placed on humanitarian
assistance to provide vaccinations and selective priority drugs. Such humanitarian assistance is sporadic,
however, resulting in frequent acute shortages of vaccinations and priority drugs. There are many
examples of the problems created by this drug shortage, and people are selling off assets (cows, horses)
to buy drugs, or turning to faith healers and herbal remedies. The health system is collapsing due to lack
of drugs and supplies, particularly at the primary health care level. In the current situation most patients
must provide bedding, food, drugs and syringes for themselves and cover the costs of transport. This
collapse is further exacerbated by the use of outmoded treatment regimes, which often include
polypharmacy and long periods of unnecessary hospitalization.
1.37   Emergency services have been protected so far, although health officials voice doubts about how
long this emergency cover can be maintained. Emergency surgery (i.e. cesarian sections and other
emergency obstetric care, acute appendicitis, strangulated hernia repair, acute trauma, etc.) continues but
patients must provide many items. Routine surgery (chronic hernia repair, gallbladder removal, gastric
ulcer surgery, etc.) has virtually ceased. Many patients with chronic illnesses (chronic bronchitis, angina,
diabetes, epilepsy, kidney disease, etc.) have stopped visiting health facilities because of the lack of
drugs, and the introduction of a fee for some services. The lack of essential drugs for public health
priority programns such as TB and STD control programs signal a breakdown in the basic health system.
1.38   Health worker salary levels are compressed and very low (chief doctor US$25/month, hospital
doctors US$13/month, nurses US$11/month), and payment has sometimes been delayed by several
months. Several non-cash payment systems have been introduced in different raions to compensate for
the lack of revenue flow. Health workers receive part of their salaries in the form of produce from local
enterprises (wheat, cloth etc.) or vouchers. Some health staff compensate for these low salaries by
working an extra shift in their health facilities. Many compensate by cultivating their gardens to produce
extra fruit and vegetables for home consumption or sale in the market. Some of the most talented doctors
have left to work in other jobs (mainly business), or migrated to other countries. Mass unemployment
has, however, prevented many health workers from seeking alternative employment. The health facilities
appear to be adequately staffed, despite these low salaries and long delays in payment. Many health
workers are motivated and committed to their health service system, but are finding it a challenge to
continue working without drugs for their patients.
1.39    A shortage of gasoline and spareparts have contributed to a reduction in the number of
ambulance teams, coinciding with a reduction in the public transport system. Pregnant women are now
advised to keep a stock of gasoline in their homes for the ambulance or private vehicles. Disturbingly,
some health workers report an increase in the number of home deliveries. Supervision of health staff by



12
Ministry, oblast and raion health officials remains intact, although reductions in public transport and
gasoline shortages have substantially reduced the frequency of visits.
1.40   A variety of cost-saving measures have been introduced by different health facilities to
compensate the reduction in their budgets: beds and staff have been reduced; day patient services and fee
for service have been introduced; some hospitals now grow wheat and make their own bread; hospital
pharmacies have been set up to produce infusion solutions and herbal remedies; garbage is removed by
hospital staff in preference to paying outside contractors; coal heaters have been replaced by electric
heaters; and physical therapy has partially replaced drug therapy for some ailments. The chief doctors
and their staff have shown great resourcefulness in their attempts to reduce costs, while trying to maintain
the quality of patient care.
E. The Current Status of Physical Facilities
1.41   A comprehensive review on the status of health facilities in the country, with a particular focus
on those at the PHC level, revealed that adequate funds are no longer available to pay for running and
maintaining buildings, plant and equipment making it increasingly difficult to maintain acceptable levels
of efficiency and quality of the facilities. A large part of the equipment is not working due to shortages
of spareparts and a widespread lack of maintenance. Furthermore, essential consumables needed to
operate the equipment are not available or in short supply, seriously detracting from the quality and
effectiveness of the medical services being provided.
1.42   Even though as a result of the mentioned shortages and inefficiencies buildings and equipment
are not adequate to respond to medical needs, they also represent a fixed cost to the Government in terms
of personnel and energy consumption. A reform program aimed at improving efficiency and decreasing
waste will require the closing of some facilities and the adaptation, remodeling and re-equipping of other
establishments. At present, there are very few norms and standards regarding physical infrastructure that
could respond to current needs. With respect to the human resources that would be needed, while in the
past there was apparently a well functioning hierarchy of technical and administrative expertise to support
the Ministry of Health's investment activities, including engineers, architects and builders, this is no
longer the case. The few remaining members in the Office of Capital Investments are not well trained
to manage the technical changes that will be required to reform the network and to bring the facilities up
to modern standards, while achieving savings. The Central Office for maintenance has been completely
disbanded.
1.43   At present there is no overview of the status of buildings and equipment and it is not possible to
make overall plans and establish priorities for reform and maintenance activities of the whole public
health network. The scarce resources available should be used for rehabilitation and for organizing an
updated system of maintenance, once decisions are taken on partial or total closing of some facilities and
on remodeling work and installation of new equipment. For this change of focus, however, a significant
reorganization is required including retraining of a small core of technical staff in planning and managing
the reform and the improvement of the existing health physical infrastructure network. This may also
require contracting of private sector expertise.
F. The Current Status of Pharmaceuticals
1.44   Prior to independence, The Kyrgyz Republic received most of its pharmaceuticals from the FSU.
With the breakup of the Soviet Union, pharmaceutical availability was severely disrupted. With the
collapse of the economy, the country cannot afford to buy drugs at market prices. To compound these
problems, state monopolies on procurement and distribution, outmoded storage facilities, and outmoded



13
lists of clinically inappropriate drugs have all exacerbated the problems of pharmaceutical supply. The
Kyrgyz Republic was also left without a quality control system for drugs because determination of safety
and efficacy of drugs was done by the Ministry of Health of the USSR in Moscow. To make matters
worse, the high costs of outpatient drugs result in individuals being inappropriately hospitalized in order
to receive free pharmaceuticals. The situation is so severe that for most non-emergency cases, hospitals
can no longer afford to provide drugs, and individuals frequently must provide their own drugs in order
to receive appropriate inpatient treatment. An adequate supply of essential drugs is critical to the
effectiveness of any health care system, but it is particularly important at a time when resources are being
shifted from inpatient to outpatient facilities and from curative to preventive care. However, in order to
assure an adequate supply of essential drugs, it is not enough simply to finance the purchase of additional
medicines. Without fundamental improvements in pharmaceutical management, much of the potential
benefit of increased physical supply is likely to be squandered, and none of the benefit is likely to be
sustainable once the additional funds have been spent. Moreover, the danger of ineffective, or even
unsafe, medicines being supplied may be rising, in light of the worsening economic conditions, the
growing shortage of drugs, and the unscrupulousness of low-quality manufacturers. A more serious
source of waste is undisciplined, cost-ineffective public spending. Despite cuts in Government funds for
drugs, the funds that are available still are being used to buy an excessively large number of drugs, many
of which have never been proven to be effective. Expensive branded drugs are sometimes purchased
instead of less expensive, therapeutically equivalent generic products. And, because a large number of
different drugs are purchased in relatively small quantities, the demand for many important, widely used
medicines often is not met, while the price that must be paid to obtain these drugs is higher than would
be the case if larger quantities of fewer drugs were purchased through competitive bidding. Reform of
pharmaceutical procurement and management is critical. Lists of essential clinically effective drugs based
on the recommendations of WHO must be established, and effective procurement and distribution
networks must be put in place. The safety and efficacy of the country's pharmaceutical supply must be
assured.
G. Need for Long-Term Reform
1.45   Based on the preceding analysis, the following are identified as the main areas of the health
system where problems exist:
(a)    overemphasis on inpatient and tertiary care, with poor diagnostic capacity, treatment
protocols, pharmaceutical supplies and facilities at ambulatory levels;
(b)    federal norms promoting outdated clinical practices and inefficient facility configurations;
(c)    large numbers of low quality medical treatments, and an excess of hospital beds;
(d)    rigid 18-line budget procedures for paying polyclinics, hospitals and salaries for all
medical care personnel, which provide no incentives for economic efficiency in resource
use or referral patterns and perpetuate imbalances toward inpatient care;
(e)    outmoded health programs in a number of important areas such as maternal and child
health (MCH), TB, STDs, and adult health;
(f)   lack of an up-to-date essential drug list, poorly structured monopolistic state procurement,
storage, and distribution systems, and shortages of essential drugs;
(g)    limited technology and shortages of basic equipment and supplies; and



14
(h)    history of chronic underfunding exacerbated by the current economic situation.
1.46   As a consequence, the quality of health services and effective access to these services has declined
in recent years (as evident in falling utilization rates).  Total resource availability has declined
considerably and the remaining resources are utilized in a highly inefficient way. The incentive structure
underlying the resource allocation system in the sector contain limited incentives for inducing cost-
effective behavior by medical care providers.
1.47   The issue in the Kyrgyz Republic's health system in the short-run, therefore, is to make the best
possible use of the limited resources available, while laying the basis for future enhancements in areas
such as quality assurance, accreditation of health facilities and new forms of financial resource
mobilization. The major areas of reform in this first stage are identified as:
(a)    emphasizing cost-effectiveness criteria in public financing of health service delivery: this
will require that resources are channeled to diseases which have a high burden and which
can be treated through cost-effective ambulatory protocols;
(b)    re-orientation of resource use towards ambulatory primary care and away from inpatient
and tertiary care: this will require strengthening of ambulatory care through investments
in new ambulatory treatment protocols, diagnostic capacity, supply of essential drugs, and
rehabilitation of facilities for expanded ambulatory care;
(c)    ensuring that the focus on cost-effective ambulatory care is sustainable through changes
in the incentive structure facing medical care providers: this will require separation of
purchasing and provision of services with the introduction of a new payment system for
providers, such as a capitation-based payment system, which will place responsibility for
cost containment on the providers and will encourage use of cost-effective ambulatory
protocols and avoidance of inappropriate referrals, while maintaining pre-agreed,
specified service quality levels;
(d)    introducing mechanisms for cost-effective procurement of major consumables, especially
pharmaceuticals: this will require identification of clinically effective and cost-effective
essential drugs and the strengthening of pharmaceuticals regulatory bodies; and
(e)    facility and staff rationalization for the health sector in order to realize savings from the
shift in service provision from inpatient to ambulatory methods: this will require
closure/consolidation of facilities and re-training/release of some staff, especially at the
tertiary level and at inviable small inpatient facilities.
1.48   These reforms would be expected to generate structural efficiency (i.e. achievement of health
gains with least cost), sustain these cost-savings over time, as well as achieving health gains, ceteris
paribus, through improvements in the effectiveness and quality of services provided.
H. Government's Goals and Policies for the Health Sector
Goals
1.49   Like other member nations of the World Health Organization, the Kyrgyz Republic has developed
health goals that it wants to achieve by the year 2000. The Kyrgyz Republic's State Program For A
Healthy Nation Targets For the Year 2000 include: (a) reduction of Infant Mortality by 25 percent; (b)
reduction of Child Mortality under five by one-third; (c) reduction of Maternal Mortality by 25 percent;



15
(d) reduction of Cardiovascular Diseases and Cancer by 15 percent; (e) reduction of Mortality by
Diabetes by 20 percent; (f) reduction of the Crude Mortality Rate by 15 percent; (g) reduction of the
incidence of tuberculosis by 10 percent; and (h) reduction of the incidence of sexually transmitted diseases
by 50 percent. Within the context of the MANAS Health Care Reform Program, the Government is
attempting to develop a rationalistic framework for developing solutions to these serious health problems
of the country, which have been exacerbated by the difficulties of the transition. By December 31, 1996
MOH would, as part of project implementation, present to the Association for review and comments, the
MANAS Health Care Reform Program and a schedule of rationalization of health facilities and thereafter
carry out the program in accordance with that schedule, taking into account the comments of the
Association.
Policy
1.50   Before negotiations the Government provided the International Development Association (IDA),
hereafter referred to as the Association, with a draft policy statement outlining its health sector reform
strategy. This policy statement was reviewed and discussed during negotiations. Signature of the policy
statement in a form acceptable to the Association was a condition of Board Presentation. The Prime
Minister transmitted the policy statement with a signed letter, dated April 5, 1996, which summarized
the policy's main points. A copy of this letter is contained in Annex 1. The full policy statement and
letter in both Russian and English are contained in Annex 15. The statement contains undertakings:
(a)    That during the execution of the Project the level of annual State budgetary expenditures
for the health sector will remain executed at a level, not less in real, inflation-adjusted
terms than such total executed expenditures in 1994. Savings resulting from carrying out
the Project will be put back into the health sector.
(b)    That the health sector needs to be rationalized and modernized and a first step will be
taken during the time the Project is implemented by merging specialty departments and
health facilities; increase the emphasis on primary and outpatient care and reduction of
health personnel, hospitals and hospital beds. As part of this restructuring,
(i)    The STD and AIDS programs will be integrated in other primary care programs
and new treatment methods and protocols will be introduced to enhance
ambulatory treatment of sexually transmitted diseases.
(ii)   If results of the pilot projects on ambulatory treatment of TB are satisfactory,
introduce ambulatory treatment as standard treatment nationwide; reallocate
current tuberculosis specialists from tuberculosis in-patient facilities to primary
health care facilities; and phased closure of TB sanatoria in the country.
(iii)   Emphasis will shift from new construction to rehabilitation of health facilities and
from specialty hospitals to integrated hospitals.
(iv)   Allocations in the local and national budgets for repair and maintenance of health
facilities will be maintained at the same executed real level of the 1994 budgets
and further that these allocations will be utilized only for the purposes intended.
(c)    That the population will have a free choice of health care provider/physician.



16
(d)    That the Borrower will take all necessary legal, organizational and financial steps to
ensure that only drugs that are safe and proven effectiveness will be manufactured,
distributed, sold, and used in the Kyrgyz Republic; that the borrower will establish an
Essential Drugs Program, including a limited list of essential drugs; that the Goverrnent
intends to require that government funds be used, directly or indirectly, to finance the
purchase of only drugs on the approved essential drugs list; and that the Government will
continue to eliminate obstacles to the growth of a financially stable, well-regulated system
of private pharmacies and pharmaceutical distributors.
I. Other Donor Activities and Coordination
1.51   The major donor agencies working in the Kyrgyz Republic are USAID, UNICEF, WHO, UNFPA
and UNDP. USAID, UNICEF and UNDP all have small country offices in Bishkek. USAID has a
regional office in Almaty and the UNICEF country office is supervised by the Central Asian office of
UNICEF in Islamabad, Pakistan. UNFPA plans to set up a regional office with a regional advisor. GTZ,
the German Goverrunent's technical assistance agency, and DANIDA, the Danish Govermnent's
development agency also have small offices with a resident country program officer. ODA's Know How
Fund is also very active in the health sector in the country, and is expected to provide technical assistance
to the Government to rationalize health facilities in Bishkek city. Many smaller agencies are working on
emergency food programs (Red Cross, Mercy Corps International, etc.) Annex 14 provides a summary
of agencies involved in health reform activities, drug programs, children's health, women's reproductive
health, and a description of the coordination of these activities and the proposed Project. Collaboration
agreements have been reached with UNICEF on the Project's support for acute respiratory infections and
the control of diarrheal disease (ARI/CDD), and GTZ on activities related to the promotion of adult
health and lifestyles.



17
PART II. LESSONS LEARNED FROM PREVIOUS BANK EXPERIENCE
2.1    A review of the analysis of medical systems performed in Russia and the other FSU countries
reveals a pattern which is symptomatic of the legacy of Soviet medical practices. First, the lack of
discipline inherent in any system where cost is not a factor produced a medical system highly skewed to
tertiary level care and hospitalization in specialized and functionally independent facilities with staff and
overhead costs out of proportion to patient load. Incentives were biased away from services towards
facilities and hospital stays, and providers were paid on a fixed salary. This system has proved not only
to be unaffordable under the present fiscal austerity, but unworkable in a market economy. The provider
payments component of the Project proposes to deal directly with these issues. Second, lack of exposure
to and incorporation of western medical practices has resulted in medical practices and protocols which
are not in accord with modern developments. The current cadre of medical professionals need to have
their training upgraded and expanded. A significant effort is undertaken in the Project to introduce
modern methods, materials, publications, ambulatory techniques, and recent research findings to the
primary health care sector. Third, the level and quality of physical construction in the sector has
emphasized new construction almost to the virtual exclusion of maintenance and upgrading of present
facilities. Community involvement has been generally absent. The facility rehabilitation component
focuses on establishing methods and administrative steps to introduce the concept of estate management
with community participation in primary health facilities. Fourth, throughout the FSU the actual level
of financing directed to health has suffered as the federal level has divested responsibility and financial
support to the oblast and raion authorities, and central financial authorities have been under pressure from
the IMF with regard to fiscal deficits. There is strong pressure on medical authorities to rationalize
expenditures within a shrinking annual allocation. The Project seeks to establish a "floor" for budgetary
allocations to health, as well as encourage the hard task of reducing physical and staffing levels to fit
current needs without reducing quality of services. The Kyrgyz Republic, in many respects of size,
commitment to reform, and ability to make decisions offers an excellent opportunity to attack these issues
and provide lessons for its larger neighbors who are all facing the same challenges.
2.2    With respect to implementation this is the first lending operation for the health sector in the
Kyrgyz Republic. In general, the Kyrgyz Republic's health sector shares the same characteristics of other
FSU countries, i.e. the dispersion of responsibilities among various levels of Government, lack of
coordination among various entities involved in health care, inexperience with Bank procedures, weak
implementation capacity, and a resistance to credit-funded external technical assistance. In the area of
pharmaceuticals, a Rehabilitation Credit had an original allocation of US$1.5 million to finance imported
drugs, but due to lengthy delays and changing priorities in Government the preparation and finalization
of list of drugs to be purchased, combined with offers of donations from donors, resulted in only
US$100,000 of the total amount allocated being used. For small-scale rehabilitation of facilities, the
Social Safety Net Project has almost completed rehabilitation on 48 sites around the country. The lessons
learned would be applied to the rehabilitation activities under the proposed Health Project, including the
early refocusing of MOH on rehabilitation and supervision of works, together with outside engineers, and
development of simplified bidding documents for rehabilitation works. Finally, the Project Coordination
Unit of the Social Safety Net Project has gained valuable experience with Bank procedures, and the PCU
would perform, on a contract basis, the procurement, disbursement and reporting functions for the
MOH.
2.3    The use of the Disability Adjusted Life Years (DALY) methodology presented in the World Bank
1993 World Development Report provided an excellent tool of analysis to select those medical conditions
which had the highest priority, and to choose only those interventions which are both cost-effective and
can be provided in an outpatient care setting. Special efforts have been made to incorporate the lessons
of other donors, and one component - provider payments - is a direct extension of the USAID-funded



18
demonstration project in Issyk-kul, which is part of a larger effort involving several countries of the FSU.
The lessons learned throughout the FSU will be incorporated as they emerge. The Bank's Poverty
Assessment and Strategy for the Kyrgyz Republic calls for the major causes of death and disability to be
targeted, and the basic underlying inefficiencies which remain in the health care system to be addressed.
In particular, modern clinical practices will need to be introduced, along with new incentive-based
payment mechanisms for medical service providers. The goals and strategy of the proposed Project are
entirely congruent with that strategy, and with the Government's health priorities.



19
PART III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives and Concept
3.1    The objectives of the Project are: the reform and improvement of the Borrower's health care
system; improvement of the health status of the population; improvement of the clinical effectiveness of
the service delivery system; improvement of the economic efficiency of the delivery system; and
extension of access of appropriate care, enhancement of the quality of care, and assurance of the long-run
financial viability of the system.
3.2    The Project's objectives are also goals which are fully consistent with those developed under the
first phase of the MANAS Health Care Reform Program. Policies and programs developed to achieve
these goals would be monitored throughout the Project to assure that they also deal with the underlying
social and economic problems that undennine health status, as well as with the public goods aspects of
certain health services where the benefits and/or costs to society far exceed those to the individual (e.g.
immunizations, TB, and STDs), thereby necessitating state financing of goods and services provided in
these policy areas. Effective policy development must also deal with the existing economic constraints,
and base policies on cost-effectiveness criteria. Policies also deal with inefficiencies in the basic system,
whether they relate to ineffective clinical practice or economic inefficiency. Long-run sustainability and
maximum improvements (or reductions in the rate of decline) in health status must be achieved in the
context of the economic realities facing the country as a whole, and the health sector in particular.
3.3    This proposed four and a half year Project will focus on: primary health care, limited facilities
rehabilitation, a medical care provider payment system, and pharmaceutical management. The primary
health care focus of the proposed Project corresponds with these priorities. The interventions envisaged
under the Project are both clinically and cost-effective.  Moreover, focusing on primary care,
rationalizing drug procurement and ensuring the availability of essential drugs, and providing financial
incentives for system restructuring through a medical care provider payment component will result in
cost-effective improvements in the country's health status and improvements in the clinical effectiveness
and economic efficiency of the health system, all within the context of the current economic constraints.
3.4   Increasing the effective utilization of the primary health care system will have the effect of
decreasing demand at the hospital level. In conjunction with this decreased demand, provider paymnent
schemes as proposed in the Project will accelerate the closure of inefficient hospitals. Increasing
utilization of the primary health care system will not occur solely by training providers. A reliable drug
system operating within the Government sphere is imperative if demand for and utilization of primary
health care services is to be sufficient enough to decrease hospital utilization. Another activity encourages
more effective and efficient utilization of the primary health care system through the repair, refurbishment
and minor alteration of select ambulatory facilities.
3.5    The medical care provider payment component will furnish the financial incentives needed to
support the changes in clinical practice and the restructuring of the delivery system inherent in the other
components. It will consist of a new system under which the oblasts, acting as purchasing agents, will
make payments to providers on the basis of pre-agreed performance indicators. During the phase-in
period, payments would be made on a capitation basis. This approach will promote the long-run financial
sustainability of the system and rationalize the current base by promoting primary and outpatient care,
while providing strong incentives to eliminate unnecessary inpatient bed capacity. It reinforces the other
components which also promote and improve the quality of outpatient care through the availability of
essential drugs and redesigned outpatient practice protocols. Physicians will assume responsibility for



20
managing the care of their patients and eliminating unnecessary referrals to other specialists, for
diagnostic tests, and to hospitals, and in tum, will be rewarded with increased remuneration.
3.6    The pharmaceutical management component of the Project will finance the purchase and
distribution of a large quantity of essential drugs, in an effort to improve fundamentally the availability
of needed medicines. The procurement of drugs, however, will be done only in the context of far-
reaching reforms in pharmaceutical management aimed at guaranteeing "sustainability" so that
improvements in the supply of drugs -- or the increment of "health" produced by a given amount of
resources -- will last long after the Project has ended.
B. Project Desciiption
3.7    Through working with officials of the Kyrgyz Government, the MANAS team, and other donors
active in the sector, a four component Project has been developed as follows: (a) primary health care;
(b) facilities rehabilitation; (c) medical care provider payment system; and (d) pharmaceutical
management. This Project is designed to achieve the basic goals of health care reform within the current
economic and institutional constraints of the Republic. It focuses on the major burdens of disease in the
country and is designed to deal with the most egregious inefficiencies in the service delivery system. By
emphasizing cost-effective interventions, modern clinical practices, and major structural reform, the
design recognizes the current economic realities facing the system and provides the basis for long-run
financial sustainability. The Government has provided the Association with a policy statement, acceptable
to the Association, outlining its health sector reform strategy, reaffirming its commitment to health
reforns expressed in its "Master Plan for Future Developments of the Republic's Health Care System,"
einphasizing reform in primary health care, pharmnaceuticals and testing of different modalities of provider
payments as a way of seeking efficient solutions for resource allocation. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained that the Borrower would carry out an action plan agreed with the Association and in a
manner satisfactory to the Association, as part of project execution.
3.8    An important activity of the PHC component in support of all the subcomponents is the
production of mass media materials for the general public, aimed at promoting healthy lifestyles in the
family and the rational utilization of health facilities. Booklets will be widely distributed to the general
public from primary health care facilities. This will complement an extensive countrywide program
supported by the German Development Agency (GTZ) covering, among other things, adult lifestyle
issues. Under the Project local technical assistance will be provided for computer software training and
the production of mass media materials. GTZ will provide technical assistance for the production of
booklets, TV and radio programs for the general public.
Component A: Primary Health Care
(US$4.0 million total project cost)
3.9    The objective of the Primary Health Care component is to reorient health services away from
hospitals and towards upgraded primary health care centers. To facilitate this process, interventions in
this component will provide training to upgrade the skills of primary health care workers, provide them
with basic clinical equipment and ensure them an adequate supply of drugs. The longer term objective
of the Primary Health Care Component is to develop a more cost-effective health care program through
the reduction of hospitals, beds and staff from the merging of specialty hospitals (tuberculosis,
dernatovenereal, children's, matemity, infectious diseases, etc.) into the general hospital system at the
tertiary, secondary and primary health care levels. Before negotiatons the Government has concluded
that no legislation restricts different hospital functions to separate facilities nor forbids the expansion of
the range of health services provided at the primary health care level, including ambulatory treatment of



21
STDs and TB. Not later than December 31, 1996 MOH will establish new case management guidelines
for ambulatory treatment of STDs and TB, as part of project implementation.
3.10   The PHC Component would offer nationwide coverage and would be focused on the primary
health care facilities which provide health care to the country's 43 raions outside of Bishkek, through
three main interventions: (a) redefinition of medical treatment protocols; (b) training of health care
workers in priority health interventions; and (c) development of a mass media campaign for the general
public to support the health reform activities. The MOH would have overall responsibility for the PHC
component in close coordination with oblast and raion health authorities. The Technical Coordination
Committee (TCC), organized to support the technical implementation of the Project, has one full-time
advisor to coordinate all PHC activities (Annex 6 describes implementation arrangements.)
3.11   There are three subcomponents under the PHC component: (a) strengthening women's
reproductive health; (b) acute respiratory infections and control of diarrheal diseases (ARI/CDD); and,
(c) tuberculosis control. These programs cover the major health problems faced by the Kyrgyz Republic,
namely high maternal, perinatal and infant mortality rates, and rising levels of syphilis and tuberculosis.
Reductions in maternal mortality will be achieved by upgrading prenatal, delivery and postnatal care, both
in hospital and at the primary health care level; and to prevent deaths from eclampsia, hemorrhage and
sepsis. Perinatal mortality will be reduced by upgrading the monitoring of pregnancy and labor, and
improving neonatal resuscitation practices to prevent deaths and disability from newborn hypoxia and
brain trauma. The component also focuses on reducing the spread of tuberculosis and syphilis, two of the
most serious infectious diseases, through the introduction of ambulatory care for treating these conditions,
and a public health media campaign on adult health lifestyle issues.
Subcomponent A.1: Strengthening Women's Reproductive Health Management
(US$1.8 million total project cost)
3.12   The specific goals of this subcomponent are: provision of basic maternal care to all pregnant
women, and reduction of the following conditions associated with pregnancy and delivery: maternal
deaths due to obstetric complications such as hemorrhage, eclampsia, sepsis, and obstructed labor;
anemia; neonatal deaths and disabilities associated with birth asphyxia and hypothermia; and, reduction
of anemia and of complications of sexually transmitted diseases among pregnant women, as well as of
ophthalmia neonatorum among infants. Evaluation of improvements in these conditions would be done
using as a basis estinates of present levels of incidence, and comparing these values with the
corresponding statistics of subsequent years (indicators for evaluation are presented in Annex 11). This
subcomponent would complement the work of other donors, including UNICEF, UNFPA, WHO, German
Government (KFW), Central Asian Partners, BASICS, etc.
3.13   The subcomponent will help develop national case management guidelines (or protocols) for the
Mother/Baby package, following WHO and UNICEF guidelines and will increase training capacity within
the country to implement these guidelines. Before negotations, the Government has concluded that no
legislation restricts the adoption of the WHO Guidelines on maternal and child health and STDs, related
to confidential, anonymous care. Not later than December 31, 1996 MOH will establish new national
case management guidelines for women's reproductive health management, as part of project
implementation.
3.14   Main topics for training would include: medical and nursing care of normal and high-risk
pregnancies and deliveries (including use of partograms); medical and nursing care of newborns
(including resuscitation); encouraging breastfeeding during neonatal period; application of WHO's



22
"Mother-Baby" package; principles and practices of family planning; case management of sexually
transmitted diseases; and management and supervision of public health programs.
3.15   A system of countrywide training centers will be established consisting of the Centers of
Continuous Medical Education (CCMEs) in Bishkek and Osh, six Model Hospitals, six Model City
Polyclinics-one in each oblast--and six model FAPs, also one in each oblast. These model training
centers will provide "hands on" clinical training experience in health facilities using the new treatment
protocols. A core of trainers will be trained at the national, oblast and raion levels and printed training
manuals provided to trainees. Study tours will be funded for a limited number of key health personnel.
The training will be institutionalized within several key Government establishments to ensure a regular
update of case management guidelines and a program of continued education for health personnel.
3.16   The training will take place at the 20 facilities listed above, and will be carried out in three
phases: Phase 1. Training of National and Oblast Trainers; Phase 2. Training of Raion Trainers; Phase
3. Training of Primary Health Care Teams. The Project will fund all three phases for the Women's
Reproductive Health Program.
3.17   The  following  personnel would  be  trained  through  the  Project:  80  trainers  (20
obstetricianl/gynecologists, 20 pediatricians, 20 neonatologists, 20 STD specialists) who would in tum
train up to 2,000 health personnel nationwide. Special emphasis would be placed on strengthening the
MOH central, oblast and raion management to ensure adequate oversight and monitoring of the process.
The focus of training would be on the obstetric and pediatric staff working in the country's 43 raion
hospitals; and primary health care staff working in the country's peripheral health units.
3.18   The CCME in Bishkek City, will act as the focus for all training activities in the Project and will
be responsible for training coordination nationwide. In Phase I of the training program, the national and
oblast trainers will receive training at the CCME in Bishkek City. The CCME has a sub-department in
Osh City, which will play a coordinating role for the Southern area of the Kyrgyz Republic. In Phase
2, both CCMEs will be responsible for training raion trainers. The following set of office and training
equipment will be provided by the Project for each of the CCMEs: computer, printer & software (plus
training), heavy duty photocopier, fax machine, videocamera, television, VCR, slide projector, overhead
projector and screen, and 2 flipchart stands.
3.19   Three health facilities in each oblast (a multispeciality Oblast Hospital, a nearby city polyclinic
and a FAP) will be upgraded with equipment and staff training and used as model health facilities for
training purposes. During project implementation and not later than December 31, 1996 MOH would:
(i) combine services in the health facilities selected as Oblast Model Polyclinics where training under the
PHC component will be carried out, so that services for pediatrics, women's health and general adult
health will be offered in one building; and (ii) ensure timely supply and regular replenishment to all
facilities where training is carried out under the PHC component, of project financed equipment, drugs
and other materials, and ensure that part of these drugs are provided free of charge to personnel of FAPs
and ambulatory centers, participating in the training programs as trainees. The practical training will
consist of maternal and perinatal care and sexually transmitted diseases. Hospital-based specialist staff
will receive additional training in neonatal resuscitation and obstetric care. The following sets of training
equipment will be provided to each Oblast Model Hospital: television, VCR, slide projector, overhead
projector and screen, 2 flipchart stands. A limited list of clinical equipment, drugs and supplies will be
provided to the upgraded model hospitals, polyclinics and FAPs. In Phase 3 of the training program,
Primary Health Care Teams from the peripheral FAPs, Rural Physician Ambulatories (SVAs), polyclinics
and rural hospitals will receive training from the raion and oblast trainers.



23
3.20   A set of basic clinical equipment will be provided to 170 ambulatories (SVAs) nationwide. All
43 Central Raion Hospitals nationwide will be equipped with basic instruments to handle complicated
delivery cases.
3.21   The Ministry of Health is responsible for the overall management and monitoring of training
activities. To strengthen MOH management capacity, a computer, printer and software training will be
provided to the Chief Pediatrician and the Chief Obstetrician. Updates on training will be printed out
and circulated to health personnel nationwide by the two CCMEs.
3.22 The subcomponent would require internationally recruited specialists to assist in the design of the
training programs and of the health promotion materials. Specialties required for technical assistance
would include: women and children program management; obstetricians and gynecologists with
experience in having taught the application of the WHO "Mother-Baby" Package Guidelines;
neonatologist/pediatrician; and venereologist with experience in having taught WHO's Guidelines on
sexually transmitted diseases. GTZ is committed to funding technical assistance to support health
promotion activities. Additionally, the Project would finance study tours, consultants on the technical
specialties listed above, to the extent they are not supported through grants by other donors, as well as
the translation or design, and reproduction of manuals and booklets on family health for training workers
and for public information; equipment for training; computers; and clinical equipment for basic mnaternal
and newborn care to 170 ambulatory centers nationwide. The subcomponent also includes financial
support for purchasing essential drugs in two annual tranches for 12 central raion hospitals (two per
oblast) for distribution among surrounding FAPs and ambulatory centers.
3.23   The MOH has been compiling the essential drugs list and would not later than December 31,
1997 establish a data base of National Case Management Guidelines for women's reproductive health,
STDs, ARI/CDD and TB, based on the WHO recommended guidelines, together with treatment protocols
for all those conditions, indicating that drugs used should be from its Essential Drug List only, and
thereafter update and distribute these protocols regularly to health workers nationwide.
Subcomponent A.2 Acute Respiratory Infection and Diarrheal Disease Control
(US$0.6 million total project cost)
3.24   The goals of this subcomponent are to reduce deaths of children from acute respiratory tract
infections and from diarrhea. This will be achieved by retraining hospital-based and outpatient facilities
pediatricians, infectious disease specialists and pediatric nurses, on management of acute ARI and
diarrhea cases among children, using redesigned guidelines based on best current knowledge of
appropriate treatment.
3.25   The subcomponent would help develop national case management guidelines (or protocols) for
ARI/CDD case management, following WHO and UNICEF Guidelines and will increase training capacity
within the country to implement these guidelines. Before negotiatons the Government has concluded that
no legislation restricts the adoption of the WHO guidelines on ARI/CDD. Not later than December 31,
1996 MOH will establish national case management guidelines for ARI/CDD case management, as part
of project implementation.
3.26 This subcomponent would complement the UNICEF ARI/CDD program and would finance
essential drugs for ARI/CDD to 12 central raion hospitals (two per oblast) to be distributed to
surrounding FAPs and ambulatory centers. UNICEF would provide essential drugs for the peripheral
health units. It would train staff in all those facilities and would provide them with a small pack of
essential drugs and supplies for ARI/CDD, which would subsequently be regularly resupplied from the



24
central raion hospital. Training would involve "hands-on" practical instruction with patients. Public
education would be done through lectures and booklets.
3.27   The system of countrywide training centers and courses, described in paragraph 3.15 above,
would also train health personnel in the treatment of ARI/CDD. Under the Project, training under the
ARI/CDD subcomponent will be carried out in two phases: Phase 1. Training of National and Oblast
Trainers; and Phase 2. Training of Raion trainers. Phase 3. training of primary health care teams would
be funded by UNICEF. The practical training would consist of case management of acute respiratory
disease and diarrheal disease.
Subcomponent A.3:  Tuberculosis Control  (US$1.5 million total project cost)
3.28   The TB subcomponent of the Primary Health Care component would revitalize the National
Tuberculosis Program by providing support to the existing three levels: central (at the National
Tuberculosis and Research Institute), oblast, and raion structures and to the primary health care services
countrywide for program implementation. Adoption of legislation in the form of a Government decree
and a law of Parliament, respectively, revitalizing the National Tuberculosis Program and the framework
for tuberculosis control prepared by the National Tuberculosis Research Institute was a conditon of
negotiatons which the Government has met. As part of project implementation, new treatment protocols
for TB will be adopted by December 31, 1996. It will result in more effective case detection and
treatment, use of current TB treatment protocols, and emphasize ambulatory as opposed to inpatient
treatment of tuberculosis. In short, it would improve both the clinical effectiveness and economic
efficiency of the delivery system, and improve the health status of the population through early detection
and effective treatment of tuberculosis.
3.29   The TB subcomponent consists of the following elements:
(a)    expansion, improvement and free provision of tuberculosis diagnostic services in at least
one facility per raion or city and upgrading of the quality of bacteriological services at
raion and city level;
(b)    provision of free short course chemotherapy and other treatment services to all TB cases,
with directly observed therapy during, at least, the initial phase of chemotherapy, and
achievement of high cure rates among all sputum smear positive pulmonary cases;
(c)   improvement of case management by adoption of WHO recommended national case
management guidelines, recording and reporting system, and adequate supervision;
(d)    introduction of a system of financial incentives for case detection and cure of tuberculosis
patients through the Government's own resources including a payment to any health
worker who originally refers TB suspects for examination if the diagnosis of TB is
confirmed by sputum smear microscopy, and fees for managing the course of treatment
if cure is documented (adoption of legislation introducing this system was a condition of
negotiations and has been fulfilled); and,
(e)    provision of approximately US$600,000 of TB pharmaceuticals and US$180,000 for the
transportation and distribution costs of the pharmaceuticals.
