3.30   Training and technical assistance will be crucial for staff at all levels to successfully adopt modern
supervised, short course chemotherapy and the new policy. In late 1995 two pilot projects in selected



25
raions began irnplementation to evaluate the operational aspects of the new program. These raions will
form the national training site for future nationwide project implementation. Basic training equipment
will be provided for the raions selected for the pilot projects and, later, for the whole country. The
training centers described in paragraph 3.15 above will be used for the training in the Tuberculosis
Control Program. The cost of conducting the training programs (central and oblast managers - one
course every other year; oblast and raion/city staff - seven courses, one per oblast every other year; for
PHC workers - two brief courses per raion every year; laboratory personnel at central and oblast level -
one course every other year; peripheral laboratory personnel - one course in each oblast every year), and
of the materials used will be covered by the Project. The raion coordinator should maintain a close
relationship with primary health care workers and ensure continuous education on tuberculosis control.
External consultants to assist and help in the national training courses will be necessary.
3.31   Two pilot projects have been established to evaluate the feasibility of providing ambulatory
treatment through the primary health care system, as compared to drug administration through
hospitalization of all patients at the initial phase of treatment. The results of the pilot projects, as well as
the progress of implementation of the new primary health care system, will guide the final choice of
diagnostic and treatment policies for the nationwide project.
3.32   Since hard-to-justify numbers of facilities and beds dedicated to tuberculosis control are available
in the Kyrgyz Republic, the Project will, by its accompanying changes, rationalize the number of beds
and reduce the number of facilities. As case detection and treatment will become the responsibility of
primary health care workers, the MOH should consider making current tuberculosis specialists from
tuberculosis in-patient facilities available, on a part-time basis, to primary health care facilities.
Component B: Facilities Rehabilitation
(US$2.3 million total project cost)
3.33   The facilities rehabilitation component would improve the capacity of the Ministry of Health in
managing the maintenance of buildings and equipment, and help prepare the MOH to implement
necessary rationalization programs of the health infrastructure to make it more responsive to the planned
shift from a predominantly curative system, to a major emphasis on preventive programs and ambulatory
care. This component would facilitate the transition and the implementation of adjustments in the number
of hospital beds, remodeling of facilities, and introduction of energy saving measures at health
institutions. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that during the execution of the Project the
Borrower will not undertake any new construction of health facilities without prior consultation with the
Association.
3.34   The Project will provide US$300,000 for improving conditions of 30 ambulatory centers i.e.
repairing roofs and floors, replacing ironmongery, refurbishing water and sanitary installations and up-
grading electrical supply and installations; and US$1,200,000 for converting 30 rural hospitals into
ambulatory polyclinics with holding beds (carrying out the same repairs as above, and converting existing
wards into laboratories, consultation rooms, etc.). In addition, US$300,000 for equipment and
US$150,000 for supplies will be provided to ensure efficient performance of the renovated/converted
facilities.
3.35   The Project will also provide US$50,000 (training) and US$7,200 (salary for full-time TCC
member) to support the activities of the Buildings and Equipment Committee. This Committee, which
will meet once or twice a month, as necessary, reports to the Minister of Health and represents clinical,
nursing, technical, staffing administration and financing interests in the Ministry. The Committee's tasks
include representing the MOH in making decisions regarding building and equipment activities; approving



26
the technical content of the proposals; and deciding on priorities. The Commnittee will also be responsible
for obtaining and circulating information within the MOH on civil works projects; coordinating
commissioning and staffing; delegating tasks within the Ministry and establishing and liaising with user-
groups at the different facilities being up-graded. In order to assist the Buildings and Equipmnent
Committee in the above tasks, and further in revising existing norms and standards for health facilities
and in the implementation of the civil works included in this component, US$60,000 is provided under
the Project to reimburse the fees of local architectural and engineering consultants.
3.36   The construction activities of the facilities component will take place initially in the Chui Oblast
region so as to support closely the provider payment component efforts to rationalize health services and
facilities before making major investments. The methodologies developed and solutions rendered in Chui
Oblast will then enable the MOH to apply them nationwide. As a condition of negotiations the MOH
has prepared a list for review and comments to the Association of 30 ambulatory centers and 30 rural
hospitals in Chui Oblast and Bishkek City to be repaired and converted as part of this Project. As part
of project implementation, establishment by MOH of a stakeholder group consisting of health authorities
and local government officials in each medical facility to be renovated under the Project will take place
before its renovation in undertaken.
3.37   The rehabilitation "packages" to be developed will be low cost (US$10,000-40,000 for buildings,
US$1,000-4,000 for equipment) and may be replicated with national resources in the rest of the country.
Component C: Medical Care Provider Payment
(US$4.4 million total project cost)
3.38   The health care system in the Kyrgyz Republic is dominated by the hospital sector, which
consumes more than 75 percent of resources. One reason for this inefficient allocation of resources is
the financial incentives for facilities contained in the current line item budgeting system. The budgeting
system is based on input measures such as number of beds and staff, and rewards increases in capacity
rather than the efficient provision of high quality health services.
3.39   Under the Project the methods to be used by the Ministry of Health through the oblasts to pay
hospitals, polyclinics, and physicians would be changed to foster efficiency in the health care delivery
system and promote the use of primary care. Payment for care would no longer be made on the basis
of production input measures but rather on the basis of performance as measured by coverage of the
services provided (capitation). The new provider payment methods would strengthen the connection
between the quantity and quality of services rendered, and the financial reimbursement received for those
services. Incentives would encourage treatment of patients on an outpatient as opposed to inpatient basis,
reduce length-of-stay for those patients who do require hospitalization, and reduce unnecessary referrals
to specialists and for diagnostic tests.
3.40   Provider payment reforms would change the financing and the organization of care in two
important ways. Payment for care would be separated from delivery of care. The payer would focus
on paying for the best care at the lowest cost -- purchasing care rather than manage and maintain
facilities. The provider would be made responsible for managing the services and would have more
flexibility to focus on delivering the most optimal mix of services with the best outcome. Secondly,
providers would be "at risk" in that they would now be responsible for any deficits between payments
received and costs incurred. Providers would be accountable for more efficient and higher quality of
care.



27
3.41   The Project would extend to two other jurisdictions the provider payment aspect of a USAID-
funded pilot program, which is now in the early stages of implementation in Issyk-kul Oblast. The
Technical Coordinating Committee (TCC) would monitor the progress of Issyk-kul and the extended pilot,
and keep Government officials and providers informed about the design, implementation and results of
the pilot.
3.42   Site Selection: The geographic areas selected as most favorable for extending the demonstration
are Bishkek City and Chui Oblast. These sites are preferable for several reasons: (i) relatively dense,
urban population; (ii) most affluent geo-political units (in terms of per capita income and tax revenues
paid); (iii) relative sophistication of medical institutions and trained personnel; (iv) relative excess of
medical providers, especially physicians per capita in Bishkek; (v) contiguous geographically, effectively
sharing market areas for medical services; (vi) proximity to health sector leadership for observation and
evaluation; and, (vii) proximity to technical coordinating staff for implementation purposes under the
Credit. Selected data comparing features of these two geographic units and Issyk-kul to national averages
are provided in Annex 4, Table 2.
3.43   Pilot Design. Implementation of this component requires structural change in the health services
delivery system as well as development of new provider payment systems and the corresponding financial
infrastructure to support them. Four major progranmmatic elements or modules of the medical care
provider payments component are described below:
3.44   (i) The first element is comprehensive restructuring of the primary care sector through the
establishment of new primary care group practices--Family Group Practices. In order to reimburse
providers a capitated rate for the provision of all primary health services to an individual, an entity
capable of providing the entire range of primary health services must exist and consist of a therapist
(internist), pediatrician, and obstetrician/gynecologist. Currently there are very few general practitioners.
3.45   The primary care groups would be the focal point of the new provider payment methods: they
would enroll people and receive a capitated rate for providing a specified set of health services. The
financial incentive for the groups is to provide services rather than referring patients to other parts of the
health delivery system. Different models of primary care should be encouraged, and over time clinical
retraining and new medical institute curriculum will lead to the development of general and family
practitioners. The primary care groups should be independent, form voluntarily, and be organized as a
business. They can initiate the development of non-profit entities within the health sector and also
become private practitioners.
3.46   Approximately 300 primary care group practices would be formed to serve the population of
Bishkek City and Chui Oblast, estimated at 1.6 million people. Each group would be capable of enrolling
and serving approximately 5,300 people. About 150 groups would come on line in the first year of the
Project, 100 in the second year and 50 in the third year. A significant portion of the provider payment
component budget will be directed at developing the capacity of these entities to respond to the financial
incentives of the new payment methods. The Project will fund minor renovations of new practice
locations, allowing them to establish a separate identity and be dispersed throughout the community;
equipment to enable the groups to treat patients rather than refer them; clinical training to increase clinical
capability and begin development of a cadre of general practitioners; and, establishment of financial
systems, including development of practice managers providing business skills for the groups.
3.47   (ii) The second element is free choice of primary care provider by the population and enrollment
of the entire population into primary care groups. Participation by the population in decisions about their
health care gives providers an incentive to provide high quality services. If patients are not satisfied, they



28
can change providers. Information would be furnished to the population to help them make informed
choices about their medical provider. The Project would fund a public awareness and marketing
campaign, an enrollment process, and the distribution of comparative information so the population may
judge provider performance.
3.48   (iii) The third element is new incentive-based provider payment systems for primary care services,
outpatient specialty and ancillary services, and hospital services. These payment systems replace line-
item budgets for facilities with payment for health services at the patient level. Although the experience
of the Issyk-kul pilot will determine the exact form of these systems, the main concepts are a capitated
rate and a case-based hospital payment. A capitated rate is the payment of a set fee to a provider for the
provision of certain health services to an individual. A case-base hospital payment system reimburses
hospitals a prospectively set fee depending on the type and severity of the treated case.
3.49   Currently, national, oblast, municipal, and raion governments finance and control their own
facilities using a budgetary system which allocates funds through predetermined functional categories such
as salaries and medical supplies. The health reforms envision the population choosing providers and
providers receiving payment for specific services rendered to patients. The focus of payment for health
services changes from the facility level to the patient level. A fiscal intermediary is needed to pool the
funds from the national, oblast, municipal, and raion sources and reimburse providers for health services
they provide to the population. Funds would no longer be delineated by government level and separately
allocated to facilities; services would be purchased by the fiscal intermediary from one pool of funds.
3.50   The fiscal intermediary requires institutional capacity to pool the funds and manage the new
provider payment systems. The development and installation of new computer systems is especially
important. Provider payment on a treated case basis increases the base unit for reimbursement many
times. For example, in Issyk-kul the base unit for hospital payment will increase from budgets for about
50 facilities to payment for about 50,000 treated hospital cases. This very large increase in the number
of transactions requires the development of automated systems to manage the payment process.
3.51   Computer systems would handle enrollment of the population, construction of rates for the
payment systems, recording of clinical information from facility bills, payment of providers for services,
operation of a quality assurance system, and analysis of health statistics. An accounting system would
record all financial transactions from defined source payment documents, interact with the banking system
and provide financial reports for the fiscal intermediary. Current accounting systems provide a good
starting point; however, they could benefit from the introduction of accrual accounting and more
sophisticated financial reporting to present available information in a more useful form. Internal auditing
and control procedures are necessary. Relationships between the fiscal intermediary and the treasury and
banking system need to be clearly defined. The transition to a stable market economy may require the
banking system to gradually shift its focus toward handling financial transactions efficiently, away from
serving as a control mechanism.
3.52   The Issyk-kul pilot is developing and implementing these new systems. This allows for efficient
refinement and extension of the products under the proposed project's provider payment component. The
Project will fund adaptation of the new provider payment systems to Bishkek and Chui Oblast,
procurement of computer hardware, refinement and installation of computer and accounting systems for
the fiscal intermediary to track data of enrolled patients, and training of local experts and facility
managers to implement the new provider payment systems.
3.53   (iv) The fourth element is development of health sector infrastructure to support the
implementation of new provider payment systens.  This infrastructure consists of three areas: (a)



29
integrated management information systems (MIS), such as cost accounting and clinical information
systems, to track data on the country's health care delivery, resource use and utilization patterns and
provide managers tools to improve their decision-making ability; (b) increased management autonomy,
flexibility, and accountability for health care providers; and, (c) quality assurance systems to monitor care
and prevent "gaming" or abuse under different financial incentives of new provider payment systems.
3.54   As with the Project's provider payment systems, these infrastructure products are being developed
in Issyk-kul. The provider payment component will fund adaptation of the systems, procurement of
computer hardware and software to establish the capacity necessary at the facility level, and training of
local experts and facility managers.
3.55   Finance and Training Plan: The medical care provider payment component requires US$4.4
million in IDA and counterpart funds to implement over a four and a half year period. The Government
of the Kyrgyz Republic would be required to provide contributions in terms of staff time and expertise.
A limited number of leaders would be offered the opportunity for out-of-country study tours and training.
These trips would be planned in the first year of the Project to support the initial development and
implementation of the provider payment systems, and the third year of the Project to support the
continued refinement of the provider payment systems following initial implementation.
3.56   Approximately 2,000 health care personnel would receive training under the Project's medical
care provider payment component. Most of this training would occur in years I and 2 of the Project.
The training would be preparatory to the implementation of new provider payment systems, which would
begin in year 2 and be phased-in over years 2-4. Retraining of physicians would occur over the first
three years to coincide with the establishment of primary care groups. Internists, pediatricians, and
obstetricians/gynecologists would receive both classroom and on-site training to improve their capability
to manage care on an outpatient basis and begin the conversion to general and family practitioners. This
process would be enhanced by changes in the curriculum for medical students. Training for specialists
would be offered as they begin to redefine their role in the new health care delivery system.
3.57   A clinical training center would be established and provided with equipment and materials at the
Republican Center of Continuous Medical Education to provide clinical training to physicians. The
Health Finance and Management Department of the CCME would also be established as a training center
and provided equipment and materials to hold training for the provider payments component. In addition
to more efficient provision of training, improving the education capacity will build the finance and
management skills needed within the health sector to sustain the reforms.
3.58   Implementation: The institution of new provider payment methods requires substantial technical
expertise in hospital payment, physician payment, clinical practice, cost accounting, clinical information
systems, management, finance and internal auditing, computer systems, accreditation, and quality
improvement. Although most of the products would have been developed in the Issyk-kul pilot, they will
need to be adjusted to fit Bishkek and Chui Oblast because the population composition is different.
3.59   The implementation strategy of this component is to use foreign experts selectively to produce
defined products. Each of the four program elements would have a foreign expert or module leader.
Module leaders would have three goals in their work: (i) refine the Issyk-kul products to fit Bishkek and
Chui; (ii) provide initial training to a broad audience on the systems/products through workshops, and
intensively train a local expert in the specialty area; and, (iii) work with the local expert to implement
this aspect of the Project.



30
3.60   The local expert for each programmatic area would be responsible for implementation of the
product and continued training of health personnel. Qualified computer specialists would support them.
They would both work for the fiscal intermediary or one of three associations formed to facilitate the
implementation of the provider payment reforms. The three associations are a Primary Care Physicians
Association, a Hospital Association, and an Accreditation Commission.  Establishing these three
organizations should contribute to the sustainability of the reforms. The Accreditation Commission needs
to be independent, to determine objectively whether a facility meets the minimum standards required to
operate and receive payment for services. In the medium to long-term, the Primary Care Physicians
Association and Hospital Association will serve as representative bodies in a more decentralized, market-
oriented health sector. While the local experts serving as their employees are initially supported by the
Project, they are developing marketable skills which will be in demand in the future.
3.61   The central Ministry of Health would have overall responsibility for the component with
implementation managed by the TCC. The TCC would have one full-time advisor to coordinate all
provider payment activities. Close coordination is required with several other critical organizational
components -- the central Ministry of Finance and the oblast (city in the case of Bishkek) level
administration, Department of Health, and Department of Finance. To facilitate collaboration between
the central, oblast and city level governments, Ministry/Department of Health, and Ministry/Department
of Finance, a task force would be formed with representatives of the various stakeholders. The World
Bank, TCC, and task force would continue its close collaboration with other relevant donors -- notably
USAID, ODA and GTZ.
3.62   For full implementation of the pilot to occur, the Government must ensure that all legal and
regulatory obstacles are removed. Before negotiations the Government has concluded that current
legislation does not restrict implementation of new provider payment systems in the extended pilot.
However, an order specifically allowing such payment systems is being prepared and will be adopted by
December 31, 1996 as part of project implementation. The order will address at least the following three
points:
(a)    Savings from rationalization of facilities or implementation of new provider payment
methods will be retained and reinvested in the medical sector;
(b)    Establishment of a mechanism to enforce the pooling of funds at the national, oblast,
municipal and raion level; and
(c)    Authority to establish new payment methods including at least the following:
(i)    The line-item budget system will not be used to reimburse providers.
(ii)    Determination and resolution of conflicts with the Treasury System created by
new provider payment methods.
(iii)   Capability to institute performance-based pay.
(iv)   Facility management autonomy including hiring and firing.
3.63   A smooth transition of the provider payment reforms from the Issyk-kul pilot to Bishkek City and
Chui Oblast requires continuous coordination with the Issyk-kul pilot over the next year. The TCC and
task force should meet regularly with the Issyk-kul pilot team, begin work on designing the program and
adapting the Issyk-kul products, communicate with and educate officials about the design, implementation
and results of the Issyk-kul pilot, and monitor the progress of the pilot program. The three parties should
continue to work to facilitate the rapid implementation of the extended pilot by identifying,
communicating and helping to resolve any issues which arise.



31
3.64   During negotiations, assurances related to the implementation of this component, were obtained
that the Government would evaluate the Issyk-kul provider payments pilot program, not later than
December 31, 1996 to explore the feasibility of introducing a provider payment mechanism for health
care workers, and present the results of the evaluation to the Association for its review and comments
and would subsequently apply the recommendations to the implementation of the medical care provider
payments component of the Project. As part of project implementation, the Borrower would take the
following steps:
(a)    not later than December 31, 1996, issue guidelines, acceptable to the Association, to
health authorities at all levels on leasing of publicly owned health premises to family
group practices.
(b)    MOH would, not later than December 31, 1996, carry out a study of the levels of patient
referrals from primary care physicians to specialists and for diagnostic tests in Bishkek
City and Chui Oblast; not later than June 30, 1997, MOH would submit this study to the
Association for review and comments, and subsequently MOH would implement the
agreed recommendations.
(c)    not later than December 31, 1997, (i) establish and thereafter maintain a Health Fund into
which national, oblast, municipal, and raion level health and finance authorities would
contribute and out of which medical providers will be paid, together with guidelines on
the manner and level of contribution by these health authorities and the characteristics and
costs of services to be purchased; (ii) design and implement a national health accounts
system to be able to track the allocation of resources for health and the flow of funds in
the health sector; and (iii) pass legislation allowing establishment of primary health care
provider groups as non-profit entities and opening of bank accounts by such groups.
Component D: Pharmaceutical Management
(US$8.3 million total project cost)
3.65   The pharmaceutical management component is aimed at improving the availability of essential
drugs both (a) directly, by financing the procurement and distribution of a large quantity of essential
drugs, and (b) indirectly, by supporting the implementation of far-reaching reforms designed to strengthen
the Government's drug regulatory capabilities and to increase the discipline and cost-effectiveness of
public spending. Financial support for the procurement and distribution of essential drugs is contingent
on the Government's continued determination to implement these reforms.
3.66   The pharmaceutical management component will support the following activities:
(a)    renovation of the premises of the national drug regulatory organization and acquisition
of office equipment and furniture, laboratory testing equipment and furniture, reagents
and chemical glassware, scientific literature, and other goods needed in order for the
organization to function effectively;
(b)   technical assistance and training for the staff of the drug regulatory organization, as well
as broad dissemination to other health care officials of information on drug regulatory
affairs;



32
(c)    development, production, printing, and widespread distribution of the Kyrgyz Republic's
Essential Drugs List, as well as training health care officials and managers concerning
the goals and rationale of an Essential Drugs Program;
(d)    development of new standard medical treatment guidelines, based on and consistent with
the Essential Drugs List; and,
(e)    procurement and distribution of a significant quantity of essential drugs.
National Drug Regulatory Organization
3.67   The national drug regulatory authority will be responsible for: (a) the registration of all drugs and
vaccines manufactured and/or marketed in the Kyrgyz Republic, including pre-registration review of all
technical information on safety and effectiveness, the organization of clinical trials if necessary, and post-
registration monitoring of drug utilization and possible adverse drug reactions; and, (b) quality testing
and quality assurance of all drugs and vaccines manufactures, imported, marketed, and/or otherwise
distributed in the Kyrgyz Republic.
3.68   A national drug regulatory organization was established in 1993 by decree of the Prime Minister.
Located within the MOH, the organization is known officially as the Administration for Standardization
and Oversight of the Quality of Medicines and Medical Technology. The authority and responsibilities
of this organization are reaffirmed in a proposed "Law on Medicines" that has been drafted and its
adoption by the Borrower is a condition of effectiveness. The Administration for Standardization
includes, among its subordinate departments, a national drug testing laboratory which is responsible for
(a) providing technical support of the Administration's registration decisions, and (b) ascertaining the
quality of drugs manufactures, imported, and/or otherwise distributed in the Kyrgyz Republic.
3.69   The Drug Regulatory Organization and its subordinate testing laboratory, which are co-located,
have rented 340 meters of space at the Institute of Cardiology and are negotiating with the Institute to
rent an additional 160 meters of adjoining space. With a total of approximately 500 square meters of
space, the organization will have sufficient room to perform its functions. Renovation of the premises
has begun, and a small amount of testing equipment has been procured; it will be installed when
additional renovation is completed and the necessary laboratory furniture has been acquired. Current staff
of the organization numbers 15 of a planned 35. The Project will provide extensive support to enable
the organization and laboratory to become fully operational as quickly as possible. The Project will
provide US$97,000 for renovation of the premises and US$357,000 for the purchase of: office furniture
and equipment; laboratory testing equipment and furniture; reagents and chemical glassware; scientific
literature, computer software, and databases; and other necessary supplies.
3.70   The Project will finance a substantial amount of training for the staff of the organization and
laboratory, including activities designed to foster close professional relations between officials of the
organization and counterparts in drug regulatory agencies of other countries. The Project will finance
in-country training in the use of computers and databases, the use of new laboratory equipment, and cost-
effectiveness analysis for approximately 75 people during the first two years of the Project. The Project
also will finance approximately 50 person-weeks of training and study tours abroad, spread over four
years. In order to facilitate widespread dissemination of this information within the Kyrgyz Republic and
to support a limited amount of training for persons outside the organization and laboratory, e.g. local
health care officials, the Project will also finance an annual two-day national conference on drug
regulatory affairs, including participation of foreign experts.



33
3.71   The Drug Regulatory Organization presently operates as a self-financing body, relying on income
from registration and testing fees. As a result, drug regulatory officials may have a direct economic
interest in their regulatory decisions, and this may increase the risk that ineffective, or even unsafe, drugs
will be made available to patients. In order to avoid potential conflicts of interest, the compensation of
senior drug officials and other staff with a direct voice in regulatory decisions should be financed directly
from the state budget. Before negotiations the Government did adopt revised procedures for financing
the Administration for Standardization, providing that the salaries of senior officials and regulatory
decision-makers are financed by the Government and not from fees.
3.72   The TCC  Component Coordinator for pharmaceutical management will supervise the
implementation of the various activities. In cooperation with the Chief of the Drug Regulatory
Organization, other MOH officials, and the Project Coordination Unit, the TCC component coordinator
will prepare terms-of-reference for all procurement contracts, including adequately specified lists of
equipment, furniture, supplies, and other goods to be purchased with project funds. The TCC component
coordinator will prepare, in concert with the Chief of the Drug Regulatory Organization, an annual
training plan, including the dates, location, purpose, and names of participants for all in-country training
courses and foreign study tours; the TCC component coordinator will exercise personal responsibility for
assuring that all technical and logistical requirements of the training are met. The TCC component
coordinator will maintain continual communications with other donors, in particular WHO, in order to
minimize duplication and to maximize coordination in all drug regulatory technical assistance and training
activities. Finally, the Coordinator will, each year, convene and chair a temporary organizing committee
to prepare for and to implement the annual national conference on drug regulatory affairs.
Essential Drugs Program
3.73   An Essential Drugs List (EDL), numbering 300 different products, has been adopted as a first
step toward the creation of an Essential Drugs Program (EDP). Part 1 of this list consists of the 195
"most widely distributed basic medicines" that are expected to be consistently available at, or by
prescription from, all primary and secondary health care facilities. Part 2 consists of 105 "basic
medicines used in specialized institutions."  The EDL has been endorsed by both WHO and the
Association. Before negotiations, the Government did adopt and publish the EDL, agreed upon by the
Association and WHO.
3.74   The EDL was compiled by a large ad hoc committee in the MOH and is the product of a lengthy
dialogue between the MOH and experts from the WHO and the Association. The process for establishing
and maintaining the EDL must now be institutionalized. The latest draft of the "Law on Medicines"
delegates to the MOH formal authority to compile the EDL. Final passage of this law, with this authority
included, and the adoption of formal rules and procedures for compiling and maintaining the EDL,
including provisions for its periodic review and, if necessary, revision in the future in a form acceptable
to the Association, is a condition of effectiveness.
3.75   In order to become an effective means of influence over the procurement and utilization of drugs,
the EDL must be made widely available, and the rationale for an EDL must be understood clearly and
supported by physicians, pharmacists, other health care professionals, and health care financing decision-
makers. The Project will support development and publication of a brochure, containing the EDL and
a lengthy commentary explaining how the EDL was compiled and its purpose. The Project will finance
the printing and distribution of several thousand copies of the brochure, which will be made available to
all pharmacists and health facilities prior to the start of the sizable procurement of essential drugs under
the Project. The Project will help to finance a workshop in each oblast prior to effectiveness, in order
to introduce the EDL to local health leaders and to build professional support for an Essential Drugs



34
Program. In the second and fourth years of the Project, revised editions of the EDL will be published
and distributed.
3.76   Equally necessary for effective implementation of the EDL is the development and dissemination
of new, standard medical treatment guidelines that are compatible and consistent with the limited range
of drugs included on the List. The MOH already has begun to draft new treatment guidelines, and the
Project will support a study tour prior to effectiveness, designed to further this effort.
3.77   Maximum benefit of the EDL will be achieved when the List becomes a principal mechanism for
regulating public spending on drugs. However, a requirement that public spending for drugs be limited
to the EDL is, for the time being, impracticable. Two years will be needed in order to train health care
professionals and to win their support and cooperation, but also in order to gain experience with the List
and to revise the List in light of this experience, as will inevitably be required. From the start of the
third year of the Project, public spending on drugs will be limited to the EDL: from that date, state-
owned health facilities will be permitted to buy, and specially "entitled" ambulatory patients will be
permitted to receive free of charge or at subsidized prices, only drugs included on its EDL. As part of
project implementation, the Government would from January 31, 1998 onwards allocate budget resources
for procurement and reimbursement of pharmaceuticals solely to pharmaceuticals listed on its EDL and
registered in the country. At the same time, no restrictions will be placed on the privately-financed
purchase, distribution, and sale of medicines which are not included on the EDL.
3.78   The establishment and operations of the Kyrgyz Republic's Essential Drugs Program will be
undertaken and supervised by a standing commission or committee appointed by the Minister of Health,
consistent with authority provided by the proposed "Law on Medicines" and as provided by the rules to
be adopted on compiling and maintaining its EDL. As part of project implementation, the Government
would establish this standing committee by December 31, 1996. The TCC Component Coordinator for
pharmaceutical management will be a member of this body and will coordinate the various project
activities intended to support implementation of the EDP. In particular, the TCC component coordinator
will: (a) convene and chair a small, informal working group to oversee production and distribution of the
EDL; (b) maintain close communications with WHO, UNICEF, and other donors in order to ensure that
foreign assistance in the development of new standard treatment guidelines is used with minimum
duplication and maximum effectiveness; and (c) convene and chair an informal working group to design
and implement a monitoring program which will include regular surveys of pharmacies and physicians
in order to measure the availability and utilization of essential drugs.



35
Procurement of Essential Drugs
3.79   In order (a) to improve significantly the supply of the most important and commonly used
essential drugs, and thereby to facilitate introduction of the Essential Drugs Program, and (b) to
strengthen financially key elements of the Kyrgyz Republic's drug distribution system now undergoing
privatization, funds amounting to US$6.0 million (without contingencies) over the life of the project
would finance the purchase and distribution of drugs included on the EDL. All drugs purchased with
project funds will be selected from Part 1 of the EDL and procured by the Project Coordinating Unit
(PCU). In principle, registration of drugs must be completed or in progress prior to award of contracts,
although the Borrower and the Association may agree on certain exceptions to this requirement. They
must be labeled in Russian; and all bulk packages and individual vials and containers must be identified
as containing an "Essential Drug of the Kyrgyz Republic." In order to give potential vendors sufficient
time to register their drugs, a list of drugs to be procured was published in the March 31, 1996
Development Business; final procurement will be limited to drugs included on the initial list. A similar
procedure will be observed in the case of subsequent tranches, although the minimum period between
publication of the initial procurement list and bidding will be four months.
3.80   In order to minimize supply shocks, funds will be disbursed in four annual tranches of
approximately US$2.0 million in each of the first two years and approximately US$1.0 million each in
Project Years Three and Four. Initially, onward distribution of the drugs procured with these funds will
be organized as follows. Procurements will be divided into six lots, according to a formula approved
by the Association prior to effectiveness; winning vendors will deliver shipments, in accordance with this
formula, to Naryn Oblast Pharmacia, Issyk-kul Oblast Pharmacia, Osh Oblast Pharmacia, Talas Oblast
Pharmacia, Dzhalal-Abad Oblast Pharmacia, and Kyrgyz Pharmacia (for onward distribution in Chui
Oblast and Bishkek city)--hereafter, these recipients are called the "Distributors"; bidders' prices will
include the cost of transportation and delivery to these destinations. Drugs will be sold to the Distributors
on credit, based on an arrangement to be described in distribution agreements. Preparation of bidding
documents for the first annual tranche of drug procurement, and distribution agreements for these drugs
in six oblasts, in a form acceptable to the Association is a condition of effectiveness. The Distributors
will be permitted to sell drugs procured with project funds at prices no higher than the nominal cost of
procurement and delivery plus a five percent markup (equal approximately to the cost of handling and
distribution). The requirement that the drugs be sold at controlled prices will limit the risk of diversion,
while maximizing availability. All prospective buyers, regardless of their form of organization or
ownership, will be guaranteed equal opportunities to purchase the drugs. Revenues received by the
Distributors, generated by the sale of drugs procured with project funds, will be accounted separately and
used (a) to cancel the distributors' substantial debt and (b) to rebuild the distributors' working capital.
The Government will present to the Association as part of the bidding documents to be submitted prior
to effectiveness, the contract for drug distribution, and a list with the percentages of revenue allocated
to these purposes, along with remaining revenue allocated to tax payments. In turn, the hospitals,
pharmacies, and distributors that buy Project-financed drugs from the oblast Pharmacias and Kyrgyz
Pharmacia will be permitted to sell the drugs to patients at unrestricted prices, except in the case of
legally exempted patients, and the revenues resulting from such sales will be taxed according to existing
legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic. As part of project implementation, drug procurement and distribution
will be reviewed by the Government by June 30, 1997 and thereafter at the end of each procurement
cycle so as to assure that privately owned drug wholesalers and retailers are not disadvantaged, compared
to remaining state-owned companies.
3.81   In addition to the US$6.0 million to be used to finance the procurement and distribution of drugs
from Part 1 of the Essential Drugs Lists, US$2.0 million will be made available to purchase necessary
drugs in support of the activities in the Primary Health Care component of the Project. In order to ensure



36
maximum efficiency and cost effectiveness, these PHC drugs will be procured by the PCU at the samne
time as, and in accordance with the same procedures used for, the procurement of essential drugs.
However, the PHC drugs will be onward distributed as specified previously in the description of the PHC
component of the Project.
National Drug Policy
3.82   The Kyrgyz Republic has not yet published a document, or set of documents, sufficiently broad
and encompassing as to constitute a national drug policy. Nevertheless, considerable progress has been
made in developing the individual components required of such a policy, notably the Law on Medicines.
A national Drug Regulatory Authority and associated testing laboratory have been established and are
being put into operation. Initial steps to establish an Essential Drugs Program have been taken. The
former vertically-integrated and highly centralized state monopoly in charge of drug procurement and
distribution is being thoroughly restructured and privatized. In addition, in collaboration with the
MANAS project, the MOH has drafted a lengthy, comprehensive "Master Plan" for future development
of the Republic's health care system, which includes a detailed discussion of national policy and
development priorities in the pharmaceutical sector. Taken together, these individual activities constitute
the core elements of a comprehensive national drug policy which, if articulated publicly, would raise the
effectiveness of the pharmaceutical management component of the Project and contribute positively to the
overall development of the Republic's pharmaceutical sector. The Government's policy statement as
described in para. 1.50 reaffirms its commitment.



37

A. Project Costs

expenditure are contained in Annex 12.

Basis of Cost Estimates
4.2    Project costs have been estimated in US dollars because of the difficulty in forecasting inflation
rates for local currency. Civil works cost have been derived from a detailed study carried out in August
1995 during preparation of the Project. Costs for Goods consisting of pharmaceuticals, medical
equipment and related supplies, other miscellaneous office equipment consisting of computer and related
equipment and software are based on recent contracts awarded under an on-going IDA-financed project
(Social Safety Net Project-Credit No. 2643-KG). Unit costs for medical equipment and related supplies
are based on UNICEF price lists and quotations from suppliers in Bank-financed projects in other FSU
countries. Cost for consulting services have been derived on the basis of recent cost estimates for
comparable services in other IDA-financed projects in the Kyrgyz Republic including training (both local
and overseas). The costs of consulting services would include, fees, recruitment/relocation costs,
housing, subsistence, and overheads.



38
Foreign Exchange Component
4.3    The foreign exchange component has been estimated as follows: (a) civil works - 0%; (b) goods
consisting of pharnaceuticals, medical and other equipment and related supplies - 100%; (c) consultants
and overseas training (study tours and fellowships) - 100%; (d) local consultants - 0%; (e) local training-
0%; and (f) project administration - 66%). These percentages have been determined assuming that
basically all goods (pharmaceuticals, equipment and related supplies) would be imported, and that
international consultants would be recruited internationally.
Contingency Allowances
4.4   Price contingencies, representing about 4% of the total project cost, are based on estimated
inflation rates of 2.0% for 1996, 1.6% for 1997, 2.1% for 1998, 2.4% for 1999, and 2.2% for 2000.
It is assumed that the rate of exchange will depreciate in step with local inflation. Price contingencies
on local costs (expressed in US dollars) are therefore based on the same international inflation rates.
Physical contingencies represent about 8% of the total project cost.
Taxes and Duties
4.5    Civil work contracts would be subject to all applicable local taxes which is estimated at 10% of
the total civil works cost. All Goods (pharmaceuticals, equipment and related supplies) specifically
imported for the Project would be exempt from custom duties and taxes. International and local
consultants would be exempt from the Government's normal personal income tax. At negotiations, the
Government confirmed that tax and duty free status would be granted.
B. Project Financing
4.6    The total project cost is estimated at US$20.1 million (including taxes of US$0.2 million), with
a foreign exchange component of US$14.7 million. The financing plan is shown in Table 4.2 below.
The proposed Credit of US$18.5 million would finance 100 percent of the foreign exchange costs and
about 70 percent of local costs including taxes. The proposed Credit of US$18.5 million would finance
about 92 % of total project costs and the Government would contribute US$1.6 million equivalent or about
8% of total project costs.

Recurrent Costs
4.7    Recurrent Costs (US$1.0 million) will be comprised of salaries, utilities, Government in-country
travel and per diem, maintenance and operation of equipment, rents, and operating costs.  The
Government will finance 100% of recurrent costs.



39
Incremental Recurrent Costs
4.8   Several aspects of the Project are based on the rationale that there is a need for current health
sector expenditures to be maintained at least at the 1994 ratio to GDP of 3.6 percent. Significant gains
in efficiency are expected from the introduction of provider payments for services, and the liberalization
of budgetary expenditure rigidities. Greater reliance on fee for service will increase the level of private
spending for health which at the moment is quite low. The Project would require the Government to
provide incentive payments for the TB workers (US$82,000), and an estimated 10 percent contribution
to the civil works to cover local taxes (US$196,480). The total Government contribution is estimated
at US$1,605,127. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the Borrower would deposit its
counterpart funds for the Project quarterly and in advance in the project account, establishment of which
in a commercial bank is a condition of effectiveness.
C. Procurement
Summary of Procurement Procedures
4.9    The Project Coordination Unit would be responsible for managing procurement under the
proposed project. Specialized Procurement Consultants would assist the PCU in development of technical
specifications. Procurement of goods and services would be in accordance with the World Bank's
"Guidelines, Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits" dated January 1995 and "Guidelines for
the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency" dated
August 1981. Standard Bidding Documents (Goods) and Form of Contracts (Services) issued by the Bank
would be used as appropriate for the Goods and Services to be procured. Both works and goods would
be grouped in the most efficient manner possible and the proposed packaging would be reviewed by the
Association.  Table 4.3 below summarizes the proposed procurement arrangements.  Detailed
procurement arrangements indicating the number of procurement packages, their value and method is
presented in Annex 10 and described below.
Procurement of Civil Works
4.10   Civil Works (US$2.0 million). Civil works for the Facilities Rehabilitation and Pharmaceutical
Management Components consisting of modest rehabilitation and upgrading of 60 primary health care
facilities (US$1.74 million) and laboratory renovation (US$96,000). The Government will provide
approximately US$200,000 for any local taxes in civil works contracts. Due to the (i) small contract
values, (US$10,000 to US$50,000 per contract); (ii) works that are geographically scattered and spread
over time due in part by climatic conditions; and (iii) labor intensive techniques, procurement of small
scale civil works will be procured under lump sum, fixed price contracts awarded on the basis of
quotations obtained from three qualified domestic contractors in response to a written invitation. Each
year, the PCU will advertise to solicit expressions of interest from contractors in carrying out the civil
works to be financed under the project. The Borrower shall use bidding documents developed in the
ECA region.  The invitation shall include a detailed description of the works, including basic
specifications, the required completion date, and relevant drawings where applicable. Short letters of
invitation and a brief standard form of contract have been reviewed and accepted by the Association. The
award shall be made to the contractor who offers the lowest price quotation for the required work, and
who has the experience and resources to successfully complete the contract. Contracts above US$50,000
will be awarded on the basis on NCB procedures and for this purpose, the Borrower will use the Bank's
sample NCB document dated September 1995.



40
Procurement of Goods (except pharmaceuticals)
4.11   Goods (US$3.8 million) consisting of medical and other equipment and related supplies, would
be grouped to the extent practical to encourage competitive bidding. The following methods of
procurement would be followed:
(a)   International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures would be used for goods estimated
to cost US$1.8 million related to contracts estimated to cost more than US$300,000
equivalent each;
(b)   International Shopping procedures (IS) would be used for the procurement of goods that
cannot be grouped in packages above US$300,000. Quotations from at least three
suppliers from at least two different eligible countries would be required for contracts
estimated to cost less than US$300,000 and more than US$50,000. An aggregate limit
of US$1.8 million worth of goods excluding pharmaceuticals would be procured through
IS; and,
(c)    National Shopping (NS) procedures for publishing of materials (US$0.2 million), and
contracts up to US$50,000 each would be used for locally available, off-the-shelf
equipment and furniture items of small value and standard specifications up to an
aggregate amount of US$200,000; three quotations would be required.
Procurement of Pharnaceuticals
4.12   Procurement of US$8.7 million of essential drugs is a critical element in the Project and will
require special attention. All pharmaceutical procurement will follow World Bank Guidelines and the
policies established for financing procurement of pharmaceuticals and vaccines2.  International
Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures would be used for pharmaceuticals estimated to cost US$8.4
million. ICB Procurement for the Project, for pharmaceuticals, taken together with other equipment and
supplies will amount to about 83 % of all goods and pharmaceutical costs. The Bank's "Standard Bidding
Documents for Procurement of Pharmaceuticals and Vaccines" dated September, 1993 will be used for
ICB pharmaceutical contracts over US$300,000. Preparation of bidding documents for the first annual
tranche of drug procurement in a form acceptable to the Association is a condition of effectiveness. For
procurement of packages less than US$300,000, International Shopping will be used in an amount not
to exceed US$300,000. For certain drugs required under the TB component, which would involve close
cooperation with WHO, procurement of up to US$50,000 of drugs may be done through UN agencies,
where it can be demonstrated that speedier service and more economical prices are a result. In all such
cases, the World Bank's procurement policies and procedures would apply. For pharmaceuticals available
from only one source, Direct Contracting (DC), in an amount not to exceed US$50,000, would be used,
and prices would be derived from drug price bulletins issued periodically by UNICEF and similar sources
to determine reasonableness of prices offered by single bidders. The Project would finance the
procurement of drugs included on an Essential Drug List prepared by the Borrower and approved by
WHO. Standard bidding documents will be used and procurement will be under generic names.
Procurement lists will be published in "Development Business" six months prior to initiating procurement
to allow for any necessary registration. Registration of a drug would have to be completed before it can
be supplied in the Kyrgyz Republic. An exception to this requirement of completed registration could
be made, with the approval of the Association, for drugs registered in an OECD country or in the Russian
Federation. Also, all drugs to be financed under the Project would have to be registered in their own
2/Procurement Technical Note 4.3.2, August 27, 1993



41
country of manufacture and authorization certificates from all original manufacturers will be required.
Drugs will be in standard formulation in agreed packages and no non-standard pack and mini powder bulk
material will be permitted except for such items which are procured for compounding of ointments and
mixtures at the dispensary level. Any special disease drugs procured will be per international treatment
regimens acceptable to WHO and UNICEF.
4.13   The MOH  would be responsible for final determination of the type and quantity of
pharmaceuticals to be purchased. The PCU would be responsible for administering the process and
ensure that all Bank Guidelines are followed. A pharmaceutical consultant may assist the MOH and the
PCU to develop the technical specifications of all pharmaceutical procurement. Wholesalers in the
Kyrgyz Republic will sign an agreement with the MOH that details the respective responsibilities of each
in the distribution of drugs. Preparation of these distribution agreements in a form acceptable to the
Association is a condition of effectiveness.
4.14   Procurement from UN Agencies. Should it be determined that procurement of drugs and other
related medical supplies in the health sector could be carried out in a more efficient and effective way
from UNICEF or other UN Agencies, then such procedure could be used, subject to prior review by the
Association.
Preference for Domestically Manufactured Goods
4.15   For contracts for goods, including pharmaceuticals, to be awarded on the basis of ICB, the
Borrower may grant a margin of preference of 15% or the amount of applicable customs duties,
whichever is lower, to qualified domestic manufacturers of goods in accordance with World Bank
Procurement Guidelines (January 1995).
Procurement of Consulting Services
4.16   Consulting Services (US$3.3 million). Specialists and/or consulting firms financed under the
Project would be hired in accordance with the "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World Bank
Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency" dated August, 1981. For specialized short-term
consulting services to the PCU an amount of US$90,000 (4 person months of specialized short-term
procurement advice) will be authorized, as an amendment to the on-going contract with the firm providing
long-term procurement advice to the PCU selected competitively and already in place under the Social
Safety Net Project. This arrangement would insure continuity and fast response. There will be a need
to consult closely with WHO on the introduction of modem medical practices that meet WHO guidelines,
and approximately 25 consultant months of WHO individual specialist services will be sought, largely
for the Primary Health Care component.
4.17   The Provider Payments component will require the services of an international firm for up to 34
person months of specialized technical assistance (US$820,000). The component will extend the lessons
of the reform program now underway in the Issyk-Kul oblast. The Issyk-Kul pilot project will continue
until December 31, 1996, and recommendations on the basis of the evaluation of its impact and
effectiveness will be incorporated into the Health Project.  Consultants previously hired by the
Government for the Issyk-Kul pilot project under previous financing may be considered for submission
of a proposal to assist in carrying out the Provider Payments component, with the prior approval of the
Association.



42
4.18  In addition, approximately 390 consultant years of local consultants (US$870,000) will be
required to implement the sub-components of the provider payment component. Over the four year
period of project implemeritation, this will involve approximately 100 local consultants a year, of which
more than 50% (220 person years) will be involved in the management of the 300 family practice groups
being formed under the Project. These local consultants will be selected and hired under contracts used
by the TCC for local consultants which have been approved by the Association. The TCC would prepare
a manual outlining the procedures used for selection and hiring of these consultants. The management
of these contracts will be under the administration of the specialized associations being created under the
Project to represent the interests of the various medical groups, i.e the Primary Care Physicians
Association, the Health Fund, the Hospital Association, and the Accreditation Commission. The TCC
will oversee the hiring process and the PCU has developed standard documentation for employment,
reporting and payments. The Association has reviewed and approved this documentation. Annex 8
provides the descriptions of the Terms of Reference for the proposed consultants. Local consultants will
also be provided to the TCC and PCU (54 person years) and local consultants/engineers (15 person
years).
Training
4.19 Training (US$1.2 million). For local and overseas training and study tours financed under the
Project, the qualifications of candidates along with their course of study, and proposed training
institutions and costs, and in-country local training courses would be subject to prior review by the
Association. Candidates would be selected on the basis of established criteria, to be agreed upon with
the Association as part of the annual reviews. Local training courses will be offered in Bishkek and at
model hospital facilities in the oblasts throughout the country. Up to 200 short-term training courses
(averaging about one week in length) will be offered to approximately 4000 medical and administrative
personnel of the MOH. The Government's Training Center, the Center of Continuous Medical Education
(CCME) will organize and manage most of this training activity at its facilities in Bishkek and Osh.
Other training will be carried out at model hospital facilities participating in the program, utilizing
trainers trained at the CCME who will then travel to the different oblast and raion sites to mount short
term courses there. The CCME is the only professional medical training facility servicing the medical
community and the MOH in the country. The Project will finance approximately US$400,000 of local
training costs, and US$400,000 of international training. The Government will contribute US$400,000
for local training costs. Annex 9 summarizes the training to be financed under the Project.
Project Administration
4.20   Project Administration (US$0.6 million) consists of the Technical Coordination Committee (TCC)
and the PCU. Part V of this report provides a full description of the arrangements for project
administration and management. The PCU has signed an inter-ministerial agreement with the MOH.
The PCU currently has a procurement consulting firm under contract.

Notification of Business Opportunities
4.21   A General Procurement Notice was published in the March 31, 1996 edition of the UN
publication Development Business and, as appropriate, would be updated and published annually
thereafter. For pharmaceuticals, the list of essential drugs to be procured in the first year of the Project
was published in this notice to give bidders the opportunity to register their drugs. This will be the list
of drugs which may be financed under the Credit. For goods to be obtained by ICB, individual bidding
opportunities would be advertised in a major local newspaper 30 days prior to availability of bidding
documents and transmitted to potential bidders who expressed interest in bidding in response to the
published General Procurement Notice. The local advertisement procedure would be repeated for all
bidding packages utilizing ICB. These advertisements would be in the English language.
Project Preparation Facility (PPF)
4.22   A PPF (P-923-KG Health) in the amount of US$0.5 million was signed June 14, 1995 and
amended on December 28, 1995 to advance project preparation. Work includes consulting services to
the TCC and the PCU for the preparation of procurement arrangements, and to the component areas of
Primary Health Care, Facilities Rehabilitation, Provider Payments and Pharmaceutical Management.
Study tours and a limited amount of equipment and supplies are also eligible expenses. Rehabilitation
of the Drug Testing Laboratory was initiated under small scale civil works. Procurement will follow
Bank Guidelines.
Review By the Association of Procurement Decisions
4.23   The documentation for all procurement transactions not subject to prior review by the Association
would be subject to ex-post review during the Association's periodic project supervision missions. To
ensure compliance with the World Bank's Procurement Guidelines the following procurement actions
would be subject to prior Association review:
(a)    Civil Works: All works (including repairs to existing facilities and installation of equipment)
shall be procured under lump sum, fixed price contracts. The PCU will use a simplified version
of the standard bidding documents, developed by ECA region, which would be based on a
simplified bill of quantities including, inter alia, the method of payment and a form of contract.
The PCU would be responsible for the evaluation and recommendation of award of contracts.
Technical verification of the work carried out would also be the responsibility of the PCU in
cooperation with the MOH. Prior to award the Association would review the first two
rehabilitation contracts for work on health facilities and monitor other awards and works on a
selective basis during project supervision.
(b)  Goods induding pharmaceuticals: In general, pre-identified master lists of goods, indicating
grouping, along with estimates of cost and proposed method of procurement would be pre-
reviewed by the Association. Items would be grouped to the extent practical to encourage
competitive bidding and to permit bulk procurement. Bidding documents, evaluations and
recommendations of awards for goods and pharmaceuticals procured under DC and ICB would
be subject to prior review by the Association.
(c)  Consultants: The selection of consultant firms and individuals would be in accordance with the
World Bank Guidelines for Use of Consultants (August, 1981). Terms of Reference for all
consulting assignments would be subject to prior review by the Association. Terms and
conditions of contracts for all technical assistance assignments, invitation packages and evaluation



45
reports of assignments costing more than US$100,000 each for firms, and US$50,000 each for
individual consultants would be subject to prior review by the Association. The use of WHO
consultants for specialized medical expertise associated with introduction of WHO standards
would be in accordance with the Bank's policy on use of Advisory Services by UN Agencies
Under Bank-Financed Projects.
4.24   After award, should any material modifications or waiver of the terns and conditions of a
contract result in an increase above 15% of the original price, the Association would reserve the right
of prior review of such modifications (included would be modifications in contracts for consulting
services).
4.25   General Review: The Association would monitor procurement activities, contract management,
and project record-keeping during supervision missions. The Association would undertake a focussed
project launch workshop in June, 1996 and a comprehensive mid-term review of project progress; both
activities would place special emphasis on implementation including procurement matters.
Procurement Monitoring and Reporting
4.26   The PCU would be responsible for establishing and maintaining a computerized procurement
monitoring and reporting system acceptable to the Association. Status reports from this system would
accompany the PCU's regular quarterly project progress reports sent to the Association.
Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR)
4.27   Preparation of a Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) is in progress for the Kyrgyz
Republic. The Government has initiated reforms in the public procurement system and new legislation
is expected in March, 1996. Following this standard procurement documents specifically designed for
the Kyrgyz Republic will be developed.
D. Disbursements
Schedule
4.28   The proposed Credit would be disbursed over about five years which includes six months for the
finalization of accounts and submission of withdrawal applications and other reports. No standard
disbursement profile exists for the Kyrgyz Republic. The Project is expected to be completed by
December 31, 2000 with a Credit Closing Date of June 30, 2001.
