4.30   The standard disbursement profile for population, health &  nutrition projects requires
approximately 32 quarters or eight years to disburse a credit. Notwithstanding this profile, the pattern
of disbursement for this Project has most of the funding for training and technical assistance taking place
during the first two years of project execution. A five year period is shown above as it is expected that
the Project will become effective early in FY97. The last year of disbursement anticipates final
disbursements in FY01. The Project's administrative arrangements are already in place and operating
under the PPF and there is a mobilized and trained Project Coordination Unit in place and working.
Procurement actions for civil works have been prepared under the PPF and would be ready to initiate
upon Credit effectiveness. Bidding documents for the first year's drug purchase will be prepared under
the PPF and would be ready to issue upon Credit effectiveness. The positive list of the drugs eligible
to be financed under the Credit has been published in Development Business on March 31, 1996 and the
drug testing laboratory has been rehabilitated under the PPF to allow early registration. The entire PPF
(US$0.5 million) is expected to be disbursed before Credit effectiveness. The disbursement schedule in
Table 4.4 allows for some lag time in actual disbursements to account for normal delays in preparation
and presentation of documentation. While ambitious, the disbursement schedule is attainable.



47
4.31   For expenditures relating to (a) contracts for goods valued at less than US$50,000 equivalent,
and (b) contracts for works each valued at less than US$50,000 equivalent, reimbursements would be
made on the basis of Statement of Expenditures (SOEs). The documentation to support these expenditures
would be retained by the PCU for their respective project activities for at least one year, after receipt by
the Association of the audit report for the year in which the last disbursement is made.  This
documentation would be made available for review by the auditors and Association supervision missions.
Disbursements for all other items would be made against full documentation and respect procurement
guidelines where applicable. Supervision missions would review documentation retained by the Borrower
in the field. If ineligible expenditures, included those not justified by the evidence furnished, or amounts
in excess of agreed disbursement percentages are financed from the Special Account, the Association
would have the right to withhold further deposits into the Special Account. The Association may exercise
this right until the borrower has: (i) refunded the amounts involved, or (ii) (if the Association agrees)
submitted evidence of other eligible expenditures that the Association can use to offset the ineligible
amounts. The minimum amount of each withdrawal application for direct payment would be US$50,000.
Special Account
4.32   To facilitate and expedite disbursements, a Special Account (SA) in dollars would be established
by the Borrower in a commercial bank on terms and conditions acceptable to the Association. An initial
deposit of US$250,000 would be made, representing estimated average expenditures to be paid from the
SA over a three-month period. To facilitate payments of rehabilitation activities, funds may be
transferred when payments are due or not more than 90 days in advance in Kyrgyz som directly from the
SA to bank accounts in branches or correspondent banks at the oblast or city level. All foreign
expenditure payments would be made directly from the Special Account. The amount of the initial
deposit will be reviewed after six months and, if necessary, increased.
4.33   The Special Account would be in the name of the Borrower and would be administered by
authorized staff of the PCU. Applications for replenishment of the SA would be submitted to the
Association by the PCU monthly or whenever the account is drawn down by about 50% of the initial
deposit, whichever comes first.
4.34   The SA would be maintained in a commercial bank. It has been confirmed that the chosen bank
must be capable of meeting the requirements of the Association with respect to opening and maintaining
the SA. The bank chosen must be: (i) financially sound as demonstrated by its latest accounts, as audited
by an internationally recognized auditing firm; (ii) have a significant foreign correspondence network
covering all major currencies; (iii) have a reasonable capacity and experience for issuing letters of credit,
for making direct foreign payments and other international transactions with appropriate arrangements
for the training and development of its staff; (iv) be capable of performing a wide range of local banking
services, including cash payments, transfers to other domestic banks, issuance of debit notes, application
of conversion rates from foreign currencies, and maintenance of adequate accounts for the SA; (v) be
willing to issue a Comfort Letter to assure that amounts deposited in the SA would not be set off or
otherwise seized or attached to satisfy amounts due to the bank by the Borrower; (vi) be willing to
maintain adequate accounts as required by the Association, provide to the Association monthly bank
statements, and any other information considered necessary (copies of all transactions would be routinely
submitted to the implementing agencies); and (vii) be willing to charge reasonable rates for its services
and provide reasonable interest on balances held from time to time.



48
E. Project Accounts, Budgets and Audits
Project Accounts and Budgets
4.35   Financing of project activities would proceed through a Project Account (PA) to be opened by
the PCU, through which project funds would be channeled.
Accounts and Audits
4.36   The Borrower would be responsible for the appropriate accounting of the funds provided by the
Association under the Credit, for reporting on the use of these funds, and for ensuring that audits of the
financial statements or reports are submitted to the Association. Under the PPF, a computerized
accounting system identical to the one already established for the Social Safety Net Project, has been
established under the PCU's financial reporting system. The project accountant in the PCU would
maintain consolidated accounts for the Project and would prepare quarterly financial reports. The
accounts would be maintained for all project funds, i.e. Credit funds and local contribution.
4.37   All project records and accounts, including those for the SOEs, the SA, and PA would be audited
annually in accordance with the "World Bank Guidelines for Financial Reporting and Auditing of Projects
Financed by the World Bank," (March 1982). An international auditing firm would be appointed to
perform project audits. The Association would be provided within six months of the end of each fiscal
year with an audit report of such scope and detail as the Association may reasonably request, including
a separate opinion by the auditor on disbursements against certified statement of expenditures.



49
PART V. PROJECT MANAGEMENT, IMPLEMENTATION, EVALUATION AND
SUPERVISION
A. Status of Project Preparation
5.1    The Project is in an advanced stage of preparation. The Ministry of Health has established a
Technical Coordination Committee (TCC) and appointed a director, and four technical component
coordinators (CCs) for each of the major activities in the Project: primary health care, facilities
rehabilitation, medical care provider payment and pharmaceutical management. The MOH has also
signed an agreement with the existing Project Coordination Unit for the Social Safety Net Project, which
will be used to manage the procurement, disbursement and administrative reporting requirements of the
proposed Health Sector Reform Project. Two MOH staff are seconded to work within the PCU, receive
hands-on training and ensure close coordination between the two entities.
5.2    A pilot TB project, funded by the Policy and Human Resources Development (PHRD) grant from
the Japanese Government, began in October, 1995 to ensure it fits within the Project's overall objectives
to make more effective use of scarce resources by reducing the length of hospitalization, number of beds,
and other costly interventions. The pilot will serve to evaluate and assess the effectiveness of comparing
partial hospital based treatment for TB to a fully ambulatory treatment from the beginning of therapy.
The results of the pilot project will guide the final choice of diagnostic and treatment policies for the IDA
supported Health Project.
5.3    USAID is funding a provider payment pilot project in Issyk-kul Oblast. A three year effort, due
to complete its first phase in December 1996, it will provide excellent experience and working models
for wider application. An evaluation of this experience will be conducted by the Government using PPF
funding. The results will be incorporated into the IDA-funded Project, which is due to begin immediately
after effectiveness.
5.4    Understandings have been reached during the pre-appraisal mission with other donors on
collaboration with regards to certain aspects of the Project. The roles of UNICEF and IDA supported
projects in the area of ARI/CDD have now been defined. Similarly, the roles of GTZ and IDA have
been better delineated, so that promotional activities in adult health lifestyles and family health will
complement and support each other so that program and resources can be strengthened and expanded.
B. Overall Project Management and Implementation
5.5    The overall implementation and administration of the Project will be the responsibility of the
Ministry of Health. To assist in the various aspects of the Project, the MOH will depend upon several
entities to provide policy advice, technical direction and specialized services. These will be carried out,
respectively, by a Project Policy Council (PPC), a Technical Coordination Committee (TCC), and the
Project Coordination Unit (PCU) of the Social Safety Net Project. Annex 6 provides a description of the
management and implementation arrangements.
Project Policy Council
5.6    The Project Policy Council, which was formed in July, 1995, will oversee the policies undertaken
by the MOH and other entities involved in project implementation to reform the health sector. The
Project Policy Council is chaired by the Minister of Health or his designee, and members include MOH
Deputy Ministers, representatives from the involved departments of the MOH, Chui and Issyk-kul Oblasts
and Bishkek city medical officials, Pharmacia, representatives from the medical community and a



50
representative from the Ministry of Finance. It will advise the Minister on all important policy aspects
of the Project and would meet once every three months, or more often if its Chairman so decides. The
Technical Coordination Committee (TCC) would serve as the secretariat of the Project Policy Council.
Technical Coordination Committee
5.7    The Technical Coordination Committee, which has been formed and has been operational since
August 1995, will manage the technical implementation of the Project. The TCC is headed by a Director
who reports to the designated Deputy Minister of Health, who serves as the Ministry's counterpart and
chief liaison officer with the Association for the Project, on a day-to-day basis. It is comprised of a full-
time Director and four technical advisors, found acceptable to the Association, who are designated as
Component Coordinators. The Director has a relevant degree and experience in the health sector. The
Director and technical staff are assisted by one administrative assistant. The TCC functions on a
permanent basis during the course of the Project. In addition, current operating personnel from each
organization who are assigned to implement a specific activity (e.g. tuberculosis, drug regulatory
organization, etc.) within each of the four components would be named as a liaison person to the TCC,
and would be responsible for the day-to-day management of their respective activities.
5.8    The TCC provides the technical direction to the several components and subcomponents. It
formulates and recommends for approval any policy reforms associated with the Project, and periodically
assesses the status of and progress towards overall project goals. It coordinates activities among the
components, and also coordinates the project's activities with the other donor activities in the health
sector. It coordinates the budget planning and control of project funds, and coordinates with the Ministry
of Finance as required. Problems which are not resolved at the component level are to be raised to the
entire TCC. The TCC will request and authorize the PCU to prepare disbursement documentation for
signature by the Minister or his designee for payment of goods and services. Finally, the TCC maintains
progress indicators, designs and arranges for periodic project evaluations and manages the periodic
meetings of the Project Policy Council. Before negotiations the MOH has provided office space, local
telephone service, local transportation expenses, an administrative assistant and two positions seconded
to the PCU funded through MOH budgetary resources. During negotiations assurances were obtained
that the Borrower shall maintain the TCC with adequate authorities to effectively coordinate the activities
under the Project under the management of a director and four component coordinators, all with
qualifications and experience acceptable to the Association, assisted by an administrative assistant. The
project finances specialized technical services, the TCC Director, the four TCC technical component
coordinators, office equipment and one vehicle.
5.9    During negotiations assurances were obtained that the Borrower would prepare, under terms of
reference satisfactory to the Association, on or about August 31 of each year, an annual progress report
integrating the results of its monitoring and evaluation activities on the progress achieved in the carrying
out of the Project and the action plan during the period preceding the date of said report and setting out
the measures recommended to ensure the efficient carrying out of the Project and the achievement of the
Project's objectives during the period following such date; and review with the Association, by October
15 of each year, or such later date as the Association shall request, said report and thereafter take all
measures required to ensure the efficient completion of the Project and the achievement of the objectives
thereof, based on the conclusions and recommendations of the said report and the Association's view on
the matter. The Government would make annual allocations and execute budgetary resources to the MOH
in accordance with the funding requirements of the Project, and in accordance with its policy statement
dated April 5, 1996 to the Association.



51
Component Coordinators
5.10   The Director of the TCC is assisted by four technical Component Coordinators (CCs) who
provide policy and technical direction to the four functional sectors of activity under the Project, namely
(i) primary health care, (ii) pharmaceutical management, (iii) medical care provider payment, and (iv)
facilities management. The CC's responsibility is to ensure that the day-to-day managers, i.e. the
individuals within each activity who are assigned the responsibility of implementing specific activities,
receive the necessary support and policy guidance to execute their activities, and also to ensure that the
separate activities are coordinated within the overall program of reform. This includes the coordination
with other donor activities, and the sharing of information and problem solving among all the
participating agencies and entities. They are responsible for preparing training plans and arranging for
their execution within their respective sector. They are responsible for reporting to the TCC Director
all significant activities, progress, problems or policy issues which emerge in their sector, and making
recommendations for action. They follow up on all relevant recommendations of the Project Policy
Council, and prepare or cause to be prepared any reports required in the execution of the activities within
their sector.
Project Coordination Unit
5.11   The PCU of the Social Safety Net Project, which is under implementation, has been requested
to serve as the procurement and administrative consultant of the MOH for the Health Sector Reform
Project under the PPF. The MOH has negotiated and signed an intergovernmental agreement with the
PCU which will reimburse the PCU for those direct and indirect costs associated with the Health Project,
and these costs shall be funded under the Credit. The PCU will service the specific needs of the Project
in those administrative functions required for efficient project management and implementation; it will
do so at a cost considerably less than setting up a separate PCU in the MOH. Before negotiations the
MOH has provided the PCU with two positions, which at the end of the Project would revert to the MOH
and serve as a nucleus for the MOH to establish a procurement capability within the MOH in the future.
These MOH staff working within the PCU will receive hands-on training, and ensure close coordination
between the two entities. Salaries will be paid from the MOH budget. The Project will finance the
services of general and specialized procurement specialists in pharmaceuticals, medical equipment, and
civil works adrninistration. During negotiations assurances were obtained that the Borrower shall
maintain the PCU and vest it with the responsibility for procurement, preparation of disbursement
requests and reporting in respect of the Project under the management of a director, with qualification
and experience acceptable to the Association, assisted by support staff in adequate numbers. Specifically,
the PCU will perform the following functions:
(a)  Procurement:  provide assistance, advice, and contract management to the implementing
departments and entities in the procurement of civil works, equipment, pharmaceuticals and
related services. It would also provide assistance, advice and contract management in the
recruitment of consultants and technical assistance/training services. The PCU would prepare
all tender documents and technical specifications, and manage the process of receiving and
evaluating competitive proposals (with the active participation of the TCC and Component
Coordinators within the TCC). Ensure that all procurement actions meet Bank Guidelines and
that Association no objections are sought and obtained as necessary.
(b)  Finance and Accounting: prepare and maintain detailed project and summary accounting and
financial analysis records and reports. It would also arrange for and execute all necessary project
account audits.



52
(c)  Disbursements: acting on the advice of the TCC and the Component Coordinators within the
TCC, prepare and process Bank withdrawal applications and disbursements of project funds, and
maintain all necessary special accounts of project funds.
(d)  Reporting: prepare all necessary reports required by the Association. For technical and periodic
quarterly progress reports, the PCU will insure that the TCC and Component Coordinators
prepare their contributions in a timely manner.
(e)  Civil Works: Design and implement a system of supervision involving the local comrrmunity,
MOH on-site personnel and independent engineers.
(f)  Training: train the MOH on-site personnel in accounting.
C. Project Monitoring and Evaluation
5.12   The Project includes a mechanism for monitoring the progress in its implementation and
measuring its impact. This mechanism is based on a set of indicators developed specifically for each of
the subcomponents of the Primary Health Care component and for the Facilities Rehabilitation, the
Medical Provider Payment and the Pharmaceutical Management components. The indicators are adjusted
to the disparities in the complexity and newness of the Project's various activities. Annex 11 provides a
detailed description of actions to be taken by the Government as well as details on the performance
monitoring indicators. Monitoring is carried out by the TCC, assisted by consultants.
5.13   The most important expected result of the Primary Health Care component is to improve the
health status of the population, concurrent with Ministry of Health and MANAS goals.  This
improvement in health status will be achieved by expanding effective ambulatory care for diseases of
importance and by providing the essential drugs, facilities, training and equipment to do such. As a result
of this rationally targeted expansion of the ambulatory care system along with accepted clinical and
preventive protocols, access to appropriate care improves by definition and quality of care parameters will
indicate a definite improvement.
5.14   An important aspect of the progress of the Project will involve the close monitoring of the
schedule and rate of rationalization of health facilities. Much of the economic analysis derives from the
ability and commitment of the health authorities to undertake a serious examination of the extent to which
consolidation of present redundant health facilities and the amount of budget resources they absorb can
be redirected towards primary health care and essential services. The MANAS program will specifically
address this issue, and the policy paper presented by the Government will propose general targets and
objectives. The reviews conducted by the Association during the Project will specifically address this
issue and annual discussions with the Borrower will include progress to date and expected results in the
coming year.
5.15   Other expected results will be to improve the clinical effectiveness of the delivery system by
introducing and disseminating tested and accepted ambulatory treatment protocols for PHC targeted
diseases. The concurrent provision of needed pharmaceutical in an efficient and safe health service
facility will result in the improved clinical effectiveness of the delivery system. When a health system's
clinical effectiveness occurs within the framework of targeting diseases of importance from mortality and
morbidity points of view, the health status of the population improves. The Medical Care Provider
Payment component methodology is intended to encourage providers to treat patients on an out-patient
basis, discourage unnecessary referrals and reduce hospital days. This, along with changes in protocols
formed under the other components, is expected to result in savings and efficiencies in the system.



53
PART VI. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS OF THE PROJECT
A. Framework and Justification
Integration in Country Assistance Strategy
6.1    The Bank Group's Country Assistance Strategy for FY96-98 for the Kyrgyz Republic is designed
to support the transition to a more market-oriented and macroeconomically stable economy by (i)
facilitating growth; (ii) supporting structural reform; (iii) assisting the Government to protect vulnerable
groups; and, (iv) strengthening institutional capacity. The proposed project is an integral part of this
strategy. Health service delivery, predominantly financed and provided by the public sector, is a key area
for structural reform. The proposed project aims to assist the Government to rationalize expenditures in
the health sector and place it on a more cost-effective and sustainable financial basis through extensive
structural reform of the health service delivery system. This is also consistent with the recommendations
of the recent Poverty Assessment which emphasized, inter alia, the importance of using public social
expenditure funds as effectively as possible in order that quality of, and access to, basic social services
can be improved.
Choice of Project Interventions
6.2    The Project aims to assist the Government to (a) reorient public resource flows to more cost-
effective interventions; (b) sustain cost-effective choices by medical providers through changes in the
incentive structure; and, (c) achieve health gains through strengthening the quality of care at primary
levels (ambulatory polyclinics and FAPs).
6.3    Although the current financial crisis has placed great strain on the quality and availability of
health services, the emphasis of the proposed project is not on short-term financing but on structural
reform. The analysis of the health system presented in Part I emphasized the structural inefficiencies
which characterize the sector. Additional financial resources for the sector without reforms to encourage
cost-effective resource use would result in scarce public funds achieving less health effects than they
potentially could. Furthermnore, the pervasive biases in resource allocation patterns indicate that there
is considerable scope for savings to be gained through rationalization of resource use in more cost-
effective directions. Realization of these potential gains and re-investment into the health sector would
enable the Government to improve the quality and availability of services, even in the current fiscal crisis.
In other words, application of cost-effectiveness criteria to project design narrows the apparent policy gap
between short-term resource needs and longer-term reform needs.
6.4    Cost-effectiveness in the context of the health sector is defined as the achievement of the highest
health gains (measured by DALYs) at the lowest cost. Although cost-effectiveness was most explicitly
applied in the selection of interventions to be financed within the Primary Health Care component, its
broader application resulted in other Project components focusing on the strengthening of ambulatory care
more generally, given the current overemphasis on tertiary and inpatient care and given that there are
relatively few cost-effective inpatient interventions.
6.5    Successful implementation of a program to upgrade ambulatory care will reduce the demand for
hospital based care at the tertiary and secondary levels. The redirection of this demand towards a
strengthened integrated PHC level will imply that inpatient resources previously utilized to treat some
health conditions will become redundant. Project interventions are likely to demonstrate the potential for
savings which would be released if the excess inpatient capacity was reduced. Actual realization of these



54
savings would require the Government to close redundant facilities and to reassign or release staff from
inpatient facilities.
6.6    The Project itself does not design a program of facility or staff rationalization, although it
recognizes that these measures would be necessary in order to guarantee potential savings which could
be reinvested into primary health care. By strengthening the ability of the primary level to deliver
outpatient care, however, the project design will raise the visibility of excess inpatient capacity,
demonstrate that this excess capacity could be reduced without adverse health impacts, provide incentives
through changes in both provider payment systems and medical practice, and thereby strengthen the
ability of the Government to begin the difficult process of closures and staff rationalization. Additionally,
even if facility rationalization is undertaken slowly, and potential savings are slow to be realized, health
gains can still be expected as a result of improved diagnostic and treatment capacity in the primary care
levels. These issues are elaborated in the section on Risks and Limitations.
Systemic Approach
6.7    The approach of the proposed project is a systemic one aiming to change clinical protocols and
the regulatory, payment and management structures underlying inefficient resource use, rather than a
vertical approach targeting specific clinical programs alone (e.g. TB). Although the systemic approach
leads to a more complex project than would be expected from a vertical approach, it was the preferred
design. This is in view of international experience which suggests poor sustainability of improvements
to specific programs without accompanying changes in the system-wide incentives which resulted in
inefficient resource allocation and use in the first place. In order to monitor impact of the proposed
changes and in view of limited implementation capacity, however, the Project does not attempt to
intervene in all areas where structural reforms are deemed necessary. Some of the more significant
limitations of the project design are elaborated in section C below on Risks and Limitations.
B. Benefits
6.8    An effort is made to identify benefits expected from each component. It is necessary to bear in
mind, however, that the proposed interventions are integrally linked to each other and will jointly affect
outcomes in many cases. Cost benefit analysis is not attempted because both costs and benefits are shared
across components which have different coverage levels, and because it is very difficult to establish direct
causal links between project interventions and health benefits, let alone to measure these benefits. The
emphasis of the current analysis is on establishing the cost-effectiveness of project interventions.
Component A: Primary Health Care
Application of Cost-effectiveness Criteria
6.9    In identifying priority interventions within PHC areas, it was necessary to consider the incidence
or prevalence of the disease, the likely gains in potential years of life and/or disability adjusted life years
(DALY) resulting from the burden of disease, the effectiveness of the intervention, and the cost of the
intervention. A variant of the DALY methodology presented in the World Bank's 1993 World
Development Report was applied to the Kyrgyz situation (see note to Table 6.1 below).

6.10     Based on the DALY  analysis support to 10 conditions can be offered through ambulatory care
and is a cost-effective approach--tuberculosis, diarrheal diseases, childhood cluster (i.e. expanded
program of immunization), ARI, STDs, matemal conditions, perinatal conditions,
nutritional/endocrine, and cardiovascular conditions. Taking into account the cost-effectiveness
analysis and the existing pattem of donor support, the proposed project focuses on ARI/CDD, WRH
(which includes STDs), and TB. Together, these interventions target a significant portion of the
burden of disease and use limited funds in a very cost-effective manner. The PHC component will
finance training, pharmaceuticals and equipment (particularly for TB) necessary to implement new
medical protocols emphasizing prevention and the use of arnbulatory care.



56
Potential for Fiscal Benefits
6.11   The introduction of new treatment protocols emphasizing ambulatory care will not directly
lead to fiscal savings but will demonstrate the lower cost of provision of a service as compared to the
inpatient alternative. The source of these potential savings are from reductions in the hospital length
of stay and number of hospitalizations (admissions), which in turn would imply lower inpatient staff
and facility needs. To realize these savings, inpatient facilities and staff would have to be reduced.
This would lead to realized savings in the form of saved salaries (plus payroll contributions) and
utilities costs which together, in 1994, on average accounted for 66% of the operating costs of health
facilities.
6.12   An exercise was undertaken to estimate the magnitude of savings expected from changes in
the TB treatment protocols. A costing exercise was done for 1996 using 1994 prices to estimate the
cost of treatment of all TB cases if current protocols were not changed (the without project scenario)
and if 50% of TB cases were treated according to specified combinations of the proposed protocols
(three categories) while 50% continued to be treated according to current practice (the expected
scenario in the first year of the Project). 3
6.13   The analysis suggests that the cost of treating all TB patients for 1996 could be reduced by about
28% if only 50% of cases are treated using the new protocols. For subsequent years, as adoption of the
new protocol expands, the magnitude of potential savings can be expected to increase. Overall, it is
estimated that successful adoption of the TB protocols could reduce the cost of TB treatment by 25-50%,
relative to costs under current protocols, for each year of the Project and beyond. The main source of
these savings is from a reduction in the use of costly inpatient-days and a greater use of outpatient visits
in the treatment of TB cases. As mentioned, realization of these savings will depend on closure of excess
inpatient capacity.
6.14   The TB exercise provides an order of magnitude of potential savings. The methodology could
be applied to the other PHC interventions (ARI/CDD, WRH), data availability permitting.
Coverage and Access
6.15   The coverage of the new protocols is intended to be nationwide across all six oblasts, with a
population and potential beneficiary pool of 4.5 million. The different subcomponents aim to complete
training on different schedules and full coverage of the population will be achieved only by the end of
the Project's completion. The PHC component is likely to increase access to services in at least four
ways:
(i)    introduction of ambulatory alternatives to inpatient care will enable the poor, who may
not have been able to afford costs associated with inpatient care (e.g. lost wages, food,
bed-linen, etc.) to access treatment which they may otherwise have foregone;
3/ It must be noted that the comparison is between the scenarios where the full recommended treatment, including
drugs, under the respective protocol is implemented. In reality, many patients under the current protocol are not
supplied with the necessary drugs. In actual terms, therefore, actual operating expenses (relative to the current
situation) may increase if necessary drugs are supplied as recommended under the new protocols. Comparisons of
cost-effectiveness are meaningless, however, if they are not done across comparable outcomes (i.e. equivalent
numbers of cases treated and cured). The methodology used here is justified in that the different methods lead to
the same number of cases cured and the estimate is of potential costs and savings expected in achieving this
equivalent outcome.



57
(ii)   improved quality of services in the network of primary care facilities will also reduce the
transportation costs (time and material) which patients would have bome to access
inpatient care at the more centralized tertiary care facilities;
(iii)   provision of basic supplies to all FAPs will especially enable the rural population to
access basic services without having to incur transportation costs or delays; and
(iv)   the integration of services in some model sites will enable catchment area populations
(and also those who travel to these facilities) to obtain services with lower transaction
costs.
Health Impact
6.16   The provision of basic supplies and equipment in support of the new protocols under the PHC
component is expected to lead to improvements in the quality of services provided. This will lead to
gains in healthy years of life which would not have been achieved in the absence of the Project. It is
difficult to quantify expected health impact of the component, however, since exogenous factors (e.g. a
decline in nutritional status, changes in pollution levels, etc.) may obscure the causal links between
project interventions and health outcome. As demonstrated, however, the interventions target diseases
which had a high burden of disease. Moreover, monitorable targets in terms of changes in health
outcomes will be tracked as part of project performance monitoring.
Demonstration Effect
6.17   The PHC component will also finance integration of basic primary health care provision
(Tuberculosis, AIDS control, Dermatology, Venerology and Family Planning) on unified Model sites
situated in each oblast at selected oblast and raion facilities.  This will demonstrate the feasibility of
providing primary care in a unified setting and offer a model to be replicated throughout the country.
As stated above, the component can further demonstrate the potential savings to be gained from provision
of services on an ambulatory rather than inpatient basis for the selected interventions. These lessons
could be replicated for other types of interventions on a nationwide scale.
Component B: Facilities Rehabilitation
6.18   The enormous emphasis placed on inpatient care has resulted in the systematic underfunding of
primary care facilities such as ambulatories and FAPs. As a consequence of successful implementation
of ambulatory protocols under the PHC component and more cost-effective use of inpatient facilities by
medical providers under the Medical Care Provider Payment component, greater utilization of outpatient
facilities and reduced utilization of inpatient facilities is expected. In support of this anticipated change
in the pattern of utilization, the Facilities Rehabilitation component attempts to expand the physical
capacity for outpatient care provision. The Project will finance rehabilitation and conversion of existing
capacity rather than the creation of new facilities.
6.19   Since the target facilities will be distributed throughout the oblast, the whole population of Chui,
of 774,000, may be regarded as beneficiaries. This is also likely to result in higher effective gains to the
poor relative to the non-poor within Chui Oblast, since transportation costs to better resourced city
facilities are likely to have been more of a constraint to proper medical care for poorer households than
for others.



58
6.20   It is not possible to separately identify the gains from investments under the facilities component
alone, since it complements changes under the PHC and Provider Payments components in the strategy
to improve ambulatory care provision. For example, services not targeted under the Project, but which
can be performed in an outpatient setting (e.g. cataract operations), will also benefit from improved
outpatient facilities. Expansion in effective physical capacity for ambulatory care is essentially an
enabling factor for implementation of changes under the other project components. In addition, there may
be significant demonstration effects of this component for further facility rationalization within Chui
Oblast and for replication of criteria, process, organizational forms, etc. in all the other oblasts.
6.21   The sustainability of rehabilitation efforts under the component is addressed through the
reformulation and empowerment of a Buildings and Equipment Committee which will be responsible for
future maintenance of facilities.
Component C: Medical Care Provider Payment
6.22   The Medical Care Provider Payment Component proposes to change the methods used to pay
physicians, polyclinics and hospitals in order to introduce incentives for these providers to manage
resources and deliver care more efficiently through greater use of primary care. Payment to medical care
providers from public funds would no longer be made on the basis of production inputs but on the basis
of a combination of expected volume of cases, performance and outcome. The payer (in this case, the
proposed Health Fund) would focus on paying for appropriate care at the lowest cost, while the provider
(family practitioners and hospitals) would have greater flexibility to manage costs and select protocols
in delivering care. Under the reforms, the provider would be 'at risk' in that they would be responsible
for payments received and costs of care.
6.23   These features should encourage providers to treat patients on an outpatient rather than an
inpatient basis, discourage unnecessary referrals for specialist or diagnostic services, reduce the rate of
hospital admissions as well as the length of stay in hospital (LOS). The component will also develop a
quality assurance system to ensure that quality of services is not compromised. This component attempts
to reinforce the primary care focus of the other components discussed above.
6.24   During a phase in period, capitation based payments (i.e. payments per enrolled person to finance
the costs of a set of services) will be introduced for Family Practice Groups and global budgets for
hospitals. Over the course of the Project, case-based payment systems will also be introduced in the
hospital sector. Fee for service (using a fixed schedule) would be introduced for some priority diseases.
The main advantages and disadvantages of the proposed measures, relative to the current line-item
budgeting system, may be summarized as follows in Table 6.2 below:

6.25      Under this component, a mixed payment system  will be adopted in Bishkek City and in Chui
Oblast, extending models currently being piloted in Issyk-kul Oblast under the auspices of USAID. The
main expected benefit from the implementation of the Provider Payment component in these areas are
changes in the utilization pattern of inpatient and outpatient care: decreases will be expected in the
number of inpatient admissions, the average length of stay, and the number of secondary referrals; an
increase will be expected in the number of outpatient visits. An attempt was made to model these
changes in order to obtain an estimate of the impact on total costs of inpatient and outpatient care.
6.26     In a simplified model for Chui Oblast, three scenarios were estimated:  (i)  the current cost of
inpatient and outpatient care if number of admissions, length of inpatient stay (LOS), and number of
outpatient visits were at 1994 levels; (ii) the cost of inpatient and outpatient care if the number of
admissions and the LOS fell by 10% while the number of outpatient visits increased by 10%; and (iii)

the same pattern as in scenario 2 except that the expected changes were 20% rather than 10%. The
results are presented in Table 6.3.

6.27    In scenario 2, cost of care falls by about 13%  compared to the present, while in scenario 3, the
potential savings are almost 25 % of current costs.4 The model does not identify whether it is realistic
to expect changes of 10 or 20% in utilization patterns or what timeframe is being considered. It can be
said, however, that hospital admission rates in the Kyrgyz Republic are about 15-20% higher than the
OECD average, while hospital lengths of stay are as much as 50 % higher than OECD levels for selected
disease categories (although the case-mix is different in the Kyrgyz Republic). Thus the magnitudes of
change used in the model are not unrealistic. The pace at which efficiency gains can be expected will
probably accelerate in the latter years of the Project as providers participate and learn to use the new
systems. A similar exercise was done for Bishkek city.
6.28    Note: the estimates here reflect the expected effects due not only to changes in the provider
payment systems but also changes in treatment protocols and facility rehabilitation financed under the
other components. Moreover, these estimates indicate the level of overall cost reduction that could be
expected if decisions by providers to utilize primary care alternatives are sustained. It is noted that
realization of these potential savings is largely dependent on closure of excess inpatient capacity.
Component D: Pharmaceuticals Management
6.29    This component attempts to make progress in restructuring regulation, procurement and use of
pharmaceuticals towards cost-effective ends and to improve the overall supply of medicines. The main
benefits are expected in the following areas:
4/Actual savings may be lower than estimates given here since costs saved on the margin may be less than costs
saved on average. If the Government is committed to rationalize overhead costs such as salaries, any gap between
marginal and average cost-savings will be reduced.



61
Rationali2ing the Composition of Public Spending on Medicines
6.30   Since drugs procured under the Project will be essential drugs, a significant percentage is likely
to be dispensed to exempted patients and, hence, to be financed out of local health budgets. If public
spending levels remain constant, therefore, a greater proportion of public spending on medicines will be
used to buy cost-effective essential drugs. This change in composition of public drug expenditures is
expected even prior to the third year of the Project when the Government is required to limit public
spending exclusively to the drugs on the Essential Drugs List.
Net Consumption of Medicines
6.31   Since the drugs are to be sold, either for cash or for reimbursement from Article 10 funds, the
net increase in total consumption of medicines will depend, on the-one-hand, on the budgetary resources
available to purchase medicines, and, on the-other-hand, on the financial ability of consumers to incur
out-of-pocket expenses. The increase in supply of cheaply procured drugs at the distributor levels may,
however, lead to a decline in scarcity premia inflating drug retail prices. Net consumption is likely to
increase by a modest amount. As the Project will institute mechanisms to ensure that drugs procured
under the Project are distributed to all oblast Pharmacias, the increase in net consumption is expected to
be spread across the country.
Distribuion System
6.32   The component will assist in the re-capitalization of the weakest link in the pharmaceuticals
distribution system in the Republic: the oblast Pharmacias. These Pharmacias, nominally independent,
are deeply in debt, have no access to short-term credit, and have meager inventories. By providing these
regional distributors with a substantial supply of low-cost drugs, and if the Pharmacias are permitted to
retain a significant portion of their earnings from onward sale of the medicines, it is possible that one or
more of the distributors will be able to emerge as financially viable. Financially sound distributors which
may be able to compete with each other in the future, will help sustain improved availability of medicines
well beyond the duration of the Project.
Effective Regulation of Medicines
6.33   The component will provide financing critical to strengthen institutional capacity to assure the
safety and efficacy of medicines produced and/or distributed locally. Empowered regulatory bodies will
be better placed to enforce procurement guidelines and contribute to educating practitioners and users in
the use of essential drugs. Successful implementation of regulatory capacity building under the Project
is likely to be an effective means of sustaining the benefits from cost-effective procurement, distribution
and use of medicines in the country during and beyond the Project.
C. Risks and Limitations
6.34   The reforms proposed under the Project target some of the structures in the health sector which
are most difficult to change in an orderly manner. The aim is to restructure resource flows in the sector
and this necessarily implies that there are winners and losers.
Closure and Retrenchment
6.35   The potential efficiency gains identified from reforms cannot be achieved without rationalization
of inpatient facilities and staff. The issue of closure of facilities and staff retrenchment is an enormously



62
difficult one which many countries are grappling with in health and other sectors. Resistance to
rationalization efforts can be expected not only from medical professionals and auxiliary staff fearing job
losses, but users in catchment areas of facilities which are likely to be closed. Uncertainty and anxiety
in this regard may lead to resistance to the adoption of new ambulatory protocols and payment methods
even if closure is not an explicit program in the short-term. The Government would need assistance to
undertake a process of consultation with different constituencies such as doctors, other medical staff, users
in urban and rural areas, MOH staff, the Parliament, etc. to develop a program, design a compensation
package, identify re-training opportunities, disseminate information on the need, purpose and measures
necessary and generally build a core consensus on the issues.
6.36   The strengthening of primary and ambulatory service delivery is a precursor to inpatient capacity
reduction. The Project has focussed on this first process through the already complex set of proposed
reforms. While recognizing the necessity of assisting the Government with a capacity rationalization
strategy, the project preparation team did not think it feasible to include a facility and staff rationalization
program within the Project itself, especially given limited implementation capacity and reduced central
staffing levels in the MOH. Efforts have been made to coordinate project planning with relevant efforts
being undertaken through other programs. A facility restructuring program currently being developed
by the ODA will identify a detailed facility rationalization plan for Bishkek City, including the financing
needs. The MANAS group is also developing a master plan for facility rationalization. It is not clear
whether these efforts, combined with the urgency created by the continuing fiscal crisis, will be enough
to build the political will in Government necessary to undertake physical and human resource
rationalization. As results unfold, it will become clearer whether the issue will remain a priority for
support under the next Sector Adjustment Loan (SECAL).
Potential for Private Sector Displacement
6.37   In a real sense, there is little displacement likely from the large majority of interventions proposed
under the Project since the private sector is very small at the moment. Moreover, the primary health
interventions to be financed under the Project are mostly of a public goods type, with significant gains
to society (e.g. TB, STDs, CDD, ARI, adult/family health education). These are appropriate areas for
public financing since a potentially active private financing sector may not find these areas attractive and
may finance these services at levels below those which would benefit society at large.
6.38   An area where there is an unknown net impact on the private sector, is that of current
pharmaceuticals procurement under the Pharmaceutical Management component. Since the oblast
Pharmacias will be receiving drugs at a low cost, it may become more difficult for other distributors to
sell the same drugs profitably, i.e. at a price high enough to cover their acquisition costs. It is possible
that alternative sourcing of the drugs procured under the Project could decline, especially if quantities
procured under the Project represent a substantial portion of annual demand for the medicine. The
magnitude of this effect is impossible to predict at the moment because of the lack of information on the
composition of current drug consumption, the recent nature of private distributor activity in the country,
etc. In order to minimize any displacement effect, it will be necessary to carefully choose the list of
drugs to be procured under the Project and to spread out disbursement in simple tranches.
6.39   On the other hand, the Project may facilitate the growth of the private sector. The Project
finances a large amount of training in modern clinical and management techniques which can be used by
private providers of health care. Changes in provider payment systems promoted by the Project can be
utilized to compensate private providers as well as public sector providers. Under the pharmaceuticals
management component, the structure of on-lending is designed to re-capitalize distributors who may be
able to compete on a financially viable basis in future procurement and distribution activities.



63
Fiscal Sustainability
6.40   No significant new incremental costs are expected under the Project and the counterpart financing
required from the Government over the course of the Project is estimated as averaging annually at I %
of the 1994 budgetary expenditures on health. Of greater concern for the overall feasibility of the
Project, however, is the level of budgetary commitment expected for the health sector for the duration
of the Project.
6.41   Several aspects of the Project require that health sector budgetary expenditures be maintained at
the real levels of health budget execution of 1994.
(i)    As an example, in order to compensate providers under the new payment methods,
adequate public resources will be necessary in the Health Fund such that realistic
capitation compensation rates and hospital global budgets can be set. If public financing
declines beyond current levels, the financial viability and credibility of the new payment
system will be severely undernined.
(ii)    Further, the above analysis demonstrates that the increased volume of utilization of
outpatient facilities expected from the reforms will imply capital needs to finance
conversion to and rehabilitation of outpatient facilities.  This provides one of the
arguments for the proposed Covenant requiring Government to maintain public health
budgets at a reasonable level: even if savings are expected and realized through closure
of inpatient facilities, there would be considerable need to re-invest savings in upgrading
and expanding outpatient capacity in order to accommodate the desired changes in
utilization patterns.
(iii)   The public health system's access to the drugs procured under the pharmaceuticals
management component (but not the PHC component) is circumscribed by the total public
funds available in central and oblast budgets to purchase these drugs directly or through
reimbursement of exempted consumption.
6.42   As documented in Part 1, real expenditure levels in the Kyrgyz health sector have already declined
significantly during the course of transition, jeopardizing both effective coverage and quality of services
provided. In view of these earlier declines, and of the need to re-invest any savings generated by the
Project towards sustaining the reform process, it was the judgement of the project appraisal team that
further declines in health spending could delay, or effectively negate, the improvements in utilization and
health status expected from the reforms. These concerns have been discussed with the Government and
a covenant will be included to require that the level of total executed budgetary financing of the health
sector is not less than the 1994 executed level in real inflation adjusted terms (using IMF estimates of
GDP deflators) for the project implementation period.
6.43   Additional sources of financing of health services are also being explored. The USAID is
exploring the feasibility of a payroll tax based mandatory health insurance system through its existing
technical assistance program in Issyk-kul Oblast. Given the ongoing revenue shortfalls faced by the
Government, the likelihood of any additional financing being raised through payroll taxes seems unlikely
in the short-term. User fees for services, while already in operation for some types of services is not
extensive. The MANAS Group is discussing the introduction of a minimum package of cost-effective
services which would be provided free of charge and beyond which user fees could be introduced for the
non-poor. Given the extensive prevalence of poverty in the country and the likely administrative costs
of administering a fee system, net revenue expected if fees are introduced are likely to be modest at best.



64
It was also judged that revision of the exemptions policy for consumption of medicines is neither
politically nor administratively feasible in the short term, and again, may not generate significant savings
if implemented. Adherence to the covenant will thus require clear Government commnitment and inter-
sectoral adjustments within total public sector spending.
6.44   As has been demonstrated, if real budgetary levels are maintained, potential savings are realized
through facility rationalization, and realized savings are re-invested in primary care, the delivery of health
care in the Republic will be on a far more financially sustainable and cost-effective basis than it is at
present.



65
