Translated version of policy statement letter from the Prime Minister of the Kyrgyz Republic
Parliament of the Kyrgyz Republic
Parliament Building
Bishkek
April 5, 1996
Regional Vice President
World Bank
Mr. Johannes Linn
Dear Mr. Linn:
I am pleased to present to the Board of Directors of the International Development Association
for its consideration a Statement by the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic regarding its Policy toward
the future development of health care services in our country. This Policy Statement is an accurate and
concise distillation of the comprehensive strategy and detailed plan for health care reform that has been
developed under the MANAS program. The MANAS program, as you know, is the result of more than
two years' intense, collaborative work between the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic, the World
Health Organization, and other donors, and it commands the full support of the Ministry of Health. In
the very near future, this program will be presented to the Government for its review and endorsement,
after which it will be published. I relate these details in order to emphasize that the attached Policy
Statement is indeed a very carefully considered and true expression of our plans and intentions.
During preparation of the project to be financed under the proposed Credit from the Association,
a number of questions were raised in connection with the Government's policy. In this context, I wish
to take this opportunity to address these questions and to assure you that my Government is fully
committed to taking the following steps which, I believe, you will find reflected in the attached Policy
Statement:
(1)    during execution of the Project, the level of annual, consolidated state budgetary
expenditures implemented in the health sector will not be less than the level of such expenditures
implemented in 1994, taking into account adjustments for inflation, and savings resulting from
implementation of the Project will be re-invested in the health sector;
(2)    during execution of the Project, activities aimed at rationalizing and modernizing the
health sector will be continued, including gradual merging of specialized departments and health facilities,
an increase in the amount of resources allocated to primary and ambulatory care, and a reduction in
numbers of beds, staff, and other resources consumed in hospitals;
(3)    as part of this restructuring, the following specific actions will be taken: a) programs for
the treatment of AIDS and other STDs will be combined with other primary care treatment programs,
and new methods and protocols of treatrnent will be introduced in order to permit expanded ambulatory
treatment of STDs; b) results of pilot projects demonstrating the effectiveness of ambulatory treatment
of tuberculosis will be fully taken into account and reflected in the development of national standards for
treatment of TB and specialists and other staff will be re-allocated from in-patient TB facilities to primary
health care centers and specialized TB sanatoria will be gradually closed; c) capital expenditures will be



70                               ANNEX 1
concentrated, to the maximum extent possible, on the rehabilitation of existing health care facilities, and
capital expenditures will be made available for new construction of only general hospitals and other health
facilities that provide a broad range of services; d) during execution of the Project, the level of annual,
consolidated state budgetary expenditures allocated to the maintenance and repair of health care facilities
will not be less than the level of such expenditures implemented in 1994, taking into account adjustments
for inflation;
(4)    individuals and families will be allowed full freedom to choose from which health service
providers and physicians to receive treatment, and this freedom will be assured and protected;
(5)    a national policy concerning pharmaceuticals will be implemented, including: a) adoption
of all necessary legal, organizational and financial measures in order to ensure that only drugs which are
safe and of proven effectiveness will be available for use in the Republic; b) implementation of an
Essential Drugs Program; (c) starting from the third year of the Project, allocation of state budgetary
resources for the procurement, distribution, or provision of only pharmaceuticals which are contained on
the Republic's Essential Drugs List; d) continued elimination of obstacles impeding the establishment and
financial success of privately-owned pharmacies, distributors of pharmaceuticals, and pharmaceutical
manufacturers.
During negotiations of the Credit Agreement, held recently in Washington, another key question
was raised in connection with the Government's Policy, and I would like to take this opportunity to
clarify this as well.
The rationalization and modernization of the health care sector, and the allocation of increased
resources to support ambulatory methods of treatment, will necessitate the redeployment of a significant
number of specialists and other health care personnel. This will be a very complicated and at times
painful process and will have to be conducted with utmost care and concern for the people involved.
Considerable re-training will be required, some of which is envisioned to take place under the Project.
I wish to assure you, however, that the first priority of the Government is to improve the health of the
people of our Republic, and that we will not hesitate to take even painful steps if necessary in order to
achieve this goal.
Sincerely,
By Is/ A. Djumagulov
Prime Minister of the Kyrgyz Republic



71                             ANNEX 2.A
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 2.A: PRIMARY HEALTH CARE - WOMEN'S REPRODUCTIVE HEALTH
SUB-COMPONENT
A. Background
1.    There are one million women of reproductive age in the Kyrgyz Republic and, in 1993, just over
120,000 live births. This sub-component is designed to reach these women and impact upon their health
status and that of their newborns.
2.    Maternal, perinatal and reproductive health activities are implemented through the Maternal and
Child Health Department at the Ministry of Health (MOH). This department has shrunk from eight
positions to five positions in the last couple of years. There are plans to merge this department into the
Prevention and Treatment Department at the MOH, and thereby reduce staff positions even further. At
the regional level there is a Chief Pediatrician and a Chief Obstetrician for each of the 6 Oblast Health
Administrations and also for Bishkek City Health Department. There is one Medical Institute for training
medical students; 10 medical colleges for training paramedics; and a Department of Continuous Medical
Education for refresher training of doctors and paramedics. The Republican Institute for Obstetrics,
Gynecology and Pediatrics acts as a tertiary referral hospital and research center, and trains post-diploma
specialists.
3.    The Kyrgyz Republic has an extensive health service infrastructure from the district (Raion), to
the regional (Oblast) and Republican levels. The existing organizational structures in the health system
hamper the provision of optimnal care. For instance, the current system of urban health services is highly
specialized and fragmented. For gynecological problems and pregnancy a women must visit a women's
consultation center; for contraception and infertility, a marriage and family center; and for AIDS testing,
an AIDS center. Similarly, the separation of diagnosis of STDs in women's consultations from their
treatment in venerology clinics lessens the likelihood that women and their partners will in fact be treated.
Separating delivery in the maternity home from the provision of acute neonatal care in the children's
hospitals inhibits prompt treatment of sick babies. There is also a wastage of limited resources through
the duplication of services: for example normal maternal patients and "pathological" maternal patients are
treated in separate hospital departments with separate labor, delivery, surgical and post-delivery facilities.
Premature and sick and full-term babies are also kept in different departments.  Some of these
shortcomings are identified by the WHO/World Bank consultant Prof. H. Ramic in his report "Maternal
and Perinatal Health in the Kyrgyz Republic, June 1995 ". One of the aims of the Primary Health Care
Component is to merge specialist facilities and integrate their services at the primary health care level,
thereby streamlining the referral system and reducing the need for separate specialized hospitals,
departments and clinics.
Maternal Mortality Rates per 100,000 live births (MOH data)
1990             1991              1992              1993             1994
81.4             76.4              70.1             80.1              80.1
4.    The maternal mortality rate (MMR) at 80.1 per 100,000 live births, is far higher than that of
Russia (51), Ukraine (32), Costa Rica (20) or the European Union (10), despite the fact that over 95%
of births occur in hospitals and prenatal health care is widely available. The MMR remained stable



72                              ANNEX 2.A
between 1993 and 1994 at 80.1 per 100,000 live births having risen from a 1992 rate of 70. 1. The WHO
definition of maternal mortality and live births has not been adopted as yet and the true MMR may well
be 30% to 50% higher than the official Government rates. Birth intervals are short, only one year in
22% of women. Almost 10% of births are to women less than 19 years of age. The birth rate is
dropping in the current difficult socioeconomic conditions. Almost 75% of all maternal deaths are in
rural areas. Inter-oblast variation in maternal death rates can be quite substantial. Over the period 1992-
1993, in those oblast where the Ministry of Health made a concerted effort to supply drugs and train
primary health care workers, they were able to reduce the maternal death rates considerably. The
shortage of resources, constrained the Ministry of Health's ability to extend this support to all oblasts.
This sub-component aims to rectify this.
5.     The perinatal mortality in 1991 was 26 per 1000 births and is moderately high for a country with
universal coverage of maternal and child health services. It has however been dropping slightly since
1991. but the actual contribution of perinatal mortality to the infant mortality rate has been increasing;
from 25% in 1994 to 29% in 1995. The main cause of perinatal death are in descending order:
respiratory distress syndrome, birth hypoxia/asphyxia, intracranial birth trauma, and sepsis. The rate of
low birth weight in the Kyrgyz Republic is below 6 %, quite a low level even in developed countries, but
the birth weight specific mortality rates are somewhat high. Most of the problem lies in the survival of
newborns with 2,500 grams of birth weight or more. This is likely to be due to the quality of perinatal
medical care. The Kyrgyz Republic could probably reduce substantially this current level of perinatal
mortality if greater survival could be achieved for the heavier babies. The low rates of Caesarean section
(of which two thirds are emergency and only one third elective) and assisted deliveries could explain to
a large extent the high rates of mortality among the heavier babies.
6.    A comprehensive approach to the inpatient management of pregnant women, her labor and
delivery, and the care of her newborn infant has been shown to decrease mortality in other countries.
Cooperative efforts between obstetricians and pediatricians is essential to this success. Regionalization
of perinatal care consists of treating low risk pregnant women and newborns in raion centers and referring
high risk women, sick and/or premature infants to perinatal centers. The current list of risk factors for
pregnant women is too stringent and leads to the inclusion of almost 80% of women in the at risk
category, leading to unacceptably high referral rates to higher levels of care. As a result, minor problems
are overstated and serious ones may be overlooked. Given the difficult conditions for women in the
Kyrgyz Republic approximately 20-25 % of pregnant women can be expected to be at risk of morbidity
during pregnancy and childbirth. Internationally, the risk group is usually 10% or less. New protocols
will be introduced to rationalize this screening of pregnant women into high risk groups, thereby shifting
limited resources to the serious cases who need them the most.
7.    Deaths from eclarnpsia, hemorrhage and sepsis, the leading causes of maternal death in the
Kyrgyz Republic, can all be prevented using a range of cost-effective interventions at the primary health
care level. A third of maternal deaths are due to eclampsia (high blood pressure and swelling leading
to fits and coma) and these eclamptic deaths have increased in proportion to the other main causes,
hemorrhage and sepsis, over the last few years. This increase is thought to be due to lack of training and
basic equipment in prenatal care at the primary health care level coupled with a shortage of transport.
Abortion is also a leading cause of maternal deaths. Abortion rates are very high, with an average of
2.3 to 2.7 abortions per woman. Although reliable data on contraceptive prevalence is lacking, it would
appear that there is a large unmet need for contraceptives. Abortion remains the primary means of
fertility control. There is a particular need for contraceptive counselling in the postnatal period and after
an abortion. High rates of anemia in Kyrgyz women, about 30% of pregnant women, have consequences
for both the mother and her unborn child. Severe anemia in the mother will limit blood supply to the
fetus, increasing the risk of newborn asphyxia/hypoxia. In the event of hemorrhage during delivery,



73                              ANNEX 2.A
anemia greatly increases the likelihood of the women suffering from shock or death. As a result, routine
iron folate and multivitamin supplementation will be introduced for pregnant women.
8.    In general, hospital practices during delivery are fairly comparable to European practices, but
there are several major shortcomings which must be corrected: first and second stage of labor should be
limited in time; the use of forceps and vacuum extractors should be encouraged; the indications for
Caesarean section must be revised; the fetus should be monitored regularly; the prevention and
management of postpartum hemorrhage must be improved. This sub-component aims to upgrade the
obstetric skills of obstetricians and midwives to improve their monitoring of labor and their clinical
judgement on when and how to intervene with an intervention (labor induction, augmentation, vacuum
extraction, Caesarean section, etc.). Breastfeeding will also be encouraged in this sub-component, in
collaboration with UNICEF who plan to establish a Baby-Friendly Hospital Initiative, through the
development of a supportive environment in maternity homes (rooming-in, early mother-baby contact,
on-demand feeding, presence of family members, etc.).
9.     The current system does not sufficiently emphasize health education as part of prenatal care, nor
do sufficient educational and promotional materials exist. There is also a low level of knowledge of the
needs of pregnant women among the general population. In a country, such as the Kyrgyz Republic, with
very high literacy levels in males and females, this should not be the case. Information, education and
communications are essential to improve knowledge of family planning, nutrition and maternity care,
including recognition of danger signs of complications of pregnancy, among women, family members,
community leaders and health care providers. To rectify these shortcomings in the health system, this
subcomponent will focus on teaching counselling techniques to health workers; and will fund the
development, printing and distribution of a health education booklet for the general public.
10.   The epidemiological picture for syphilis and gonorrhea has fallen somewhere between the pattern
seen in developing countries; that is epidemic levels of diseases, and the picture seen in industrialized
countries; that is low and still falling rates of infection confined to high risk groups. Since 1993, there
has been a ten fold increase in the incidence of syphilis. This dramatic increase reflects a trend reported
in many other republics of the former Soviet Union. At the same time detection rates for gonorrhea have
declined after remaining at a stable level over the last two decades. This data suggests that the level of
unsafe sexual activity in the Kyrgyz Republic is increasing and less STD cases are seeking treatment in
the government health system. Little information is available on the incidence of chlamydia. Up until
1995, HIV had not been detected in the resident population despite a massive screening program. STD
and AIDS are managed through two separate vertical organizational structures that merge at the district
level. Access to STD treatment is limited to a few specialized dermatovenereal clinics in which people
must registers their names in order to obtain free treatment. The result is that most STD cases either treat
themselves or seek anonymous care from non-accredited private doctors. Adolescents are particularly
vulnerable because of their lack of money and information. More detailed information is available in the
World Bank report "Assessment of the Sexually Transmitted Diseases Program in the Kyrgyz Republic:
October 1994". One aim of this sub-component is to encourage the anonymous ambulatory management
of patients with syphilis and gonorrhea and the integration of venerology services into the primary health
care level.
11.   In sumnmary, the health infrastructure is extensive, women are educated and relatively mobile.
Family planning is still not readily available to the majority of women, but with donor assistance the
range of services are expanding. Safe abortion is offered through the government health system.
Maternal services are available at primary health care facilities, but are under-utilized. Delivery
attendants are trained, the vast majority of deliveries take place in hospital, but the clinical practices need
upgrading and a regular supply of essential drugs, supplies and equipment must be established. IEC



74                             ANNEX 2.A
capability is available but under-utilized. Improving the quality and efficiency of health care, including
decentralizing services, as well as developing a more comprehensive strategy for women's health, and
improving information, education and commnunication; will all be priority interventions in this sub-
component.
B. Role of Other Donors
12.   The German Government's KFW is supplying clinical equipment to all FAPS, SVAs and Central
Raion Hospitals in the Kyrgyz Republic. GTZ will fund a technical advisor to the Republican Health
Center to improve national health education and health promotion activities. USAID's Women's
Reproductive Health Program has been focusing on strengthening family planning services and
contraceptive supply. This extensive program has a number of contractors (Association for Voluntary
Surgical Contraception, JHPIEGO, The Futures Group, Population Communications Services)
implementing activities in the Kyrgyz Republic. UNFPA is supplying contraceptives, plans to establish
a regional office for Central Asia, and has a local country program officer in Bishkek. The Center for
Diseases Control, funded by USAID, and has an office with two regional advisors in Almaty and is
working on surveillance and computer training. UNICEF has a country office in Bishkek and plans to
develop a Baby-Friendly Hospital Initiative. The WHO/Euro and TICA supported MANAS Health Sector
Reform Project for the Kygryz Republic developed a national health reform masterplan in 1995, and will
assist with implementation of the masterplan in 1996. The WHO/Euro CAREC program will introduce
maternal and child health pilot sites and a sentinel surveillance system in two raions in the Kyrgyz
Republic (and five other Central Asian countries). WHO has a Central Asia regional advisor on AIDS
based in Almaty who could work closely on the integration of the STD/AIDS structures. There is a
WHO Information Center for Central Asia, in Bishkek, with a well stocked library of WHO materials
both in Russian and English. AIHA (American International Health Alliance) is working in various parts
of the former Soviet Union, and has conducted training courses on inmnediate neonatal resuscitation,
based on training courses developed by the American Pediatrics Association. Central Asian Partners, a
small NGO based in Bishkek, is working on the upgrading of obstetric skills in several health facilities.
13.   This sub-component complements the work of other donors (UNICEF, UNFPA, WHO CAREC
program, German Government KFW and GTZ, Central Asian Partners, BASICS, etc.).
C. Rationale for Bank Involvement
14.   This sub-component makes up for a major deficit in the assistance being provided by other donors
to priority health programs; namely a comprehensive safe motherhood and perinatal health intervention
for implementation nationwide. Many donors are providing small scale activities in a few selected health
facilities. USAID and UNFPA have focused on a specific element of safe motherhood; contraceptive
supply and family planning activities. This sub-component will focus on all the other components:
maternal health, perinatal health, and sexually transmitted diseases case management; and integrate them
with the family planning activities. The aim of this sub-component is to reduce the heavy burden of
maternal and perinatal deaths and ensure the long-term sustainability of a comprehensive maternal and
perinatal health program within the government health structure through a process of rationalization and
decentralization.
D. Women's Reproductive Health Sub-Component Description
15.   This sub-component will fund 5 months of technical assistance; a study tour; computers; training
equipment; clinical equipment for 206 health facilities- translation and printing of health worker training
materials; essential drugs for model Oblast training centers and 12 Raion hospitals, nationwide training



75                             ANNEX 2.A
of up to 2000 obstetricians, gynecologists, venereologist, neonatologists, pediatricians, and primary health
care workers.
16.   The long term goals of the Women's Reproductive Health sub-component are the:
(i)    Reduction of maternal deaths due to obstetric complications such as hemorrhage,
eclampsia, sepsis, obstructed labor
(ii)   Provision of a basic package of maternal care to all pregnant women
(iii)   Reduction of anemia in pregnant women
(iv)   Reduction of neonatal deaths and disabilities associated with birth asphyxia and hypoxia.
(v)    Reduction of ophthalmia neonatorum.
(vi)   Reduction of complications from sexually transmitted diseases in pregnant women
17.   To realize these goals, the activities within the four years of this sub-component are to:
(i)    Strengthen Program Management:
(a)    Develop a national policy;
(b)   Improve maternal and perinatal program management at the MOH and Oblast
Health Administration levels (training, study tour, supply computer).
(ii)    Upgrade Health Provider Skills:
(a)    Develop a training package;
(b)    Strengthen and decentralize training sites;
(c)    Train a core of National and Oblast trainers in adapted WHO "Mother-Baby
Package";
(d)    Train health providers in adapted WHO "Mother-Baby Package" at Republican,
Oblast and Raion levels;
(e)   Introduce adapted WHO "Mother-Baby Package" in pre-service and in-service
training curricula of doctors and paramedics
(iii)   Upgrade Health Facilities:
(a)    Develop a "model" hospital, polyclinic, and FAP in each oblast;
(b)    Provide essential maternal health drugs to selected health facilities nationwide.
(c)    Provide a basic list of clinical equipment for selected health facilities nationwide.
(iv)   Improve Public Awareness:
(a)    Make available culturally appropriate printed materials, for the general public,
on maternal and perinatal health and appropriate health seeking behavior.
18.   At the end of the project there would be:
(i)    Strengthened Program Management:
(a)   Increased management capacity in the Department of Maternal and Child Health
at the Ministry of Health and the Oblast Health Administrations;
(b)    A national policy for a comprehensive safe motherhood and perinatal health
program and a strategy to rationalize and integrate activities.
(ii)    Upgraded Health Provider Skills:
(a)   Increased training and management capacity within the Department of Continuous
Medical Education in Bishkek City and Osh; and a decentralization of training



76                              ANNEX 2.A
to the newly established Oblast Model Hospitals;
(b)    Core teams of trainers at central, Oblast and Raion level;
(c)    Training packages for four levels of health workers;
(d)    2000 Health workers trained in the WHO "Mother-Baby Package:
(e)    Obstetricians at Republican, Oblast and Raion Hospital levels trained to use
partograms and assisted delivery techniques (vacuum extraction and forceps).
(f)    Neonatologists, and midwives trained in newborn resuscitation;
(g)    Primary health care workers trained in maternal and perinatal care.
(iii)   Upgraded Health Facilities:
(a)    Model hospital, polyclinic and FAP in each Oblast with restructured, rationalized
and integrated health services;
(b)    Clinical equipment in 170 SVAS, 20 model health facilities, and 43 Central
Raion Hospitals.
(iv)   Improved Public Awareness:
(a)    Booklets on family health for the general public distributed free of charge from
primary health care facilities.
19.   Five months of international technical assistance will be required over the four and a half years
of the project: 2.5 months in Year 1, 1.5 months in Year 2, and 2 weeks in Year 3 and Year 4 (see
attached terms of reference in Table 3). The technical assistance will be required to conduct training
courses, organize and conduct the study tour, assist in the development of booklets for the general public.
Local technical assistance will be required for computer software training.
20.    The following range of foreign specialist skills will be required to undertake these activities:
(i)    Women's and Children's Health Program management specialist;
(ii)    Obstetrician/gynecologist with experience in teaching the WHO "Mother-Baby Package"
Guidelines;
(iii)   Neonatologist/pediatrician with experience in teaching the WHO "Mother-Baby Package"
Guidelines;
(iv)   Venereologist with experience in teaching the WHO Standard Guidelines on Sexually
Transmitted Diseases.
21.   The activities of the sub-component are described in greater detail below:
(i)    Strengthen Program Management:
22.   Develop a national policy: The various Republican level institutions involved in maternal and
child health will be encouraged to coordinate activities with the MOH and Oblast Health Administrations
through the formation of a national task force: Kyrgyz State Medical Institute, Faculty of Continuous
Medical Education, Republican Paramedic Medical College, the Republican Institute for Obstetrics,
Gynecology and Pediatrics, Republican Maternity Hospital, the Republican Marriage and Family Center,
the Republican Dermatovenereal Dispensary, and the Republican AIDS Center. A national policy and
planning workshop is scheduled to prepare the policies and plans of action to define national objectives,
establish targets and subtargets for the major activities: training, supervision, logistics, commnunication,
monitoring and evaluation. The task force will develop a national policy on safe motherhood and
perinatal health which will include the rationalization of services and integration into the primary health
care level. Special attention will be paid to programs for marginal groups, including adolescents.



77                             ANNEX 2.A
23.   The WHO definitions for health indicators will have to be adopted and medical audit and quality
assurance procedures put into place. A new system of supervision will replace the old style punitive
approach based upon the use of orders and decrees, which have largely ossified in time. Existing
maternal and child health management information systems will be improved through a rationalization
of all reporting (introduce new home-based mothers records, tally sheets, referral letters, report forms,
etc.). The registration of newborns should be improved with direct referral of the newborn card to the
registration office with minimal costs to the parents. Linkages between the different levels of health
facilities needs to be improved. Service provision by the private sector and non-government organizations
must be encouraged for cost saving purposes, and these new services coordinated and integrated into the
general referral system. A basic package of essential services for pregnant women and newborns to be
funded and provided by the government health system will have to be negotiated.
24.   A small baseline health facility survey will aim to establish baseline data in maternal and perinatal
health practices against which improvements in the standard management and clinical can later be
measured. This survey will be undertaken by the MOH and Oblast program managers as part of the
policy and planning workshop. The survey coupled with a focused program review (FPR) will then be
repeated mid-project and at the end of the project. The results of this health facility survey, which is
really an internal phased evaluation mechanism for the program managers, will allow them to "tailor"
the national maternal and perinatal care program, monitoring progress and identifying priority areas which
will require special attention at different points in the project.
25.   Improve maternal and perinatal program management at the MOH and Oblast Health
Administration levels: Special emphasis will be placed on strengthening the MOH Central, Oblast and
Raion management to ensure adequate surveillance, supervision and monitoring of the programs. A range
of workshops and on-the-job training will be conducted for MOH and Oblast Health Administration
program managers and other key people (management training, computer software training, national
policy development workshop, regular program review workshops). The sub-component will provide
equipment for the Chief Obstetrician at the Ministry of Health to strengthen surveillance, training,
supervision and management (computer, printer & software training).
26.   The MOH has been compiling the essential drug list and will be responsible for maintaining and
regularly updating a database on rational drug prescribing and the WHO case management protocols.
The MOH Chief Obstetrician and Chief Pediatrician will be trained to allocate their time more efficiently
by refocusing their work to policy development and monitoring. However, the major work must be
largely decentralized to the Oblast Health Administration.
27.   A study tour will be arranged in the first year of the project for 10 people over a two week
period. Participants will include maternal and child health program managers from the Ministry of Health
and Oblast Health Administrations (Chief Pediatricians and Chief Obstetricians), and national specialists
in obstetrics, neonatology and venereology. The group will travel with one facilitator and one interpreter.
The objective of the study tour is to observe cost-effective and comprehensive National Maternal and
Perinatal Health programs (following WHO guidelines both in program management and clinical case
management with integration of services at the primary health care level) in other countries. The study
tour participants should visit the following:
(i)    Ministry of Health to discuss the National MPH program with MOH program managers
(ii)   Regional Health Departments to discuss the Regional MPH programs with Regional
program managers
(iii)   Hospital maternity, pediatric and venereology departments providing shared care with the
primary health care levels.

(iv)   Primary health care clinics with integrated services
(v)    Community based services
(ii)    Upgrade Health Provider Skills:

31.   Develop a training package: National policies will be developed with a well defined basic health
care package for pregnant women and their infants to be funded and provided by the government health
system. These policies; together with the adapted WHO Mother-Baby Package; will be used as the basis
for the technical guidelines on maternal and perinatal health care and the case management of sexually
transmitted diseases. The following WHO training modules adapted from the "WHO Mother-Baby
Package" to the special health needs of the Kyrgyz Republic will be taught to hospital specialists and
primary health care workers:
(i)    Medical and nursing care of normal and high risk pregnancies.
(ii)    Medical and nursing care of normal and high risk deliveries (including partograms).
(iii)   Medical and nursing care of newborns (including resuscitation).
(iv)   Breastfeeding during the neonatal and perinatal period.
(v)    Principles and practice of family planning.
(vi)   Sexually transmitted diseases case management.
(vii)  Prenatal and postnatal counseling techniques
32.   Other WHO training modules could include:
(i)    Care of the mother and baby at a health center WHO/FHE/MSM/94.2E
(ii)    Detecting pre-eclampsia WHO/MCH/MSM/92.3E
(iii)   Detecting prolonged labor: The Partogram, WHO/FHE/MSM/93.8E
(iv)   Maternal and perinatal infections, WHO/MCH/9 1. 1 OE
(v)    Obstetric and contraceptive surgery at he district hospital, WHO/MCH/MSM/92.8
(vi)   Thermal control of the newborn, WHO/FHE/MSM/93.2E
(vii)  The prevention and management of postpartum hemorrhage, WHO/MCH/90.7.EF
(viii)  Essential elements of obstetric care at first referral level. WHO 1991, ISBN 924
(ix)   Maternal care for the reduction of perinatal and neonatal mortality, WHO/86.E
(x)   Integrating maternal and child health services with primary health care, practical
considerations. WHO/ISBN 92.4
33.   The policy and guidelines will be developed in Russian by a committee of national experts, with
the assistance of a team of international technical advisors during the project preparation phase (funded
by PPF). The guidelines will cover two major areas of the program activities: one - upgraded hospital
practices in prenatal care, delivery care (including partograms to monitor labor; augmented labor; and
assisted delivery techniques such as vacuum extraction, forceps) and newborn resuscitation and
management of sexually transmitted diseases; and two - upgraded primary health care practices in
maternal health, perinatal health and sexually transmitted diseases management. Guidelines with clinical
management and criteria for referral will be developed for four different levels of health facilities:
Level 1.     Specialist tertiary and Oblast Hospitals
Level 2.     Central Raion Hospitals
Level 3.     Polyclinics, SVAs and APTKS.
Level 4.     Feldsher/Accoucheur Posts
34.   The guidelines will be printed early in the project to coincide with the start of training activities.
All health workers trained will be provided with case management charts and other materials adapted
from the original WHO management charts, as work aids for effective case management.
35.   Strengthen and decentralize training sites: An objective of this sub-component is to increase
training capacity within the country through a process of institution strengthening and decentralization
to implement these guidelines in a sustainable way. The quality of practical training will be improved
through a focus on intensive "hands-on" training in a clinical setting with real patients. The training will
take place at the two already established Centers of Continuous Medical Education in Bishkek and Osh



80                             ANNEX 2.A
(both faculties of the Medical Institute)-, and the model health facilities (one multispecialty hospital, onie
multispecialty polyclinic, and one FAP) to be developed during the project in each of the 6 Oblasts.
36.   The Center of Continuous Medical Education (CCME), in Bishkek City, will act as the focus for
all training activities in Phase I of the project and will be responsible for training coordination nationwide.
The CCME has a sub-department in Osh City, and this sub-department will play a training and
coordinating role for the south Kyrgyz Republic. The following set of office and training equipment will
be provided under the Primary Health Care Component for each of these CCMES: one computer, one
printer & software (plus training), one heavy duty photocopier, one fax machine, one television, one
VCR, one slide projector, one overhead projector and screen; I flipchart stand and a set of training
physiological models (women's pelvis to practice assisted delivery; and newborn baby to practice newborn
resuscitation).
37.   Train a core of National and Oblast trainers in adapted WHO "Mother-Baby Package": In
Phase 1. of the training program the National and Oblast Trainers will receive training at the CCME in
Bishkek City and the Republican hospitals. These trainers will be selected from medical and paramedical
colleges and the Republican Postgraduate Institutes. Intensive in-hospital training of these national and
oblast hospital specialists by foreign experts in obstetrics, neonatology and venereology will be required
in the initial phase of the project; followed by a training of trainers course.
38.   Train health providers in adapted WHO "Mother-Baby Package" at Republican, Oblast and
Raion levels: In Phase 2 the Oblast model hospitals will be trained and they will then be responsible for
training Central Raion Hospital staff. Local part-time training coordinators will be funded by the project
to work in each of the 6 Oblast model hospitals. The practical "hands-on" training will consist of
maternal and perinatal care in the hospital inpatient, hospital outpatient, polyclinic, and FAP setting. The
following sets of training equipment will be provided to each Oblast Model Hospital under the PHC
Component (and must be shared by both the Women's Reproductive Health sub-component and the
ARI/CDD subcomponent): Television, VCR, slide projector, overhead projector and screen; I flipchart
stand and a set of physiological training models (women's pelvis to practice assisted delivery; and
newborn baby to practice newborn resuscitation). A limited list of essential maternal health care drugs
and clinical equipment will be provided to the upgraded model hospitals, polyclinics and FAPs for the
ongoing training activities.
39.   In Phase 3. of the training program, Primary Health Care Teams from  the peripheral
Feldshers/Accoucheur Posts (FAPs), Ambulatories (SVAs), Polyclinics and Rural Hospitals will be
trained by Oblast Hospital and Central Raion Hospital staff (this will depend on distances to be travelled
and the arrangements for training will vary in different Oblasts).
40.   Introduce adapted WIHO "Mother-Baby Package" in pre-service and in-service training
curricula of doctors and paramedics: To ensure longterm sustainability of these training activities the
newly developed guidelines will be integrated into the pre-service and in-service training at the medical
and paramedical colleges. Trainers from these schools to be trained as National and Oblast trainers.
(iii)   Upgrade Health Facilities:
41.   Develop a "model" hospital, polylinic, and FAP in each oblast: Maternal, gynecological and
children's departments in Oblast hospitals will be rationalized to form a model hospital complex in each
Oblast (merging of departments and buildings, redeployment of staff, pooling of equipment and
spareparts, etc.). An adult therapy clinic, women's consultation clinic, and children's clinic will be
merged (if not already merged) to form a model polyclinic nearby the model hospital in each Oblast . A



81                             ANNEX 2.A
nearby FAP will also be selected as a model FAP. These 20 facilities (2 CCMEs, 6 Model Hospitals,
6 Model Polyclinics and 6 FAPS) will be upgraded with equipment, essential drugs and staff training and
used as model health facilities for the training activities in each Oblast. The longterm goal is that the
upgrading and rationalization process that takes place in these model sites will be replicated in health
facilities throughout each Oblast.
42.   Provide essential maternal health drugs to selected health facilities: The sub-component will
provide essential drugs in two annual tranches (see Table 5 for proposed list). These drugs will be
earmarked for maternal and perinatal health training activities in 20 model training centers. After specific
training a small pack of essential drugs and supplies for obstetric emergencies will be given to every FAP
and SVA team attending the training course. The remaining essential drugs will be distributed to 12
Raions (28 % of all Raions) for distribution to SVAs and FAPS. The Raions will be selected on the basis
of having the worst maternal, perinatal and infant health indicators in that Oblast.
43.   Provide a basic list of clinical equipment for selected health facilities nationwide: The
following basic clinical equipment will be provided to 170 SVAs nationwide (this will complement the
equipment donated by the German Government's KFW) to strengthen the work of the primary health care
level: 1 Tuning fork, I Reflex hammer, 1 Stethoscope, I Auriscope, 1 Sphygmomanometer, I Needle &
syringe sterilizer, 5 Glass syringes, 1 Thermometer adult, I Thermometer infant, 1 Nurses Instrument
Suture kit, I Glucometer sei, I Examination lamp, I Sterile instrument container set for autoclave, 2 Trash
cans, 1 Flashlight, 1 Peak flow meter, 1 Basic light microscope, I Fetoscope, I Tongue depressor, 10 sets
Urine analysis dipsticks (see Table 7 for specifications). This basic equipment will also be provided for
the model hospitals (2 sets), polyclinics (2 sets) and FAPs (2 sets) in each Oblast to strengthen their role
as model training sites. A more extensive set of equipment will be provided to the 30 converted Rural
Hospitals being refurbished under the Facilities Rehabilitation Component. These 30 sets will include
equipment for infant resuscitation, a fetoscope, a hemoglobinometer and infant beds. All 43 Central
Raion Hospitals will be provided with a basic instruments to handle complicated delivery cases.
(iv)   Improve Public Awareness:
44.   Make available culturally appropriate printed materials, for the general public, on maternal
and perinatal health and appropriate health seeking behavior: This activity strengthens the capacity
of MOH's Republican Health Education Center to inform the general public about women's reproductive
health issues and appropriate utilization of the primary health care services. In an effort to integrate the
different activities of the primary health care component; namely maternal and newborn care, home
management of common childhood diseases such as respiratory and diarrheal diseases, and adult health
care to prevent and control sexually transmitted diseases, tuberculosis, smoking and diet related
conditions; funds will be provided for the development, printing and distribution of one booklet providing
information on all these conditions in the context of family life. This book will be printed in Russian and
Kyrgyz. The Republican Health Center will develop the booklets with technical assistance provided by
a GTZ funded technical advisor who will provide technical assistance in pretesting messages through
techniques such as target group surveys and focus group discussions. The objective is to conduct field
testing during development of the booklet so that it is both visually pleasing to the population and contains
appropriate and understandable information. Topics to be covered will include preparing for pregnancy
(including nutrition), what to expect in pregnancy and delivery; recognition of danger signs, problems
and emergencies; what health services are available for pregnant women, whom to contact for help, how
to contact them and the means available to travel to the health facility in the case of an emergency;
breastfeeding, and caring for an infant. The booklets will be distributed free of charge to the general
public by health facility staff at the primary health care level.



82                             ANNEX 2.A
E. Program Supervision
45.   Regular supervision of the National Maternal and Perinatal Program, by MOH and Oblast
program managers is essential, to ensure implementation of the sub-component's activities, particularly
regarding case management and reporting. The MOH and Oblast Health Administrations must ensure
that their supervisory staff are mobile enough to perform adequate supervision. These travel costs should
come out of the counterpart funding of the project.
F. Project Management
46.   The MOH will have overall responsibility for the PHC component, including the Women's
Reproductive Health sub-component, in close coordination with Oblast and Raion Health Administrations.
The TCC will have one full-time advisor to coordinate all PHC activities. To strengthen MOH
management capacity management training courses will be conducted by the project and a computer,
printer, and software training will be provided to the Chief Obstetrician.
G. Benefits and Risks
47.   The Ministry of Health, has been downsized, and plans are underway to cut staffing levels even
further. This is coupled with a lack of management capacity. The ability of the MOH to supervise the
program effectively is in doubt. However, there is tremendous government commitment to health reform
and safe motherhood programs and some very talented and dedicated people in the government health
system. The project design includes a phased implementation of the PHC Component to avoid excessive
burden on the MOH. Changing case management protocols is notoriously difficult, particularly at the
tertiary health facilities level. Unless case management at these Republican hospitals (the training ground
for future doctors) are improved, it will be difficult to make changes lower down the health system.
Attempts to integrate services will meet with strong opposition from health personnel at all levels of the
health system whose jobs are at stake. There is some resistance in the government to the treatment of
certain social groups with STDs on an anonymous ambulatory basis.
H. Monitoring and Evaluation
48.   Three sets of indicators will be used to monitor the implementation of this sub-component: input
indicators, process/output/service indicators, and outcome indicators. The data will be collected through
current reporting mechanisms and additional health facility surveys (baseline, mid-project, end-of-project).
The TCC Primary Health Care coordinator and MOH can use the following indicators to monitor the
progress of the project. These can be converted into checklists and used as spot checks on field visits.
A more limited list of Performance Monitoring Indicators have been selected for the monitoring of the
project by the World Bank.
49.   Input Indicators will track planned resource inputs such as the following:
(a)    Study tours in the first year of project.
(b)    Person weeks of technical assistance per year.
(c)    Procurement and disbursement of essential drugs.
(d)    Procurement and disbursement of clinical equipment.
(e)    Procurement and disbursement of training equipment (TV, VCR, models, etc.).
(f)    Procurement and disbursement of computers and printers.
50.   Process indicators will track the training activities:



83                              ANNEX 2.A
(a)    Actual number of trainers trained at Republican level compared with planned number
(b)    Actual number of trainers trained at Oblast level compared with planned number
(c)    Actual number of training manuals printed compared with planned number
(d)    Actual number of booklets printed compared with planned number
51.   Output/service indicators will track the impact of the sub-component on health services:
(a)    Percentage of pregnant women receiving prenatal care in the first trimester of pregnancy.
(b)    Percentage of pregnant women receiving iron supplements.
(c)    Percentage of newborns breastfed within half an hour of birth.
(d)    Percentage of pregnant women treated correctly for pre-eclampsia at the FAP, SVA,
rural hospital and central rayon hospital levels.
(e)    Percentage of deliveries that are monitored with a partograin.
(f)    Percentage of deliveries that are assisted with forceps, vacuum extraction or cesarean
section.
(g)    Percentage of newborns that are resuscitated correctly.
(h)    Percentage of syphilis cases treated on an anonymous ambulatory basis.
(i)    Average length of hospital stay for pregnant women.
(j)    Average length of hospital stay for women after delivery.
(k)    Average length of hospital stay for STDS.
(1)    Number of beds for the treatment of STDS.
(m)   Percentage of raion hospitals equipped and functioning as referral centers.
(n)    Percentage of communities with organized transport systems in place to effect referral.
(o)    Percentage of health centers with referral links (eg., communication, transport) to
facilities that can provide emergency obstetrical care.
(p)    Time required for an emergency patient to receive appropriate treatment at referral site.
52.   Outcome indicators will track change in health status of the mothers and infants. The
mortality rates are not expected to change significantly in the four year life of the project.
(a)    Maternal Mortality Rate.
(b)    Perinatal Mortality Rate.
(c)    Infant Mortality Rate.
(d)    Percentage of women of reproductive age with anemia.
(e)    Proportion of maternal deaths due to eclampsia, sepsis, hemorrhage.
(f)    Caesarian section rate.
(g)    Forceps/vacuum extraction rates.
(h)    Percentage of newborns with Apgar scores over 7.

93                             ANNEX 2.B
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 2.B: PRIMARY HEALTH CARE - ACUTE RESPIRATORY INFECTIONS AND
DIARRHEAL DISEASE CONTROL SUBCOMPONENT
A. Background
1. In 1991, the corrected infant mortality rate (IMR) for the Kyrgyz Republic, 40 per 1000 live births,
was slightly lower than the average IMR for upper middle income countries (about 45 per 1,000 live
births). A few countries (such as China and Sri Lanka) with lower income per capita report an IMR better
than that of the Kyrgyz Republic, but most have an IMR even higher. The mortality indicators however,
have shown little improvement over the past ten to fifteen years (the IMR declined only ten percent from
1970 to 1985).
Uncorrected Infant Mortality Rates per 1000 live births (source MOH)
1988        1989         1990        1991         1992        1993         1994
36.6        32.7         29.9        29.6         31.5         31.9        29.4    l
2.    In the Kyrgyz Republic, as shown in the table below, acute respiratory infections, perinatal
conditions and diarrheal diseases are responsible for over 90% of infant deaths. As can be seen in the
"Prioritization Framework" table, acute respiratory infections and diarrheal diseases account for most of
the potential Disability Adjusted Life Year (DALY) loss per year. An acute respiratory infections
program and diarrheal diseases control program (ARI/CDD program) are the most cost-effective health
interventions to reduce the IMR in the Kyrgyz Republic. However, international experience has shown
that a reduction in IMR would not be expected until 5-6 years after the introduction of the program.
Respiratory infections accounted for over half of infant deaths in Naryn, Osh, Djalal Abad and Talas
Oblasts. The proportion of diarrhea deaths are particularly high in Osh and Djalal Abad Oblasts in the
south of the country. Most acute respiratory disease deaths occur in the long winter months (90% of the
Kyrgyz Republic is covered in high mountains) and most diarrhea related deaths in the summer months,
particularly in the warmer southern regions. A child who is malnourished and has an acute respiratory
infection or diarrhea is more likely to die than a child who is not malnourished and has an acute
respiratory infection or diarrhea. Malnutrition is on the increase in the Kyrgyz Republic. Over 7% of
all pre-school children are acutely malnourished, and this rises to 15% in some regions (Kyrgyz Multi-
Purpose Survey 1994).
Percentage of Infant Deaths due to Acute Respiratory Infections, Perinatal Causes and
Diarrhea by Oblast in 1993
Kyr    Bish    Issy    Naryn    Osh        Djal    Talas    Chui
ARI              45       8      48       52        50      53        52       37
Perinatal        26      76       15       27       18      16       21       29
Diarrhea         14       4       15       3        19      15        16       12



94                               ANNEX 2.B
3.     Most of the infant deaths due to infectious diseases such as acute respiratory infections and
diarrhea are concentrated in the rural areas of the Kyrgyz Republic, where the rates of mortality due to
these causes is two to three times higher than that of urban areas. The rural areas are more mountainous
and health facilities more limited. The infant mortality in rural areas increases sharply during the winter
months, probably reflecting variations in mortality rates due to acute respiratory infections, as seasonality
is negligible in urban areas. Children reach the health facility too late: leading to a high proportion of
deaths within the first 24 hours of admission (32% of admissions in 1993). At the same time
hospitalization rates for children with ARIs is very high and the majority of children with ARI do not
need to be admitted. The high rates of acute respiratory infections is probably due to a combination of
three factors: intra-household combustion of wood, the ignorance of parents of the early signs and
symptoms of severe acute respiratory infection, and inadequate clinical management of the disease. These
ARI deaths are concentrated particularly in under 5 year olds.
4.     Although the rate of mortality due to diarrheal diseases is only one fourth of the rate due to acute
respiratory diseases, the majority of the deaths are probably preventable. The average case fatality rate
of 1.2% and hospital case fatality rate of 3% are very high, but could be due to the high under reporting
of diarrhea cases. Two main strategies are needed: promotion of exclusive breastfeeding for the first four
months of life and prolonged to one year, and better home and health facility case management of
diarrheal episodes following WHO guidelines, including rehydration therapy with home fluids and ORS
packets and antibiotics for dysenteric cases. Oral rehydration therapy has been promoted in the Kyrgyz
Republic since 1985. ORT corners were established at primary health care facilities. The Ministry of
Health in collaboration with UNICEF initiated a control of diarrheal disease program in 1992, and
UNICEF is supplying 500,000 ORS packets annually. Despite these efforts ORS is not on the newly
developed national list of essential drugs. Outmoded treatments such as bacterium bifidum and
"biostimulators" are still recommended for diarrhea treatment in MOH guidelines and the major teaching
hospitals. USAID (through Wellstart) and UNICEF have funded breastfeeding promotion activities and
UNICEF plan to launch a Baby Friendly Hospital Initiative.
Acute Respiratory Infections in the Kyrgyz Republic, 199 1-1993
1991              1992               1993
Number ARI            425,910            422,213            400,609
Cases up to 14
yrs
ARI per 1000            254.5             252.3              238.4
5.    It is the practice, in all the former Soviet countries, to have pediatricians treating ARIs; and
infectious diseases specialists treating diarrhea in separate infectious disease wards and hospitals. Acute
respiratory infections and diarrheal diseases are also the most common childhood diseases seen in clinics
and admitted to hospitals. In 1991, 47% of all children under one year suffered from a respiratory
infection. Nearly half of all hospital admissions in children are for respiratory diseases. In 1991, 70%
of all under one year olds seen in the outpatient department with a respiratory infection were admitted
to hospital. In the same year, 40% of all under fifteen year olds were hospitalized. These very high
hospitalization rates for what is usually a mild viral infection, are quite inappropriate. This is confirmed
by observations in the field. Hospital beds are indeed occupied by children with mild respiratory
infections who could be equally well managed at home. At the same time there are very sick children in



95                             ANNEX 2.B
the community, who are arriving at the hospital too late. What is required is a comprehensive mass media
campaign for the general public, combined with major retraining of health workers. In addition, reduced
capacity in the government drug procurement and distribution agency, Pharmacia, has resulted in a
shortage of ARI/CDD drugs throughout the government health system.
6.    Acute respiratory infections and diarrheal disease in under five year olds have already been
identified by the government and the donor community as priority health problems. In the government's
"Health For All 1994-2000" program, breastfeeding, ARI and diarrheal disease control have been
included as the priority interventions. In collaboration with the Ministry of Health (MOH), a UNICEF
funded ARI/CDD program started in 1993. In 1994, the MOH developed a National ARI/CDD Program
and appointed a National ARI/CDD programn manager, Dr. Apisa Kushbakeeva, the Chief Pediatrician
at the MOH. A National ARI/CDD Policy has not been finalized or endorsed. A report of a World
Bank/WHO consultant, Dr. E. Kudlova, "Evaluation of the Status of ARI/CDD Program in Kyrgyzstan-
July 1995" revealed that even in the Republican Institute hospitals, children with ARIs and diarrhea are
admitted unnecessarily and treated with inappropriate case management protocols. Her report identified
the following shortcomings in the MOH/UNICEF ARI/CDD programs:
(i)    Lack of national CDD/ARI policy and case management guidelines;
(ii)   Inadequate supervision, management, surveillance of ARI/CDD activities at all levels;
(iii)   Lack of WHO ARI/CDD Russian materials in the Kyrgyz Republic, despite being
available.
(iv)   Lack of ARI/CDD case management training including clinical "hands-on" practice;
(v)   Inappropriate use of drugs for the treatment of diarrhea and ARI, inappropriate
rehydration of dehydrated cases, unnecessary hospitalization of CDD/ARI cases at
tertiary, secondary and primary health care levels; and
(vi)   Some incorrect mass media health messages on the home management of ARI/CDD.
B. Role of Other Donors
7.     UNICEF, in collaboration with the MOH, began an ARI/CDD control program in 1992, using
the case management guidelines developed by WHO. UNICEF funded some training activities, provided
a television and VCR (which were lost), and supplied and transported essential drugs to primary health
care facilities bypassing the Government system. This case management training has not reached health
workers at all levels of the health system. The Republican Health Education Center has limited capacity
to mobilize the general public in appropriate health seeking behavior for ARI/CDD and UNICEF has
been forced to use the private sector to get ARI/CDD television messages out to the general public. The
full implementation of this program has been patchy due to limited resources and the diminished capacity
in several MOH departments. UNICEF will continue to take the lead in the training of health workers
at the primary health level and the World Bank subcomponent will focus on the development of model
training centers and a core of trainers. UNICEF will continue to supply ARI and CDD drugs through a
series of winter and summer programs, targeted at under 5 year olds. The World Bank/WHO consultant
calculated that the UNICEF contribution of 500,000 ORS packets cover about 70% of the estimated need.
The World Bank supply of ARI/CDD drugs will cover this shortfall in national drug requirements. The
UNICEF and World Bank drug supplies to the primary health care level will need to be closely
coordinated.
8.    GTZ, the German Government's technical assistance program, plan to fund short term technical
assistance to the Republican Health Education Center over a 10 year period, starting in 1996. The
German Government's KFW, is supplying over US$2 million worth of equipment to all Feldsher
Accoucheur Posts (FAPs), Rural Physician Ambulatories (SVAs) and Central Rayon Hospitals and the
shipment is expected to arrive in early 1996. The equipment provided under the PHC Component will



96                             ANNEX 2.B
complement this equipment. WHO has an Information Center and full-time liaison officer based in
Bishkek city. The Information Center has a library with a wide selection of WHO books including some
on ARI/CDD in Russian and English and can be contracted to translate materials into Russian.
C. Rationale for Bank Involvement
9.     In the short term, the current economic crisis will slow down or reverse improvements in living
conditions, but a significant reduction in the burden of disease can still be achieved at low cost through
improving the coverage and quality of some health interventions. This should be possible in view of the
high level of education of the population, and the vast health infrastructure. About sixty percent of the
Kyrgyz Republic's infant mortality is caused by acute respiratory infections and diarrhea. ARI and CDD
programs using WHO guidelines are two of the most cost-effective programs; as shown in the 1993
World Development Report: Investing in Health.
10.   The World Bank activities will complement the more comprehensive nation-wide primary health
care training program planned by UNICEF for 1996-2001, by focusing on the hospital level. An
evaluation of the ARI/CDD program in 1995, by a WHO consultant, showed that although some progress
had been made in training at the peripheral level, case management is patchy, and a comprehensive
retraining program throughout all levels of the health system, from the major teaching institutes
downwards, is required. The Bank project will focus on improving the quality of "hands-on" case
management training and institutionalizing this practical training within the Department of Continuous
Medical Education and a network of Oblast model health facilities (one hospital, one polyclinic and one
FAP per Oblast) to be developed under the PHC component. The Bank project will also strengthen the
capacities of government institutions to provide essential ARI/CDD drugs and promote health education.
D. ARI/CDD Subcomponent Description
I 1. - The long term goals of the Acute Respiratory Infections and Control of Diarrheal Disease
subcomponent are to reduce deaths in children from acute respiratory tract infections and diarrhea.
12.   To realize these goals, the activities within the four years of this project are to:
(i)    Strengthen Program Management:
(a)   Improve ARI/CDD program management at the Ministry of Health and Oblast
Health Administration levels (training, study tour, supply computer);
(b)    Develop a national strategy for the rationalization and integration of ARI/CDD
services.
(ii)    Upgrade Health Provider Skills:
(a)    Develop a training package;
(b)    Strengthen and decentralize training sites;
(c)    Train a core of National and Oblast trainers
(d)    Train hospital health providers at national, Oblast and Raion levels;
(e)   Introduce adapted WHO ARI/CDD case management guidelines in pre-service
and in-service training curricula of doctors and paramnedics.
(iii)   Upgrade Health Facilities:
(a)    Develop a "model" hospital, polyclinic, and FAP providing WHO recommended
ARI/CDD case management in each Oblast;
(b)    Provide essential ARI/CDD drugs to selected health facilities nationwide



97                              ANNEX 2.B
(coordinating with UNICEF's drug supply to the PHC level).
(c)    Provide a basic list of clinical equipment for selected health facilities nationwide.
(iv)   Improve Public Awareness:
(a)    Make available culturally appropriate printed materials, for the general public,
on the home management of ARI and diarrheal diseases and appropriate health
seeking behavior.
13.   At the end of the project there will be a:
(i)    Strengthened Program Management:
(a)    Trained child health program managers at the Ministry of Health and Oblast
Health Administrations;
(b)    A national strategy, as part of the National Policy for ARI/CDD Control
activities, to rationalize and integrate activities.
(ii)    Upgraded Health Provider Skills:
(a)    Increased training capacity and management capacity within the Centers of
Continuous Medical Education in Bishkek and Osh; and a decentralization of
training to the newly established Oblast Model Hospitals;
(b)    Core teams of trainers at national, Oblast and Raion level;
(c)    500 health providers trained in the adapted WHO ARI/CDD case management
guidelines;
(d)    Pediatricians and infectious disease specialists at national, Oblast and Raion levels
(UNICEF will train all primary health care level staff).
(iii)   Upgraded Health Facilities:
(a)    Model hospital, polyclinic and FAP in each Oblast with restructured, rationalized
and integrated pediatric and infectious diseases services.
(b)    Essential drugs for ARI/CDD training activities provided to 20 model training
centers. After specific training a small pack of essential drugs and supplies for
ARI/CDD will be given to every FAP and SVA team attending the training
course.
(c)    Essential drugs provided in two annual tranches for ARI/CDD to 12 Raions (28%
of all Raions) for distribution to SVAs and FAPs. The Raions will be selected on
the basis of having the worst maternal, perinatal and infant health indicators, in
close coordination with UNICEF.
(iv)   Improved Public Awareness:
(a)    Booklets on family health for the general public distributed free of charge from
primary health care facilities.
14.   Technical Assistance: Four months of international technical assistance will be required over
the four years of the project: 2 months in Year 1, one month in Year 2, and 2 weeks in Year 3 and Year
4. The technical assistance will be required to conduct training courses and a study tour (see attached
terms of reference in Table 2). A doctor with considerable experience in pediatrics, and the management
of diarrheal cases and acute respiratory infections is preferred. The consultant(s) should be familiar with
WHO training materials on ARI/CDD, and should have experience in conducting case management and
program management training courses. Local technical assistance will be required for computer software
training.



98                             ANNEX 2.B
15.   The activities of the subcomponent are described in greater detail below:
(i)    Strengthen Program Management:
16.   Improve ARI/CDD program management at the Ministry of Health and Oblast Health
Administration levels: Special emphasis will be placed on strengthening the MOH national, Oblast and
Raion management to ensure adequate surveillance, supervision and monitoring of the ARI/CDD
programs. A range of workshops and on-the-job training will be conducted for MOH and Oblast Health
Administration program managers and other key people (management training, computer software
training, national policy development workshop, regular program review workshops). The subcomponent
will provide equipment for the Chief Pediatrician at the Ministry of Health (computer, printer & software
training). The Chief Pediatrician will be trained to allocate her time more efficiently by refocusing their
work to policy development and monitoring. The major work must be largely decentralized to the Oblast
Health Administration.
17.   An Acute Respiratory Diseases and Control of Diarrheal Diseases Study Tour will be arranged
in the second year of the project for 10 people over a two week period. Participants will include child
health program managers from the Ministry of Health and Oblast Health Administrations (Chief
Pediatricians), and national clinical specialists in pediatrics and infectious diseases. The group will travel
with one facilitator and one interpreter. The objective of the study tour is to observe effective National
ARI/CDD programs (following WHO guidelines both in program management and clinical case
management with integration of services at the primary health care level) in other countries. The study
tour participants should visit the following:
(i)    Ministry of Health to discuss the National ARI/CDD program with MOH program
managers (including mass media campaigns, surveillance, ORS supply and distribution,
etc.);
(ii)   Regional Health Departments to discuss the Regional ARI/CDD programs with Regional
program managers
(iii)   Oral Rehydration Units in a primary health care level facility
(iv)   ARI/Diarrhea Training Units to observe the "hands on" case management training of
workshop participants and to gain "hands on" experience in treating children with
diarrhea and dehydration. The participants should spend a couple of days gaining
experience in the administration of ORS to a dehydrated child. There should be an
adequate number of dehydrated cases to allow this.
(v)    Hospital pediatric wards to demonstrate that ARI and diarrhea cases can be treated in the
same ward;
(vi)   Primary health care clinics with integrated services;
Community based services.
18.   A Terms of Reference for this study tour is attached in Table 3. Possible countries could include
Egypt, Bangladesh (International Center for Diarrheal Disease Control in Dacca); and Pakistan (USAID
funded Diarrhea Training Units).
19.   Develop a national strategy for the rationalization and integration of ARI/CDD services: The
various Republican level institutions involved in child health will be encouraged to coordinate activities
with the MOH and Oblast Health Administrations through the formation of a national task force: Kyrgyz
State Medical Institute, Faculty of Continuous Medical Education, Republican Paramedic Medical
College, the Republican Institute for Obstetrics, Gynecology and Pediatrics, and the Republican Institute
for Infectious Diseases. A national strategy and planning workshop is scheduled to prepare the policies



99                              ANNEX 2.B
and plans of action to define national objectives, establish targets and subtargets for the major activities:
training, supervision, logistics, communication, monitoring and evaluation. The task force will develop
a strategy on the rationalization of services and integration into the primary health care level, to be
incorporated into the National ARI/CDD Program.
20.   The WHO definitions for health indicators will have to be adopted and medical audit and quality
assurance procedures put into place. A new system of supervision will replace the old style punitive
approach based upon the use of orders and decrees, which have largely ossified in time. Existing child
health management information systems will be improved through a rationalization of reporting (introduce
new home-based mothers records, tally sheets, referral letters, report forms, etc.). At present health
providers and managers spend a large proportion of their work day on data collection and report writing,
detracting from their other duties. The registration of newborns should be improved with direct referral
of the newborn card to the registration office, with minimal costs to the parents. Linkages between the
different levels of health facilities needs to be improved through rationalization of referral criteria. Service
provision by the private sector and non-government organizations must be encouraged for cost saving
purposes, and these new services coordinated and integrated into the general referral system. The MOH
has been compiling the essential drug list and will be responsible for maintaining and regularly updating
a database on rational drug prescribing and the WHO case management protocols. Regular updates should
be circulated to health providers.
21.   A small baseline health facility survey will aim to establish baseline data against which
improvements in the standard ARI/CDD case management can later be measured. This survey will be
undertaken by the MOH and Oblast program managers as part of their case management training
workshop. The survey and a focused program review (FPR) will then be repeated mid-project and at the
end of the project. The results of this health facility survey, which is really an internal evaluation
mechanism for program managers, will allow them to "tailor" the national CDD and ARI program,
monitoring progress and identifying priority areas which will require special attention at different points
in the project. UNICEF will organize the equally important household surveys on ARI/CDD home
management, the first one having been conducted in November 1995.
(ii)    Upgrade Health Provider Skills:
22.   The health worker training will be implemented in three phases as shown in the table below:
Phase 1. Training of National and Oblast Trainers
Phase 2. Training of Oblast Hospital and Central Raion Hospital staff and trainers
Phase 3. Training of Primary Health Care Teams (UNICEF program).
The project will fund Phases 1-2 and UNICEF will fund Phase 3.
23.   This will require the following workshops:
(i)    Practical intensive "hands-on" training of pediatricians and infectious disease specialists;
(ii)    Training of trainers case management training for National trainers;
(iii)   Training of trainers case management training for Oblast trainers;
(iv)   Case management training for staff from 6 Oblast Model Hospitals (one workshop per
Oblast);
(v)    Case management training for staff from 43 Central Raion Hospitals;
24.   A more detailed breakdown of the training is shown in the training timetable. A core National
and Oblast training team of 14 pediatricians, 14 infectious disease specialists, and 14 nurses will be
trained in Phase 1. They will then train Oblast model health facility (15 per Oblast) and Central Raion
hospital staff (6 per Central Raion Hospital). A total of 420 health providers will be trained in Phases 2.
