25.   Develop a training package: A well defined set of national policies will be developed and these
will be used as the basis for the technical guidelines on case management of diarrheal diseases and acute
respiratory infections. The guidelines will be developed by a team of national experts, with the assistance
of an international technical advisor. The guidelines will cover two major areas of the program activities:
first - management of the patient at home and in the outpatient departments, and, second - management
of complicated cases in hospital. Different types of guidelines will be developed for different types of
health workers and different types of program activities: rational use of drugs, community involvement,
prevention, etc.. These guidelines will involve reduction in the inappropriate use of antibiotics and other
drugs; in order to decrease the cost of treatment, the need for hospitalization, and the harmful
consequences of inappropriate drug treatment.
26.   The focus of the case management training is on the recognition of clinical signs in the child and
the appropriate use of antibiotics for a few specified disease categories. Participants will learn how to give
oral rehydration salts to a child with different grades of dehydration. The current case management of
respiratory diseases and diarrheal diseases in the major hospitals will need considerable upgrading before
they can be used as training units. Many children with respiratory infections and diarrheal diseases
receive multiple antibiotic therapy and unnecessary hospitalization. Children with diarrhea and no signs
of dehydration receive intravenous fluids often under sedation. The outmoded hospital case management
practices have considerably impeded the impact of the UNICEF funded training at the PHC level. Unless



101                            ANNEX 2.B
the tertiary and secondary levels adopt the new treatment protocols it is very difficult for the primary
health care level to adopt these protocols. This subcomponent will focus on training at these tertiary and
secondary levels to enhance the work of UNICEF at the primary health care level. Intensive on-site
training of model health facility staff by ARI and CDD clinical experts will be required in the initial
phase of the project to bring them up to the standards of WHO recognized ARI and CDD training units.
These training courses will use WHO case management guidelines which are already available in Russian.
ARI training should take place in the winter season and CDD in the summer season. It may be possible
to do combined training in September-October when the seasons merge and both types of clinical cases
will be present in the hospitals.
27.   The following WHO training modules will be taught to hospital level pediatricians, infectious
disease specialists and pediatric nurses (and Model Polyclinic and FAP staff) under this subcomponent:
(i)    Outpatient management of young children with ARI
(ii)    Hospital management of young children with ARI
(iii)   Outpatient management of young children with diarrhea
(iv)   Hospital management of young children with diarrhea
28.   Facilitator's Manuals and Participant's Manuals for the training modules above are already
available in Russian and will be printed in this subcomponent and distributed to the trainers and
participants. The guidelines will be printed early in the project to coincide with the start of training
activities. All health workers trained will be provided with case management charts and other materials
adapted from the original WHO management charts, as work aids for effective case management. Other
WHO training modules for CDD will include:
(i)    Management of bloody diarrhea in children
(ii)    Management and prevention of acute diarrhea
(iii)   Rational use of drugs in diarrhea in children
(iv)   Guidelines for treatment of cholera
(v)    Dietary management of a child with diarrhea
(vi)   Readings on diarrhea - Student manual
Other WHO training modules for ARI will include:
(i)    Antibiotics in the treatment of ARIs in small children.
(ii)    Oxygen therapy for ARI in young children.
(iii)   Management of fever in young children with ARI.
(iv)   Bronchodilators and other medications for wheeze associated illness in young children.
The WHO Information Center, in Bishkek, has a library with many of these materials and can be
requested to order updated WHO guidelines as they become available over the course of the project.
29.   Strengthen and decentralize training sites: An objective of this subcomponent is to increase
training capacity within the country through a process of institution strengthening and decentralization
in order to implement the ARI/CDD guidelines in a sustainable way. The quality of practical training will
be improved through a focus on intensive "hands-on" training in a clinical setting with real patients. The
training will take place at the two already established Centers of Continuous Medical Education in
Bishkek and Osh (both faculties of the Medical Institute); and the model health facilities (one
multispecialty hospital, one multispecialty polyclinic, and one FAP) to be developed during the project
in each of the 6 Oblasts.
30.   The Center of Continuous Medical Education (CCME), in Bishkek City, will act as the focus for
all training activities in Phase 1 of the project and will be responsible for training coordination
nationwide. The CCME has a sub-department in Osh City, and this sub-department will play a training



102                            ANNEX 2.B
and coordinating role for the southern Kyrgyz Republic. The following set of office and training
equipment will be provided under the Primary Health Care Component for each of these CCMEs: one
computer, one printer & software (plus training), one heavy duty photocopier, one fax machine, one
television, one VCR, one slide projector, one overhead projector and screen; 1 flipchart stand).
31.   Train a core of National and Oblast trainers: In Phase 1 of the training program, the National
and Oblast Trainers will receive training at the CCME in Bishkek City and the National hospitals. These
trainers will be selected from medical and paramedical colleges and National Postgraduate Institutes.
Intensive in-hospital training of these national and oblast hospital pediatric and infectious disease
specialists by foreign experts will be required in the initial phase of the project; followed by a training
of trainers course. Some trainers will also participate in the study tour to practice "hands-on" case
management in other countries.
32.   Train health providers at National, Oblast and Raion levels: In Phase 2, the Oblast model
hospitals will be trained and they will then be responsible for training Central Raion Hospital staff. Local
part-time training coordinators will be funded by the project to work in each of the 6 Oblast model
hospitals. The practical "hands-on" training will take place in the hospital inpatient, hospital outpatient,
polyclinic, and FAP setting. The following sets of training equipment will be provided to each Oblast
Model Hospital under the PHC Component (and must be shared by both the Maternal and Perinatal
Health subcomponent and the ARI/CDD subcomponent): Television, VCR, slide projector, overhead
projector and screen; 1 flipchart stand. A limited list of essential ARI/CDD drugs and clinical equipment
will be provided to the upgraded model hospitals, polyclinics and FAPs for the ongoing training activities.
33.   In Phase 3. of the training program, Primary Health Care Teams from the peripheral FAPs,
SVAs, and Rural Hospitals will be trained by the Ministry of Health and UNICEF. They can draw upon
the core of Oblast trainers trained under this subcomponent.
34.   Introduce adapted WHO ARI/CDD case management package in pre-service and in-service
training curricula of doctors and paramedics: To ensure long term sustainability of these training
activities the newly developed guidelines will be integrated into the pre-service and in-service training
at the medical and paramedical colleges. Trainers from these colleges will be trained as National and
Oblast trainers.
(iii)   Upgrade Health Facilities:
35.   Develop a "model' hospital, polyclinic, and FAP providing WHO recommended ARI/CDD
case management in each Oblast: Pediatric infectious diseases departments (where children with diarrhea
are treated) and pediatric departments (where children with ARIs are treated) in Oblast hospitals will be
rationalized to form a model hospital complex in each Oblast (merging of departments and buildings,
redeployment of staff, pooling of equipment and spare parts, etc.). An adult therapy clinic, women's
consultation clinic, and children's clinic will be merged (if not already merged) to form a model
polyclinic nearby the model hospital in each Oblast. A nearby FAP will also be selected as a model
FAP. These 20 facilities (2 CCMEs, 6 Model Hospitals, 6 Model Polyclinics and 6 FAPs) will be
upgraded with equipment, essential drugs and staff training and used as model health facilities for the
ARI/CDD training activities in each Oblast. The long term goal is that the upgrading and rationalization
process that takes place in these model sites will be replicated in health facilities throughout each Oblast.
36.   Provide essential ARI/CDD drugs to selected health facilities (coordinating with UNICEF's
drug supply to the PHC level): The subcomponent will provide essential drugs in two annual tranches.
See suggested list in table . These drugs will be earmarked for ARI/CDD case management training



103                            ANNEX 2.B
activities in 6 Oblasts (6 Model Hospitals, 6 Model Polyclinics and 6 FAPs). After specific training a
small pack of essential drugs and supplies for ARI/CDD will be given to every FAP and SVA team
attending the training course. The remaining essential drugs will be distributed to 12 Raions (28% of all
Raions) for distribution to SVAs and FAPs. The Raions will be selected on the basis of having the worst
maternal, perinatal and infant health indicators. UNICEF will also continue to donate and directly
distribute drugs for the summer diarrhea season and the winter ARI season. This UNICEF donation
covers about 60-70% of the total drug requirements. The project will provide the remaining 30-40% of
drugs.
37.   Provide a basic list of clinical equipment for selected health facilities nationwide: Under the
Primary Health Care Component, the following basic clinical equipment will be provided to 170 SVAs
nationwide (this will complement the equipment donated by the German Government's KFW) to
strengthen the work of the primary health care level: 1 Tuning fork, 1 Reflex hammer, 1 Stethoscope,
1 Auriscope, 1 Sphygmomanometer, 1 Needle & syringe sterilizer, 5 Glass syringes, 1 Thermometer
adult, 1 Thermometer infant, 1 Nurses Instrument Suture kit, 1 Glucometer set, 1 Examination lamp, 1
Sterile instrument container set for autoclave, 2 Trash cans, 1 Flashlight, 1 Peak flow meter, 1 Basic light
microscope, 1 Fetoscope, 1 Tongue depressor, 10 sets Urine analysis dipsticks (for specifications see
Table 5). The remaining 43 SVAs in Chui Oblast will be provided with equipment under the Medical
Provider Payment Component. This basic equipment will also be provided for the model hospitals (2
sets), polyclinics (2 sets) and FAPs (1 set) in each Oblast to strengthen their role as model training sites.
(iv)   Improve Public Awareness:
38.   Make available culturally appropriate printed materials, for the general public, on the home
management of ARI and diarrheal diseases and appropriate health seeking behavior: Dr. Eva
Kudlova's report highlighted some of the problems with the information available to the general public
on the home management of ARIs and diarrhea. Although many people know about ORS, they do not
seem to know how to give it correctly. Parents do not know when to seek medical help for the sick
children. In an attempt to correct this lack of public awareness funding will be provided for the
development and printing of a booklet. In an effort to integrate the different activities of the primary
health care component; namely maternal and newborn care, home management of common childhood
diseases such as respiratory and diarrheal diseases, and adult health care to prevent and control sexually
transmitted diseases, tuberculosis, smoking and diet related conditions; funds will be provided for the
development, printing and distribution of one booklet providing information on all these conditions in the
context of family life. This book will be printed in Russian and Kyrgyz. This activity strengthens the
capacity of the Republican Health Education Center to mobilize the general public in home-based
management of ARI and diarrhea and appropriate utilization of the primary health care services. The
booklets will be distributed free of charge through the primary health care system.
39.   The Republican Health Center will develop the booklets in collaboration with GTZ funded
technical advisor who will provide technical assistance in pretesting messages through techniques such
as target group surveys and focus group discussions. The objective is to conduct field testing during
development of the booklet so that it is both visually pleasing to the population and contains appropriate
and understandable information. Topics to be covered will include the home management of ARI and
diarrhea, the recognition of danger signs in children; what health services are available for children,
whom to contact for help, how to contact them and the means available to travel to the health facility in
the case of an emergency; breastfeeding, and caring for an infant. This booklet will also focus on
reducing ARI risk factors such as reducing adult smoking and improving ventilation in the house,
especially with cooking fires. Information on the home management of diarrhea and ARI will be available
after the UNICEF sponsored Household Survey due to be conducted in November 1995. Information



104                            ANNEX 2.B
obtained from this survey on home practices should be incorporated into the booklet.
E. Program Supervision
40.   Regular supervision of the National ARI/CDD program, by MOH and Oblast program managers
is essential, to ensure implementation of the subcomponent's activities, particularly regarding case
management and reporting. The MOH and Oblast Health Administrations must ensure that their
supervisory staff are mobile enough to perform adequate supervision. These travel costs should come out
of the counterpart funding of the project.
F. Project Management
41.   The MOH would have overall responsibility for the PHC component, including the ARI/CDD
subcomponent, in close coordination with Oblast and Raion Health Administrations. The TCC will have
one full-time advisor to coordinate all PHC activities. To strengthen MOH management capacity a
computer, printer and software training will be provided to the Chief Pediatrician.
G. Benefits and Risks
42.   It has been agreed between the World Bank mission and UNICEF, that UNICEF has the larger
budget for ARI/CDD and should be the lead agency. This subcomponent has been carefully designed with
the UNICEF Regional Mission for Central Asia, to complement UNICEF planned activities. There is
always a risk that these plans may change. In 1995, a World Bank/WHO consultant's review of the
UNICEF funded ARI/CDD program has revealed a rather patchy training strategy to date. Although
UNICEF now plans a more comprehensive approach, there is still a risk that their training activities will
not be institutionalized, and therefore not sustainable. One to avoid this is for UNICEF to use the
National and Oblast core trainers, the CCMEs, and model health facilities in their training program. The
benefit to UNICEF taking the lead, is that the MOH has been used to working with UNICEF on
ARI/CDD, and this collaboration will now continue.
43.   The Ministry of Health, has been downsized, and plans are underway to cut staffing levels even
further. This is coupled with a lack of management capacity. The ability of the MOH to supervise the
program effectively is in doubt. However, there is tremendous government commitment to health reform
and ARI/CDD programs and some very talented and dedicated people in the government health system.
The project design includes a phased implementation of the PHC Component to avoid excessive burden
on the MOH. Changing case management protocols is notoriously difficult, particularly at the tertiary
health facilities level. Unless case management at these Republican hospitals (the training ground for
future doctors) are improved, it will be difficult to make changes lower down the health system. Attempts
to integrate services will meet with strong opposition from health personnel at all levels of the health
system whose jobs are at stake.
H. Monitoring and Evaluation
44.   Three sets of indicators will be used to monitor the implementation of this subcomponent: input
indicators, process/output/service indicators, and outcome indicators. The data will be collected through
current reporting mechanisms and additional household (UNICEF survey) and health facility surveys. The
Technical Coordination Committee's Primary Health Care Coordinator and MOH can use the following
indicators to monitor the progress of the project. These can be converted into checklists and used on field
visits as spot checks. A more limited list of Performance Monitoring Indicators have been selected for



105                             ANNEX 2.B
the monitoring of the project by the World Bank.
45.   Input indicators will track planned resource inputs such as the following:
(a)    Person weeks of technical assistance per year
(b)    Procurement and disbursement of essential drugs to primary health care facilities.
46.   Process indicators will track the training activities:
(a)    Actual number of booklets printed compared with planned number
(b)    Number of workshops conducted
47.   Output indicators, focused on primary health providers who have received training and
the general public, will assess the unpact of the subcomponent on health services:
Acute Respiratory Infections:
(a)    Proportion of staff working with ARI cases who have been trained in WHO Case
Management
(b)    Proportion of health facilities receiving a regular supply of essential ARI drugs.
(c)    Proportion of ARI cases seen at the health facilities that have been correctly assessed.
(d)    Proportion of pneumonia cases seen at health facilities who will receive standard case
management of pneumonia.
(e)    Proportion of ARI patients seen at health facilities who should not receive antibiotics but
are given them.
(f)    Proportion of ARI cases without pneumonia who are hospitalized.
(g)    Proportion of caretakers of ARI patients who will receive advice on home treatment.
Diarrheal Diseases:
(a)    Proportion of staff working with diarrheal cases who have been trained in WHO Case
Management
(b)    Proportion of Model health facilities receiving a regular supply of essential drugs for
diarrheal diseases
(c)    Proportion of diarrhea cases seen at health facilities that will be correctly assessed.
(d)    Proportion of dysentery cases seen at health facilities who will receive standard case
management of dysentery.
(e)    Proportion of diarrhea patients seen at health facilities who should not receive antibiotics
but are given them.
(f)    Proportion of diarrhea cases without dehydration or other complications who are
hospitalized.
(g)    Proportion of caretakers of diarrhea patients who will receive advice on home treatment.
48.   Outcome indicators will track change in health status of the children. These are not expected
to change significantly in the four year life of the project. Changes occur only 5 to 6 years after the
implementation of a fully comprehensive ARI/CDD program.
(a)   Infant Mortality Rate
(b)    Under 2 and Under 5 Mortality Rate
(c)    Proportion of infant deaths due to acute respiratory tract diseases
(d)    Proportion of infant deaths due to diarrheal diseases

Table 2               TERMS OF REFERENCE
Contractor: Competitive Bidding
Scope: National Acute Respiratory Infection and Diarrheal Disease Control (ARI/CDD) Program for the
Kyrgyz Republic (see attached Implementation Schedule, Training Schedule, and Terms of Reference for
study tour)
Activity: Technical Assistaice
Supervisor: Government of the Kyrgyz Republic & Technical Coordination Committee of the
Government of the Kyrgyz Republic/World Bank Health Sector Reform Project
Timing: From the middle of 1996-2000, four months of international technical assistance will be required
over the four years of the project: 2 months in Year 1, 1 month in Year 2, and 2 weeks in Year 3 and
Year 4.
Objective: To provide technical assistance to the Ministry of Health in the implementation of the Acute
Respiratory Infection and Diarrheal Disease Control Program subcomponent of the Government of the
Kyrgyz Republic/World Bank Health Sector Reforn Project.
Specific Requirements: The consultant(s) should preferably be Russian speaking, and have previous
experience working on ARI/CDD programs in the Kyrgyz Republic or another former Soviet country.
A doctor with considerable experience in pediatrics, and the management of diarrheal cases and acute
respiratory infections is preferred. The consultant(s) should be familiar with WHO training mnaterials on
ARI/CDD, and should have experience in conducting program management courses, training of trainer
courses; and practical "hands-on" training in a clinical setting.
Background: The following shortcomings were identified by a World Bank/WHO Consultant in the 1995
evaluation of the Kyrgyz Republic's National ARI/CDD Control Program:
1.    Lack of national CDD/ARI policy and case management guidelines.
2.    Inadequate supervision, management, surveillance of ARI/CDD activities
at all levels.
3.    Lack of WHO ARI/CDD Russian materials in the Kyrgyz Republic, despite being available.
4.    Lack of ARI/CDD case management training including clinical "hands-on" practice.
5.    Inappropriate use of drugs for the treatment of diarrhea and ARI, inappropriate rehydration of
dehydrated cases, unnecessary hospitalization of CDD/ARI cases at tertiary, secondary and
primary health care levels
6.    Some incorrect mass media health messages on the home management of ARI/CDD.
The goals of the ARI/CDD subcomponent within the four years of the project are to rectify these
shortcomings and achieve the following:
1. Strengthen program management;
2. Upgrade health provider skills;
3. Upgrade health facilities;
4. Improve public awareness.
To realize these goals the consultancy team, in collaboration with the MOH, will organize and conduct
the following activities within the four years of the project:



108                            ANNEX 2.B
1. Strengthen Program Management:
- Baseline ARI/CDD Health Facility Review & National Strategy Development Workshop;
- Management training workshop for MOH & Oblast program managers;
- Provide ongoing technical assistance to the MOH and Oblast program managers;
- Study tour for key health personnel to see comprehensive ARI/CDD programs;
- Monitor software training for program managers by local consultant;
- Mid-project Mini-Health Facility Survey & Focused Program Review;
- End-of-project Mini-Health Facility Survey, Focused Program Review & Strategy Workshop.
2. Upgrade Health Provider Skills
Assist in the introduction of the adapted WHO ARI/CDD case management guidelines at Republican,
Oblast and Raion levels. The guidelines will cover management of the patient at home and in the
outpatient departments, and, management of complicated cases in hospital. The training will be on
intensive "hands-on" experience in a clinical setting with real patients. The following training activities
are required:
- Development of training package;
- Practical in-service training of hospital specialist trainers;
- Training of trainers workshop for National trainers;
- Training of trainers workshop for Oblast trainers;
- 6 Oblast Model Hospital staff training workshops (one per Oblast);
- Ongoing training evaluation of Central Raion Hospital staff through spot checks.
3. Upgrade Health Facilities:
- Monitor procurement and distribution of clinical equipment and essential drugs provided by the project
to selected health facilities.
4. Improve Public Awareness:
- Develop section on ARI/CDD in family health booklet and monitor field testing of the booklets on
family health for the general public funded by the project.
At the end of the 4 year project the MOH with technical assistance from the consultant(s) should have
achieved the following:
1. Strengthened Program Management:
- Trained child health program managers at the Ministry of Health and Oblast Health
Administrations;
- A national strategy, as part of the National Policy for ARI/CDD Control activities, to
rationalize and integrate activities.
2. Upgraded Health Provider Skills:
- Increased training capacity and management capacity within the Department of Continuous
Medical Education in Bishkek and Osh; and a decentralization of training to the newly
established Oblast Model Hospitals;
- Core teams of trainers at national, Oblast and Raion level;
- 500 health providers trained in the adapted WHO ARI/CDD case management guidelines:
-Pediatricians and infectious disease specialists at Republican, Oblast and Raion levels --
(UNICEF will train all primary health care level staff).



109                             ANNEX 2.B
3. Upgraded Health Facilities:
- Model hospital, polyclinic and FAP in each Oblast with restructured, rationalized and
integrated pediatric and infectious diseases services.
- Essential drugs for ARI/CDD training activities provided to 20 model training centers. After
specific training a small pack of essential drugs and supplies for ARI/CDD will be given to
every FAP and SVA team attending the training course.
- Essential drugs provided in two annual tranches for ARI/CDD to 12 Raions (28% of all
Raions) for distribution to SVAs and FAPs. The Raions will be selected on the basis of
having the worst maternal, perinatal and infant health indicators.
4. Improved Public Awareness:
- Booklets on famnily health, containing an ARI/CDD section for the general public distributed
free of charge from primary health care facilities.

113                            ANNEX 2.C
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 2.C: PRIMARY HEALTH CARE - TUBERCULOSIS CONTROL
SUB-COMPONENT
A. Background
1.    A WHO analysis in March 1995 found the tuberculosis situation in the Kyrgyz Republic to be
grave. Even during the Soviet period, tuberculosis case notification rates were among the highest of the
whole USSR. More recently, after a few years of decline, case rates have increased by an average 3.9%
annually between 1989 and 1994, when 60 cases per 100,000 population were reported. In Bishkek city
this rate was 90.7 per 100.000 in 1994, i.e., similar to that of some of the most affected developing
countries. The largest proportion of cases still occurs in the young adult age groups, i.e., the most
productive segment of the population, and in 1994, over two thirds of all cases occurred in persons less
than 45 years of age. Using the very scarce data available from tuberculin surveys, 25 cases of smear
positive tuberculosis would occur annually in the Kyrgyz Republic. However, this estimate must be taken
with caution. Considering the poor attractiveness of the current Program, the fact that many patients may
not be diagnosed because of the current constraints, and the very high rate among the young adults, the
incidence of tuberculosis (all cases) in the Kyrgyz Republic may be higher than reported, i.e., up to 70
per 100,000.
2.    Mortality from tuberculosis has increased by an average 13.6% per year between 1988 and 1994,
when it was 13.6 per 100,000 population, i.e., the one of the highest in the whole Europe and the former
USSR. In Bishkek city the mortality rate was 29.7 per 100.000 in 1994. Because of reduced case finding
activities, the mortality rate could be much higher than reported. At present, it can be estimated that more
20% of all cases result in deaths; therefore, true tuberculosis mortality may be in the order of 15-20 per
100,000, if undiagnosed and untreated cases are included.
3.    In addition to this serious epidemiological situation, drug resistance levels, particularly to the most
important antituberculosis drugs, isoniazid and rifamnpicin, are reported to be on the increase from already
very high levels recorded in 1985-1987. This situation may even be worsening due to the current
inadequate treatment regimens and the increasing defaulting rate.
4.    The are two types of problems which are compromising the effectiveness of the Tuberculosis
Program. The first is that the economic crisis is affecting the budget for health, resulting in lack of drugs
and supplies. The second is that the existing well developed infrastructure is not utilized in a cost-
effective manner, as the policies of the National Tuberculosis Program are not in line with the recent
WHO strategy for global tuberculosis control, which is based on provision of short-course chemotherapy
to, at least, all sputum smear-positive cases.
5.    The analysis of the findings on TB control activities revealed a number of major problems. The
expensive and poor-yielding active screening of large proportions of the population by mass miniature
radiography is still being conducted, although to a limited extent due to lack of funds for maintenance
of the mobile van teams. Diagnosis is based on various tests and gives priority to chest radiography rather
than emphasizing smear microscopy. Tuberculosis patients are hospitalized for an excessive period of
time, although recently less than in the past due to lack of funds, and often kept in a convalescent status
in sanatoria for months. Patients are not treated with standard regimens, but drug combinations are often
individualized, also due to a serious lack of essential drugs; this results in low cure rates and the
production of chronic infectious cases, often infected with strains of M. tuberculosis resistant to drugs.



114                            ANNEX 2.C
In addition, because of the weaknesses of the Program, defaulting rates are increasing rapidly, therefore
favoring continued transmission of tubercle bacilli in the community and onset of drug resistance. Finally,
WHO recommended recording and reporting systems are not in place; therefore, evaluation of treatment
cannot be accomplished.
6.    The WHO tuberculosis Program consultants made specific recommendations in order to have a
5-point policy package for effective tuberculosis control adopted by the Government. Discussions with
Dr A. Alisherov (Director of the National Tuberculosis Research Institute - NTRI) and Dr P.I. Limarev
(NTRI Chief Epidemiologist) during the October mission, as well as the recommendations from the First
Meeting of the National Tuberculosis Program Managers from the countries of Eastern Europe and the
former USSR held in Warsaw, Poland on 20-22 June 1994, resulted in the production of a document
adopting the WHO modern strategy of tuberculosis control in a cost-effective manner. This document,
already approved by the MOH, will be submitted for approval to the Cabinet of Ministers, showing the
commitment of the Program managers seriously to renovate the control strategy.
B. Role of Other Donors
7.    Despite the severity of this public health problem, no donor agency has undertaken any serious,
comprehensive and sustainable activity to help the National Program cope with the ongoing deterioration
of tuberculosis control.
C. Rationale for Bank Involvement
8.    Tuberculosis control, according to the "World Development Report 1993" is among the most
cost-effective of all interventions. A new strategy of tuberculosis control in the Kyrgyz Republic may
indeed have a profound impact, as it will prevent deaths and disabilities among the most productive age
groups, and, at the same time, make more effective'use of scarce resources by simply reducing length
of hospitalization, number of beds, and other costly interventions.
D. The Sub-Component
9.    The overall goal of the sub-component is to improve the management of the tuberculosis control
program in the Kyrgyz Republic. The two specific objectives are:
(a)    To reduce the risk of tuberculosis transmission in the community and tuberculosis
morbidity and mortality. The project would aim at expanding and improving free, mostly
ambulatory, treatment with short course chemotherapy of patients with infectious
tuberculosis to achieve reduction of infection rate in the population of the Kyrgyz
Republic.
(b)    To institute policy reforms and improve program management to achieve greater
efficiency and effectiveness of tuberculosis control activities. The project would aim at
a better integration within the revitalized primary health system, at strengthening the
institution management and financing system, personnel and operational methods for
disease control, and at developing and disseminating cost-effective methods and materials
for improving control and sustaining the progress made.
10.   The project would revitalize the National Tuberculosis Program (NTP) by providing support for
program implementation to the existing central (NTRI), oblast, and raion structures and to the primary
health care services countrywide.



115                              ANNEX 2.C
11.   The tuberculosis control sub-component will consist of the following activities implemented during
the period 1996-2000.
(a)    Improvement and free provision of tuberculosis diagnostic services in at least one facility
per raion or city (be it a tuberculosis dispensary or a general health facility), and
upgrading of the quality of bacteriological services at raion and city level.
(b)    Provision of free short course chemotherapy and other treatment services to all
tuberculosis cases, with directly observed therapy during, at least, the initial phase of
chemotherapy, and achievement of high cure rates among all smear positive pulmonary
cases.
(c)    Improvement of case management by adoption of WHO recommended recording and
reporting system, and adequate supervision.
12.   Institutional strengthening of tuberculosis control will include the following:
(a)    Establishing, within the MOH, a formal NTP and under its leadership guarantee the
operational support to the three levels of tuberculosis care, i.e., the oblast, the raion/city
and the primary health care structure. The central unit of the NTP, based at the NTRI
should have adequate staff facilities and budgets to be responsible for formulating,
supervising and enforcing national tuberculosis control policies.
(b)    Reorganizing and upgrading the oblast level of the NTP within the oblast tuberculosis
Dispensary to plan, manage and supervise activities conducted within the oblast.
(c)    Reorganizing and upgrading the raion level of the NTP in each raion in order to plan,
manage and supervise control activities within the PHC system.
(d)    Implementing administrative, technical and financial reforms to improve the system by
which the central unit finances and manages control activities through the oblast level.
13.    The following investment and activities will be implemented in the Kyrgyz Republic during a 5-
year period of implementation (1996-2000). A detailed tuberculosis policy package of administrative and
technical reforms governing tuberculosis control activities has been agreed with the NTRI. Assurances
were obtained that the Kyrgyz MOH will guarantee that the tuberculosis policy package is implemented
stepwise in the country in all its terms and conditions, and maintain its provisions in effect for the full
project implementation period. The tuberculosis policy package constitutes the basis for the development
of a national manual for tuberculosis control. It addresses the main issues currently facing the Kyrgyz
control program and provides a basis from which rapid progress on tuberculosis control can be expected.
Its main features provide:
(a)    Chemotherapy for eligible patients and all case detection examinations, follow up, and
other procedures done by existing tuberculosis and PHC facilities, and supervision of
chemotherapy within hospitals or by feldshers and polyclinics, shall be free of charge to
the patient.
(b)    Only approved short course chemotherapy (SCC) regimens would be stocked and
prescribed by the raion tuberculosis coordinator.



116                               ANNEX 2.C
14.    Diagnostic services will rely on self referral by symptomatic cases (passive case finding).
However, health workers at all levels, and particularly, peripheral doctors and feldshers, will be
encouraged to actively identify suspected tuberculosis cases and refer them to the nearest raion facility
for confirmation of diagnosis. The project encourages reorganization of the diagnostic services by which
at each raion there is at least one facility (preferably a raion hospital, alternatively a tuberculosis
dispensary or a general health polyclinic) where a microscopy center and an X-ray unit are
accommodated. These facilities should be supervised for their tuberculosis component by the raion
coordinator who should, therefore, be based ideally at the central raion hospital. Each microscopy center
will have at least one binocular microscope for rapid and reliable screening of stained sputum smears.
In raions where distances or population densities are such that reliance on one single facility for diagnosis
is inappropriate, additional diagnostic services located at polyclinics or ambulatories must be available.
15.    Chest radiography examination will be provided to all persons with symptoms suggestive of
tuberculosis referred by doctors or feldshers at the designated raion facility (a general hospital or a
tuberculosis dispensary, if available) capable of accommodating the X-ray unit and the microscopy center.
Patients with abnormal films will have at least three sputum specimens examined for tubercle bacilli. For
patients living in distant parts of the raion, a brief 6-7 day hospitalization may be necessary to complete
the diagnostic screening and make the decision on treatment. Procedures to be followed for diagnosis will
be based on clear instructions. In brief, patients with abnormal chest X-ray and positive sputum smears
will be considered tuberculosis cases and treated accordingly. Those with abnormal chest X-ray and
negative sputum will be reviewed by a doctor of the facility where the diagnostic screening was done
(preferably a tuberculosis specialist or a physician who has been trained on tuberculosis) and a decision
on treatment will be made; in these cases, sputum cultures should be obtained. Those with negative chest
X- ray will receive broad spectrum antibiotics and be discharged to their referring health care worker for
reevaluation. Naturally, as treatment decisions will depend largely on primary health care doctors,
adequate training will be required by the tuberculosis specialists available in the Raion. It would be
preferable if, during the process of primary health care reorganization, tuberculosis specialists are
designated to work in primary health care facilities or at general hospitals, so that a specialist is present
for rapid consultation in each institution where decisions on treatment are made.
16.    About 30,000 chest X-ray films and about 350,000 smears are expected to be required. As
radiographic screening to rule-out tuberculosis will be performed within primary health care facilities,
cost of films should be covered by the primary care structure, except in those cases where tuberculosis
is confirmed (in such instances, the project will cover the cost of the initial film and of a second one
when this is necessary). The project provides appropriate equipment (about 170 binocular microscopes,
1 fluorescent microscopes and other essential laboratory equipment), along with ancillary supplies for
microscopy (containers, slides, and stains), and radiography (X-ray films). Training for PHC doctors
and feldshers to recognize and refer tuberculosis symptomatics will be provided by the raion tuberculosis
coordinators.
17.    Chemotherapy:         The primary target of the tuberculosis component is the achievement of
high cure rates (usually more than 85%), as rapid case detection and sputum conversions of infectious
cases are the best means for prevention of tuberculosis transmission. The cure rate indicates not only the
proportion of cases who convert to negative sputum smear, but also the proportion of those who remain
sources of infection. Since the lives of patients not cured are prolonged by inadequate treatment over the
expected survival of untreated cases, their cumulative number increases the pool of infectious cases. In
addition, inadequate treatment is likely to produce drug resistant strains and their spread. Therefore, the
main target of tuberculosis control is to reduce drastically the sputum smear positive sources of infection.
In order to achieve this target, specific technical criteria must be strictly followed. In brief, regimens
must be adequate, and standardized according to specific patient categories. In addition, chemotherapy



117                               ANNEX 2.C
must be supervised, i.e., drug intake must be directly observed by the administering doctor, feldsher or
nurse, at least during the 2-month initial phase of treatment. Treatment will be mostly on an ambulatory
basis, although hospitalization may be necessary for advanced and severe forms of tuberculosis. A final
decision on treatment modality (fully ambulatory vs. hospitalization for the initial phase) will be made
only after analysis of results from pilot projects. Finally, to facilitate drug administration, to improve
patient compliance, especially during the continuation phase, and to protect against the onset of drug
resistance to isoniazid and rifampicin, drug combinations are essential. The project would provide for the
purchase and distribution of the five basic drugs required. Drug types and dosages have been estimated.
18.    Monitoring of treatment results is indispensable to evaluate efficacy of chemotherapy. This is
accomplished by regular sputum smear microscopy at the end of the initial phase of treatment, and at least
twice during the continuation phase, of which one at the end of it. Therefore, an additional 6 sputum
smears per patients enrolled, i.e., about 50.000 sputum smears, will be done. Treatment results will be
monitored utilizing cohort analysis of all enrolled patients based on six clearly defined categories of
treatment outcomes.
19.    Case management: The revitalized NTP will adopt and maintain a standard WHO recommended
recording and reporting system. This include the following forms: tuberculosis raion register, laboratory
register, tuberculosis treatment card, request for sputum examination, and quarterly and yearly reports.
These forms will be approved by the MOH. Translation and printing of the forms will be supported by
the project.
20.    Financial Incentives:  The government should approve and introduce a system of financial
incentives for case detection and cure of tuberculosis patients. Funds can be derived from the savings
resulting from ambulatory treatment and abandoning previous expensive practices. Key elements for
incentives will be:
(a)    A 30 som payment to any health worker who originally refers tuberculosis suspects for
examination, if the diagnosis of tuberculosis is confirmed by microscopy.
(b)    Primary health workers who manage a tuberculosis case will be paid 60 soms at the end
of treatment of a smear-positive patient in whom cure is documented. Health workers
will be paid 30 soms for the management of smear negative patients after treatment
completion. These additional payments, as well as the regular salaries, will be ensured
by the Government and paid by the raion health administration on a regular basis. If
drug administration is not fully supervised in the initial phase, as shown by the treatment
cards and registry, payment will not be made. Payment will not be made to the
tuberculosis facilities staff and to hospital staff managing hospitalized patients.
21.    All oblasts implementing the new program will adopt the standard registry and forms at the
beginning of the project. The registry will make it possible to identify raions with lower than average case
detection rates and low proportion of bacteriologically confirmed cases. This will allow the oblast
supervisors and the central unit to target management efforts and training to improve the situation. It will
allow the oblast tuberculosis coordinator to monitor and evaluate treatment results in each raion and to
identify those who need additional training and supervision. These registry, forms and reports will
provide the most important tool for program supervision and monitoring.
22.   Institutional strengthening:  Selected staff at the NTRI have been historically responsible for
control activities in the Kyrgyz Republic. The MOH will need to formalize the establishment of a central
unit responsible for planning of the operational steps in implementing, monitoring, and evaluating the



118                                ANNEX 2.C
NTP; for coordination of the NTP at Oblast level, Raion level, and primary health care level; for
purchase and distribution of the needed supplies, especially antituberculosis drugs, laboratory equipment
and stains, forms, registers, manuals, and other materials; and for training of the personnel involved in
the NTP. The MOH will guarantee that it will maintain the central unit with facilities, budgets, functions
and staffing ensuring implementation and sustainability of the project. The staff for the Central unit
should consist of at least: I director, 1 supervisor, I person responsible for drug supply, and 1 training
coordinator. Computer and printer, their maintenance and a course on computer use for 4 persons will
be covered in order to provide adequate management skills. Eight photocopy machines and 8 telefax
machines for NTRI and the oblast coordinators will be provided to allow rapid flow of information. The
clinical staff of the NTRI should consist of the necessary number of clinicians to cover a rationalized
number of beds, which should be reserved to serious or difficult-to-treat cases.
23.    Within, the central unit, the Central Reference Laboratory (CRL) should consist of:   I
Bacteriologist responsible for supervision of Oblast and Raion mycobacteriology laboratories (2
laboratories should be visited every month), 1 Microbiologist, I Senior Technician, 2 microscopists and
1 technician for culture and drug susceptibility testing. The CRL will be primarily responsible to
supervise the network of microscopy services and to train oblast dispensary laboratory technicians. The
second priority of the CRL should be to determine reliable primary and acquired resistance patterns in
the country, which constitute a valid indicator of treatment efficacy. This surveillance of drug resistance
should be done on a representative sample of smear positive cases.
24.    The Oblast unit, based at the Oblast Tuberculosis Dispensary, is responsible for coordination of
control activities in the whole Oblast and supervision of personnel with visits to each Raion/City every
two months; for organizing training programs for staff in Raions/Cities; ensuring that quarterly reports
on case-finding and treatment outcomes are carefully prepared in each Raion/City and sent to the Oblast
level; for acquisition and distribution of needed supplies and for coordination of the tuberculosis activities
with the primary care level. Once the NTP is fully implemented, the staff of the Oblast unit should
consist of at least: 1 director, 1 supervisor, 1 officer responsible for training, statistical and
epidemiological analyses, coordination of supplies, and financial matters. The clinical staff of the Oblast
Tuberculosis Dispensary and/or hospital should consist of the necessary number of clinicians to cover a
rationalized number of beds. The Oblast Laboratory should consist of: I Microbiologist, 1 Senior
Technician, and 1 microscopist. The oblast laboratory will be primarily responsible to supervise the
network of microscopy services and to train raion laboratory technicians involved with smear microscopy.
Oblast laboratories will be responsible for culture examination of smear negative patients.
25.    The Raion/City unit is responsible for implementing the NTP at the Raion or City level through
the staff of the primary health care facilities; for supervision of treatment ensuring that recommended
regimens are prescribed, that patients on the initial phase of treatment are closely supervised, that
regimens are given for the required period, that patients completing treatment are discharged from
treatment, and that sputum smears are examined at the given intervals; for supervision of the Raion or
City tuberculosis register; and for the procurement of the necessary supplies for the Raion or the City.
The staff for the Raion or City unit should consist of one raion tuberculosis coordinator, also responsible
for training, statistical and epidemiological analyses, coordination of supplies, and financial matters. As
the number of patients expected per raion does not justify the maintenance of a specific tuberculosis
laboratory, microscopy services within the raion should be accommodated into general polyclinics or
hospitals. The Raion or City Laboratory should consist of at least 1 laboratory technician trained in smear
microscopy.
26.    Health education: Patient's commitment to adherence to and completion of chemotherapy is
essential. Therefore, some health education material needs to prepared, tested and disseminated for this



119                             ANNEX 2.C
purpose. Some should aim at patients and their families, some at health care workers. The NTRI will be
responsible for the development and testing through the primary health care network.
27.   Program supervision: Regular, frequent supervision of each level of the program will be critical
to ensure implementation of the project component, particularly regarding the appropriate use of the new
registry system, to ensure improved diagnosis, chemotherapy and case management. Central, oblast and
raion supervisors must be mobile and have adequate operating budgets. One vehicle for the NTRI and
transportation costs in fuel for all supervisors will be provided. It is estimated that staff from the NTRI
will visit each Oblast quarterly; staff from Oblast level will visit each Raion monthly; and raion
coordinators will visit primary health care facilities where patients are diagnosed and treated on a constant
basis. Cost of regular national and oblast management meetings should be provided by the GOK.
28.   Training and Technical assistance: Training and technical assistance will be crucial for staff at
all levels to successfully adopt modern supervised, short course chemotherapy and the new policy.
Beginning in late 1995 two pilot projects in selected raions will start to evaluate the operational aspects
of the new program. These raions will form the core national training field base for future project
implementation. Basic training equipment will be provided for the raions selected for the pilot projects
and, later, for the whole country. The cost of conducting the training programs (central and oblast
managers - one course every other year; oblast and raion/city staff - seven courses, one per oblast every
other year; for PHC workers - two brief courses per raion every year; laboratory personnel at central and
oblast level - one course every other year; peripheral laboratory personnel - one course in each oblast
every year), and of the materials used will be covered by the project. The raion coordinator should
maintain close relationship with primary health care workers and ensure continuous education on
tuberculosis control. External consultants to assist and help in the national training courses will be
necessary.
29.    Project management: Technical guidance and supervision of the project will be provided by the
NTRI. The creation of the new Programn will imply some reorganization of the NTRI staff activities and
training. However, it should not require hiring of new staff. The recent development of a framework
for effective tuberculosis control adapted from the WHO document by the director of NTRI reflects the
commnitment to revitalize the NTP and the approval of pilot projects confirms this commitment. While
the MOH has already approved the new frarnework for tuberculosis control, the GOK is expected to adopt
the new WHO strategy before initiation of the project.   At oblast level, technical guidance and
supervision will be provided by the oblast tuberculosis coordinators. As the tuberculosis component will
depend on proper case management by primary health care workers, the strengthening of primary health
care of the Kyrgyz Republic will need to include adequate training and support related to tuberculosis
activities. In addition, as incentives will be provided to PHC workers for diagnosing and curing
tuberculosis cases, financial management reforms should include the mechanism of payment of incentives.
The GOK will bear a considerable supervision load for the project in terms of staff training and support.
External monitoring of the project will require a consultant to visit the country twice a year for the entire
duration of the project. A supervision input of the WHO tuberculosis Program is planned for about 4-6
staff weeks per year.
Benefits and Risks
30.   Benefits: There will be various types of benefits: disease transmission benefits and illnessideath
avoided benefits. They will result from rapid reduction in the pool of previously treated smear-positive
cases, and more rapid treatment and cure of new smear positive cases, and, as a consequence, from a
decrease in infection rate, resulting in permanently lowered incidence rates. The project would increase
the cure rate to over 85 % of newly treated infectious cases and improve the case detection rate to at least



120                              ANNEX 2.C
70%. In epidemiological terms, the benefits should be a rapid reduction of tuberculosis prevalence by 50-
60% from current levels and a, at lest, 5% yearly decline in the annual risk of tuberculosis infection. In
addition, mortality from tuberculosis will sharply decline within as short a period of one or two years.
Eventually a decline of the annual number of new cases due to reduced transmission of tubercle bacilli
in the community will follow. Thus, enormous benefits could be achieved in a relatively short period of
time, considering that the health infrastructure is still largely in existence. However, further delays in
addressing this key health issue would definitely impact on future efforts of control, particularly if a high
number of chronic cases resistant to drugs are produced.
31.    By the end of the project, a few thousand people will be saved from premature deaths due to
tuberculosis and thousands of new infectious cases will be prevented. Patients, their families and
communities will directly benefit from avoidance of illness costs, disability and from preservation of
productive capacity. In addition, long term benefits can be expected for the next several years as incident
cases will start to decrease significantly.
32.    Finally, since hard-to-justify numbers of facilities and beds dedicated to tuberculosis control are
available in the Kyrgyz Republic, the project will, by its accompanying changes, rationalize the number
of beds and reduce the number of facilities.
33.    Risks: If implemented as conceived and designed, risks of this project component are small.
However, strong guidance from the central unit to oblast and raion units is necessary to ensure that the
new control strategy is strictly implemented. In fact, the main risk is that the lower levels of the Program
will persist with old technical practices emphasizing case finding rather than focusing on the achievement
of high cure rates. This may result from an ineffective adoption of the new strategy of diagnosis,
treatment and monitoring, and from defective implementation of the new policy changes. To help guard
against these risks, the government must guarantee that financial and technical policy changes are
adequately implemented at all levels.
34.    Another major risk, linked with the difficult transition phase of the Kyrgyz economy and political
system, could be the failure by the GOK to provide salaries for the officers involved with tuberculosis
control at various levels and the failure to introduce and sustain incentives for primary health care
workers diagnosing and treating tuberculosis cases. Government commitment on this issue is essential.
35.    As the new tuberculosis control strategy relies on adequate organization and structure of the
revitalized primary health care system, a failure or delay in implementing such a system will negatively
impact on efforts to successfully shift from hospital based-treatment to fully ambulatory treatment. In
addition, lack of training of general practitioners on tuberculosis control may endanger the project
effectiveness to diagnose cases. This can be addressed by the inclusion of tuberculosis in the educational
curriculum of primary health care workers, as well as by the reallocation of tuberculosis specialists within
primary health care facilities to guarantee adequate assistance to general practitioners during the initial
years of the project.
Monitoring and Evaluation
36.    Detailed reporting forms for monitoring of project progress and criteria to be used following
WHO recommendations have been agreed with the NTRI. Indicators for measuring progress towards
project implementation are:



121                             ANNEX 2.C
37.   Indicators for measuring progress towards project development objectives
(i)    Achievement of an organizational structure of the program  based on the WHO
recommendations (central, oblast, raion, and phc);
(ii)   Production of a project protocol reflecting the WHO strategy for tuberculosis control
from which a national manual will be eventually obtained;
(iii)   Rationalized use of beds for tuberculosis;
(iv)   Number of trained staff at all levels (4 at central level, 3 per each oblast, 1 per each
raion). Trained staff will be aware of the new tuberculosis control strategy, be able to
supervise staff, set objectives, develop work-plans, prepare quarterly reports, and will
be able to identify and correct management problems.
38.   Indicators of Program Management: The maintenance of accurate records on each individual
patient, and periodic reporting is essential to the good management of the NTP at each level.
(i)    Recording: Data collected should give as true a picture as possible of what is actually
happening in the program of diagnosing and treating. The data should be reliable,
relevant, and uniform. To achieve this a standard set of documents and reports will be
used in all units involved in the sub-component. These are attached and shall not be
modified by the Kyrgyz in any way.
(ii)    Reporting: Reports on case finding and treatment results will be reported from the Raion
or City TB coordinator to the Central Unit via the Oblast TB coordinator. Three forms
(attached) to be used are:
(a)    Quarterly report on new cases and relapses of tuberculosis;
(b)    Quarterly report on the results of treatment of the patients registered 12 - 15
months earlier;
(c)    Quarterly report on Program management.
These three fonns will be completed by each Raion or City TB coordinator, checked by
the Oblast coordinator and submitted to the Central Unit where the results will be
computerized for easy retrieval and production of analytical reports.
39.   These indicators will be verified respectively by the following means:
(i)   training course participation and trainers' feed-back;
(ii)   site visits and progress reports by WHO consultants (twice a year);
(iii)   assessment of the quality of documents produced;
(iv)   surveillance data sent to WHO;
(v)    cohort analysis reports sent to WHO.
40.   Progress indicators will clearly show whether and where project objectives are being achieved.
A quarterly report system will allow to assess the progress of case finding and treatment results.



122                             ANNEX 2.C
41.   Loan covenants requiring special attention. To ensure success of the project there must be
commitment by the GOK to approve the following conditions:
(a)    Formalization of the proposed three-level structure of the revitalized National
Tuberculosis Program (central, oblast, raion) and approval of the policy package for
tuberculosis control prepared by the NTRI based on the following 5 elements;
(i)    The Kyrgyz Government must be committed to a National Tuberculosis Program
aiming at a nation-wide coverage, as a permanent health system activity, better
integrated into the existing primary health structure with technical leadership
from a strong central unit based at the National Tuberculosis Research Institute
in Bishkek. The structure should be appropriate to the country health system and
guarantee rational utilization of existing health services;
(ii)    Case detection through predominantly passive case-finding (i.e., detection of
cases which spontaneously present at the health services) ensuring that 3 smears
are obtained from every patient with radiologic abnormalities;
(iii)   Standardized case definitions of all tuberculosis cases, with supervised
administration of short-course chemotherapy in, at least, the initial phase for all
smear-positive cases;
(iv)   A system of regular drug supply and adequate stocks of drugs at each level
(Central, Oblast, Raion);
(v)    A standardized recording and reporting system for monitoring case detection and
treatment outcomes.
(b)    In order to ensure adequate case management of tuberculosis patients through the primary
health care system, the government should introduce a system of financial incentives for
case detection and cure of tuberculosis patients utilizing its own resources (part of them
could be derived from savings resulting from ambulatory treatment and abandoning of
previous expensive services). Elements of this system are:
(i)    A 30 som payment to any health worker who originally refers tuberculosis
suspects for examination, if the diagnosis of tuberculosis is confirmed by
microscopy.
(ii)   Primary health workers who manage a tuberculosis case will be paid 60 soms at
the end of treatment of a smear-positive patient in whom cure is documented.
Health workers will be paid 30 soms for the management of smear negative
patients after treatment completion. These additional payments, as well as the
regular salaries, will be ensured by the Government and paid by the raion health
administration on a regular basis. If drug admninistration is not fully supervised
in the initial phase, as shown by the treatment cards and registry, payment will
not be made. Payment will not be made to the tuberculosis facilities staff and to
hospital staff managing hospitalized patients.
(c)    As the case detection and treatment of tuberculosis patients will become the responsibility
of primary health care workers, the GOK should consider reallocating current



123                             ANNEX 2.C
tuberculosis specialists from tuberculosis in-patient facilities to primary health care
facilities. It will be desirable to have in each facility where major decisions are made on
diagnosis and treatment (Raion hospital and/or polyclinic) a tuberculosis specialist who
could train and assist primary health care physicians until they acquire adequate
knowledge on tuberculosis case management. It is essential that tuberculosis be
established as a major topic in the educational curriculum of a primary health care
physician and/or feldsher, in view of the larger role they will have in tuberculosis case
management. These conditions should be met in order to safely shift from a vertical
tuberculosis control program to the one integrated into the primary health care.
(d)    As ambulatory care seems an adequate and cost-effective manner to provide tuberculosis
treatment, two pilot projects before countrywide program implementation should be
established to evaluate the feasibility of providing ambulatory treatment during the initial
phase of chemotherapy through the primary health care system, as compared to drug
administration through hospitalization of all patients. The GOK should be committed to
approve fully ambulatory treatment of tuberculosis, if results of the pilot projects
demonstrate similarity of treatment outcomes. In addition, the GOK should limit the
provision of antituberculosis drugs to the raion coordinators or to institutions directly
involved with the tuberculosis program.
(e)    Study protocols similar to that for pilot project should be prepared by NTRI with the
assistance of WHO, and, translated in Russian should be ready by the project
effectiveness. All necessary documentation, i.e., treatment cards, registers, and forms
should be reproduced and distributed to the areas proposed for implementation in the year
1.
42.   ImDlementation plan: The first step towards implementation of the project is government's
agreement on the proposed framework for tuberculosis control and on the commitment to support the
project. As it is essential to define the operational problems which might arise from a completely new
approach to tuberculosis control, pilot projects are necessary which implement the new strategy of
tuberculosis control. Two 1-year pilot projects covering each between 100.000 and 150.000 population
have been established. Both these projects, expected to cover up to 200 patients in one year, would call
for better integration within the existing primary health care system regarding diagnostic procedures and
provision of treatment. One project will evaluate treatment results hospitalizing patients during the initial
phase of chemotherapy, whereas the other will assess the feasibility of fully ambulatory treatment through
primary health care providers. Funding for these projects has been obtained from a separate World Bank
grant. These pilot projects began in November, 1995. In order to ensure adequate methodology, pilot
project principal coordinators from NTRI were trained on the modem strategy of tuberculosis control
during the International Training Course held at the WHO Collaborating Center in Warsaw, Poland on
24-29 April 1995. Travel and accommodation costs were covered by WHO. The training course for
selected raion coordinators involved in the pilot project was held in Bishkek in September 1995. The
Central Reference Laboratory Coordinator was retrained in smear-microscopy during a course organized
by WHO in Tbilisi, Georgia in June 1995. In September 1995 the training course in smear-microscopy
for raion laboratory technicians was established in Bishkek. Training tools consisting of standard WHO
training modules translated in Russian, WHO treatment guidelines and laboratory manual in Russian were
provided by WHO. A protocol for a pilot project of tuberculosis control programs was prepared by the
NTRI with the assistance of WHO. This protocol translated in Russian and reproduced, was distributed
among the pilot project raions TB coordinators. Registers, treatment cards and report forms were
translated in Russian and distributed. Drugs and necessary laboratory equipment were purchased and



124                            ANNEX 2.C
stocked by the project coordinators, and will be disbursed gradually during the entire period of pilot
project.
43.   The results of the pilot projects, as well as the progress of implementation of the new primary
health care system, will guide the final choice of diagnostic and treatment policies for the nationwide
World Bank assisted project. A detailed project manual will be produced by the central unit with the
assistance of WHO. In the first year of the project the oblast of Issyk-kul and Chui and the city of
Bishkek will be covered. Before implementation of the full project, a number of training courses will be
held. Training tools tested during the pilot projects will be reproduced and distributed. Training of
primary health care workers must be particularly intensive in order for them to become acquainted with
tuberculosis diagnosis and treatment. In addition, as the decision of enrolment of a patient for treatment
will become responsibility of primary health care workers, consideration should be made to reallocate
current tuberculosis specialists from tuberculosis in-patient facilities to primary health care. Diagnostic
services will be upgraded with necessary equipment and provided with necessary supplies. Procurement
and distribution of those will be coordinated by the central unit. The necessary drugs will be purchased
and stocked by the project coordinator according to the expected number of cases in each area. Based on
the progress of the initial implementation steps, the project will be expanded to the oblast of Naryn and
Talas during year 2 and to the rest of the country during year 3.
