147                            ANNEX 3
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 3: FACILITIES REHABILITATION COMPONENT
A. Introduction
1.    The extent and content of facilities in a health infrastructure directly influence quality and
efficiency, and thereby the recurrent expenditure required in the health sector. The need for staff, their
production capacity, the need for consumables and supplies, maintenance services and energy can all be
measured in relationship to the buildings in which services are provided.
2.    At present an intense re-evaluation is being made in the Kyrgyz Republic, of the goals,
capabilities and responsibilities of the health as well as other public sectors. In particular attempts are
being made to address the consequences of the present drastic reduction in available resources. It was
therefore that the World Bank in the process of preparing a broad reaching health sector rehabilitation
project, included an evaluation of the physical and functional condition of health facilities and equipment,
and the frame-work within which the health infrastructure is financed and managed
3.    Four missions were undertaken in the periods between 3rd and 19th October 1994, 22nd February
to 10th March, 18th June to 11th July and 15th to 31st October 1995. During these missions meetings
were held in Bishkek with senior officers in the Ministry of Health, in particular those dealing with the
provision of health services, and the management of capital investment. Further meetings were held with
leading experts on architecture, capital projects management and equipment procurement.
4.    In the October 1994 mission visits were made to the Diagnostic Center, the National TB Institute
and the National Dermatology and STD Institute in Bishkek, the Raion Hospital at Kochkor, and various
facilities in the Naryn Oblast including the central hospital, the TB center, the Mental Diseases hospital
and the Dermatology and STD clinic.
5.    In the February and June 1995 missions, health facility sites in the Chui Oblast where emphasis
was made on visiting primary health care facilities including raion hospitals, rural hospitals, ambulatory
care centers and feldsher/midwife posts (FAP's)
6.    The following is a report based on those meetings and visits.
Summary of Findings
7.    In visiting a number of all types of health institutions in the Kyrgyz republic, the following main
problems are apparent:
(i)    The government is no longer making adequate funds available to pay for running and
maintaining buildings, plant and equipment in the health sector, and therefore correct
levels of efficiency and quality within the system are becoming increasingly difficult to
maintain
(ii)    A large amount of equipment is not working due to shortages of spare parts and lack of
servicing. Further important consumables are in short supply or are not available. These
factors seriously detract from the efficiency of the health delivery system.



148                                ANNEX 3
(iii)    The present over-provision of health facilities and equipment (working or not working)
at all levels, implies that correct financing of their use will require resources which at
present are not available
(iv)    Any introduction of new health programs aimed at improving efficiency in the provision
of services will often imply changes in the use of existing facilities, in some cases also
implying changes to the facilities themselves.
(v)    Very few norms and standards for health facilities have been formulated which relate to
the present situation.
(vi)    Administrators of health facilities are not well trained in estates management. They are
given very little support when introducing technical changes to their buildings and plant
(such as installing new heating systems, or when implementing changes or trying to
achieve savings.
(vii)   Though originally there was apparently a well functioning hierarchy of technical and
administrative expertise available to support MOH capital investment activities, including
MOH staff and sister ministry's architects, engineers and builders, this is no longer easy
to assemble. Further, although the Ministry of Health's office for Capital Investments is
still operating, though with a negligible work-load, the Ministry's Central Office for
Maintenance has been completely disbanded.
(viii)  The Ministry of Health has no overview of the buildings, plant estates etc, which
constitute the health service infrastructure. It is not able to make overall plans and
priorities for realignment activities.
8.     Where improvements are required to the efficiency and quality of health services provided in the
country in the existing health infrastructure, it is extremely difficult for the Ministry to implement the
changes required which will facilitate these.
9.     A significant reorganization is required within the Ministry of Health in order to re-orientate
present technical expertise on the repair, rehabilitation and reutilization of existing structures and plant.
That is to say to move away for the development of completely new and totally unnecessary capital
investment projects.
10.    In this new concept the Ministry will require tools to create an adequate organization, to collect
the necessary information and to implement the correct policies. It is suggested that the World Bank
project, through implementing limited constructional works in connection with the implementation of its
health reform project, could contribute significantly to helping the Ministry to develop these tools.
B. Background
11.    The health service system in the Kyrgyz republic is based on a very comprehensive infra-structure
of health facilities at all levels, which in spite of the country's difficult topological characteristics,
provides good access to the system for most members of the population.
12.    The health facilities include central hospitals, district hospitals, polyclinics, feldsher/midwife
stations, village ambulatory stations, as well as special separate institutions for tuberculosis,



149                              ANNEX 3
dermatology/sexually transmitted diseases. Together they provide a very wide range of services for both
out patients and in-patients, with an extensive portfolio of medical equipment and a large total of beds
Quality of the Health Infrastructure
13.   Usually planned on large open sites, the facilities making up the health infrastructure are generally
of very substantial construction with thick masonry walls both internally and externally and with heavy
reinforced concrete floors. Carpentry- and joinery work is also generally very substantially dimensioned,
though electrical and plumbing installations are usually of poor quality and less than adequate in terms
of the numbers and quality of installations and fittings.
14.   Generally the buildings reflect the quality of provision found in all other public buildings in the
country and no attempt has been made to pinch and save. However it is considered surprising that in an
environment with relatively extreme temperatures, passive means of energy conservation through the
improvement of insulation, -particularly in floors, ceilings and roofs, do not seem to have been given
very much attention
Central Health Institutes
15.   The central health institutes located in Bishkek, as typified by the Diagnostic Center and the
National Tuberculosis Center appear openly to have been establish on a 'no expense spared' concept.
These buildings are very impressive, in the case of the TB institute, almost flamboyant. There have
apparently been several approaches from different governments with a view to purchasing the hospital
to convert into an Embassy.
16.   Though still apparently functioning satisfactorily, there is evidence that both these institutes are
beginning to deteriorate functionally through a lack of routine maintenance and absence of spare parts.
The initial solution here is the injection of large amounts of finance which obviously is not available,
before the whole financing of the sector has been reorganized.
17.   It could also be relevant to encourage these institutes to pool the technical expertise that they
individually have access to and at the same time to carefully evaluate the appropriateness of the
technology that they use. It is suggested that any infusion of capital in present conditions would not
achieve any sustainable improvements.
Hospitals
18.   The hospitals are usually made up of a large number of individual departments. A relatively small
hospital with less than 400 beds, usually has separate self-contained departments for Urology, Pulmonary
/gastroenterology, Surgery, Trauma, Resuscitation, Ophthalmology, Paediatrics, Cardiology, Infectious
diseases.
19.   On the same site there is also usually a polyclinic containing units reflecting all these departmnents,
as well as a multi-disciplinary veterans hospital. On separate sites will often be located independent
institutions for obstetrics and gynecology, TB, dermatology and STD and psychiatric patients.
20.   It is suggested that this ensconcement of the departments in independent units does not easily
support modem coordinated nursing and treatment methods. It is also noted that there is very little cross-
utilization of equipment and facilities between departments, even when they are located in the same
building.



150                              ANNEX 3
21.    Generally space standards in hospitals are low and the rooms in the buildings are small in
relationship to the activities which go on there.
22.    Operating theater rooms are usually adequate in size, though many of the necessary secondary
rooms such as utility rooms, sterile set up rooms, duty rooms and change rooms for staff are missing.
23.    In the ward sections, rooms for in-patients often contain 8-9 beds, where there is only reasonable
space for 4-6 beds, and often other important rooms, (such as utility rooms, ward stores, patients and
staff toilets, bath rooms, staff duty rooms and patient day rooms) are either under-dimensioned or
missing.
Rural Hospitals
24.    There appears to be two distinct types of rural hospitals in the regions visited. One is the
'Blenheim Palace' type of building, with very heavy two story structure with often very elaborate
ornamentation in the form of columns, porticos and plinths. Usually containing as few as 30 in-patient
beds they are very inflexible in use. They are also very expensive to heat. In future use it is very difficult
to conceive what the upper story can successfully be utilized for.
25.    The other type of building is a one story rural type structure which is infinitely more appropriate
and flexible. These buildings lend themselves considerably more readily to the changes in function being
suggested
26.    The rural hospitals contain suites of different types of consultation rooms, for general medicine,
surgery, gynecology, dentistry and rehabilitation. Further there are often well equipped delivery suites
and operating rooms. Wards are usually very cramped. Throughout the buildings toilet, sanitary and
utility facilities are very minimal, and important functions are missing.
27.    A present large numbers of staff seem to be dealing with comparatively small numbers of patients
Health Centers
28.    Generally the health centers visited were reasonably well planned, with well dimensioned spaces
and adequate numbers of item of equipment and furniture. However conditions varied and the structural
problems seen, usually the result of leaking roofs, appear to be increasing. Equipment and furniture were
also generally in poor condition.
29.    Health centers generally seem to be still very well staffed, with usually 2 to 3 general clinicians,
as well as pediatricians, gynecologists and dentists. However the total 10-15 staff which appear to be
present at most health centers do not appear to be overflooded with patients, a staff to patient ratio of 1
to between 4 and 6 being typical on a daily basis.
FAP'S
30.    These are usually small buildings where a limited staff of a nurse and a midwife carry out
something comparable to a district nursing function on behalf of a small local population. These buildings
with 3-4 rooms are usually domestic in character, with few installations and little equipment.
31.    The neatness and cleanliness of these units was always impressive and on the basis of present
functions, there does not appear any need for technical or financial support.



151                               ANNEX 3
Health Facility Design
32.    There are many details of design in the institutions which were visited, which are not considered
appropriate for health buildings. For example; no effort is made to ease access to buildings, rooms,
toilets, etc., for patients on wheeled stretchers or wheel chairs, trolleys, or any other wheeled transport
or carrying vehicles. Door widths and the positions of doors do not give good access to buildings and
rooms; There are not sufficient hand wash basins in ward rooms and procedure rooms for staff to be able
to wash their hands between dealing with patients; and floor coverings, wall finishes, design and
installation of sanitary fittings, etc., do not easily facilitate the maintenance of correct conditions of
hygiene.
Installations
33.    Heating installations, electrical and sanitary reticulations and fittings, drainage and sewage
disposal facilities, are not always adequate. Further there appears to be no attempt to conserve energy.
34.    Hot water is not readily available in any areas of the institutions.
35.    Electrical reticulations appear very basic. Electrical fittings, such as socket outlets, switches etc.,
are often damaged, indicating that they are not strong enough for use in public buildings.
36.    Sanitary fittings are usually very inadequate. They are difficult to keep clean, and in many
institutions large amounts of water is allowed to run to waste due to the lack of simple maintenance to
sanitary fittings and to the water reticulation.
37.    Refuse disposal seems mostly well organized, both sites and buildings seem to be efficiently
cleared of refuse and litter. The exceptions here appear to be the central hospitals in Bishkek.
Medical Equipment
38.    Inspection of a limited number of facilities suggests that at present there is an adequate number
of items of equipment of all different types, which with correct redistribution where problems have
occurred, will in most cases be sufficient to reasonably satisfy requirements over the coming period.
39.    However the situation with spare parts is in many cases very acute, in particular as the total lack
of maintenance and thereby lack of use of the items of equipment involved will quickly lead to total
dilapidation, and the destruction of valuable resources.
40.    During the inspections it was observed that in many institutions, there were no autoclaves
available or functioning, and instruments and syringes required for patient procedures were usually
'sterilized' in ovens. It is very doubtful whether this treatment is adequate. There was also observed an
acute shortage of surgical instruments and syringes.
Supplies
41.    There is now an acute shortage of all types of software, such as mattresses, pillows, bed linen,
covers and uniforms, medical supplies such as bandages, syringes and utensils and consumable, such as
x-ray film, chemicals and reagents and filters etc., as well as all forms of detergents and cleaning
solutions. All contribute significantly in reducing the quality of the activities taking place in the



152                              ANNEX 3
institutions, and also increasing efficiency. In particular the shortages of consumables seriously inhibit
the satisfactory functioning of services provided at the institutions.
42.    These problems require urgent attention and action.
Maintenance
43.    Though there are standard allotments in the recurrent budgets for the different health institutions
to pay for maintenance, these monies have not during the last three years, been made available. The
Ministry of Health has further closed down its central maintenance department and in effect the concept
of providing maintenance for buildings and equipment outside the Bishkek area has mainly been
discontinued, at least until finance is again made available.
44.   It is recommended that in spite of the extreme shortage of resources for maintenance, a policy
is established by MOH on appropriate and feasible (possible) maintenance for health institutions.
Management of Buildings and Estates
45.   In the question of the management of health building complexes, in providing equipment and
furniture to the different rooms, in ensuring that cleaning procedures are correct and adequate, in carrying
out maintenance and in making adaptations to existing plant and facilities, (for example when adapting
heating systems for changes of fuel), the managers of health institutions do not seem to be getting
adequate technical support for their decisions.
46.   Managers of health institutions should be correctly instructed regarding different aspects of the
management of buildings and grounds of health facilities. They should be informed of correct procedures
for carrying out the various necessary procedures. They should also be provided with qualified technical
support for decision making, design, ordering and implementation.
47.   At central level the previously well functioning hierarchy of Ministry technicians, consulting
architects and engineers, appears to have been disbanded through lack of work. Members of the MANAS
project group have been assigned this area of interest but there is still urgent need to reconstitute a
qualified forum of technical expertise.
48.    This should not be too difficult, as it has existed previously. The difficulty may now relate to the
changed status of individual architects and engineers who previously provided technical guidance but who
now consider that their input should be correctly financed.
49.   It should be relatively simple to solve the financial problems. If civil works are carried out in the
project and are eligible for containing with Local Competitive Bidding, it will be necessary to include in
budgets for paying fees to local private consultants. Their activities in developing the documentation
required for carrying out the projects should also allow for them participating actively as consultants to
the MOH civil works advisory committee.
Norms and Standards for Health Facilities
50.   The MOH civil works advisory committee should also be the forum where relevant building space
and quality norms are determined for health facilities in the Kyrgyz Republic. This would apply both for
the evaluation and effective utilization and also repair of existing buildings, as well as the formulation
long term budgets and investment plans for future constructions.



153                             ANNEX 3
Building Costs
51.   Due to the apparent lack of recent examples of completed health facility construction in the
Kyrgyz Republic, the local architects and quantity surveyors did not feel able to give precise rates for
different types of building construction activities. However rough figures were given and in this respect
it was agreed that it would be appropriate (in view of the imminent (though limited) activities being now
considered), that a price system should be established and developed, in order that in the near future
reasonably accurate and transparent / adaptable / escalatable estimates can be made.
Rationale for Bank Involvement
52.   The Bank emphasizes strongly the need to iniprove efficiency in the delivery of health services
in the Kyrgyz Republic and, through concentrating on essentially primary health care programs,
emphasizes the potential for reorganizing activities from being essentially in-patient orientated, to be
essentially out-patient orientated.
53.   Improvement of Primary Health Care facilities will naturally improve the status and acceptability
of the services provided there to the population.
54.   The results which can be achieved through improving the effectiveness of different medical
programs, will be reinforced by systematic efforts made improve functional and hygienic conditions in
the health infra-structure to ensure that the necessary improvements in efficiency are achieved.
55.    Further benefits which can be a result of including civil works activities in the various sub-
components of this project is that it will be possible to influence the way in which the Ministry of Health
administers hospitals and other health buildings and their grounds. It will further encourage a greater
readiness to consider the recurrent costs effects of buildings and equipment, it will demonstrate the
potential for carrying out maintenance on minimal budgets, it will show how existing buildings can be
utilized reducing the need for new ones and it will involve different disciplines in a collaboration to
determine relevant norms and standards for use in the Kyrgyz Republic.
C. Activities Related to the Health Infrastructure
56.    Any effort to reduce the recurrent costs requirement for the health sector will imply significant
changes in the health service infrastructure. There are many possible strategies for this, all requiring
carefully planned and implemented programs. However it is recommended that future health programs
and activities should have the following main aims:
(i)    To reduce the extent of the health services delivery system
57.   This implies setting precise targets for future provision. (For example aiming to limit the numbers
of beds available in health institutions to 8 beds per 1000 population)
58.   These targets can be achieved by a number of means, including closing wards at lower level
institutions, by closing departments and by reducing the number of beds in individual wards, and by
reducing the average length of stay of in-patients in wards. This in turn can be achieved by improving
functional and hygienic conditions and thereby increasing efficiency at the institutions. (That is to say that
if all equipment is in working order and that there are sufficient drugs, reagents, X-ray film etc., and if
risks of cross-infection are kept a minimum and if the care programs aim to shorten the average length
of stay... .etc.).



154                             ANNEX 3
(ii)    To rationalize high technology within the health delivery system
59.   This can be achieved by concentrating surgical and specialized services at facilities at Raion level,
and also by evaluating carefully the distribution of equipment and installations to the lower levels.
(iui) To improve the efficient use of energy and promote the concept of enviromnental protection
60.   This can be achieved by introducing more economical heating systems which will also provide
water heating, by implementing water conservation and commodity material (eg. medical gasses)
conserving measures.
(iv)   To reintroduce appropriate maintenance services for both buildings and equipment
61.   This can be supported by establishing 'requirements for maintenance' as important procurement
criteria, and by the development of information systems for planning and administering maintenance. The
establishment of appropriate maintenance services can in the long term only be achieved by making funds
available through economies in other areas.
(v)    To inprove the overall management of the health infrastructure
62.   This can be supported by the development of app.opriate norms and standards for use in health
facility planning and evaluation. The norms would cover spacial provision norms , utilization criteria,
specification standards for materials and installations. It could further be supported by the development
and implementation of an information collecting system for providing data on the extent and condition
of facilities in the health service infrastructure.
63.   It should be emphasized that considerable more resources are used in hospitals and that the
potential in this sector for making savings is considerably greater. This fact in itself motivates for not
only focusing on Primary health.
COMPONENT ELEMENTS
64.   It can be anticipated that it will take a number of years to achieve the above goals, much longer
than the anticipated duration of the project. However, it is important to address the whole question of the
relationship of the Kyrgyz health infrastructure, its buildings, installations and equipment, to the quality
and efficiency of health services and thereafter to the recurrent costs required to finance those services
65.   The following activities would be included in the project and where possible integrated into the
various project components which are being proposed:
(a)    Carrying out pilot construction and procurement activities in connection with project components.
Essentially this will involve converting rural hospitals into polyclinics , upgrading ambulatory
centers and providing the equipment and supplies necessary to ensure efficient performance.
(b)    The training of technicians and administrators at all levels in estates management,
(c)    Development of norms and standards together with local experts,



155                              ANNEX 3
(d)    Development and implementation of information system for collecting data on health facilities and
for making masterplans for future development,
ANTICIPATED ACHEVEMENTS FROM THE INPUTS
66.   The results which can be anticipated from the input being proposed are as follows:
67.   In connection with programs being proposed in the project for the improvement of services, in
particular within the concept of primary care, Civil works and procurement activities will be carried
out as a basis for ensuring the successful implementation of the new services. Here in almost every case
works will concern the adaptation of existing facilities, and it is anticipated that the Ministry of Health
will here be able to obtain practical experience of the various (for them new) concepts being promoted.
'Reuse of existing structures', 'appropriate technology', 'user participation', 'energy conservation',
'estates management', will be concepts which it is anticipated will be demonstrated as essential for
incorporating in future work in connection with the health infrastructure of the Kyrgyz health services.
68.    As a consequence of the preparatory and development work necessary as a normal part of the
project development process for the above civil works and procurement activities, 'Estates management'
will be developed as an important concept which influences the efficient utilization of health facilities.
Using the norms and standards described below and participating in the implementation in the project of
an agreed information collection system, MOH managers will automatically be given a greater insight into
the means available to solve problems concerning the maintenance and development of the buildings and
plant under their supervision. Further in the procurement and contract management activities relating to
the project, it is aimed to effectively utilize resources available at local level. This is also anticipated to
fturther contribute to providing a basis for improving the performance of local managers in estates
management
69.   In order to carry out the intended civil works and equipment procurement activities it will be
necessary to develop in collaboration with Kyrgyz experts, appropriate Norms and Standards for
evaluating, planning, rehabilitating and constructing health facilities will be established. Generally these
norms will always refer to the present shortage of resources, and will strive to relate to accepted
concepts of appropriateness, and economy in use. The norms and standards will hopefully form the basis
for all future activities concerning the health infrastructure and will influence activities by both the
government and donors, influencing decisions which will be appropriate and sustainable.
70.   In the same way Masterplans will be developed to provide information of the condition and
content of the health facilities which will be the subjects of project activity. In this way the Ministry of
Health will be provided with a simple system for collecting comparable data on the scope and quality of
all facilities within the health infrastructure and at the same time a basis will be provided with tools for
making priorities and assessing the feasibility of making changes.
71.   The results of the project activities therefore are anticipated also to influence utilization and costs
in the secondary and tertiary health sectors.
D. Infrastructure
Objectives
72.   The main objective of this activity is to make repairs and functional changes required at raion
level health service facilities and provide specific items of equipment and consumables which will ensure



156                              ANNEX 3
that the correct conditions are available for both existing activities as well as for supporting the
implementation of new and revised programs
73.    A secondary objective is that through the development of the brief, in establishing norms and
standards, in developing routines for contracting and administration, in collecting data required for these
works, the Ministry of Health will be provided with a basis for improving its own Estates Management
and for defining the appropriate content and emphases for future rehabilitation projects.
Main Features
74.    The main feature of the activities proposed is the emphasis made on involvement of MOH/Kyrgyz
expertise, in order to ensure that the results attained are both appropriate and acceptable to local needs
and thereby are sustainable.
Requirements from the Ministry of Health
75.    The Ministry of Health would establish an appropriate policy with regards capital investments and
the management health infrastructure. It would acknowledge that every increase in buildings and plant
will imply unacceptable recurrent costs implications. The Ministry has already today many more square
meters of buildings than it requires to provide its different services, and the theme in future should be
acknowledged to be 'consolidate' 'rehabilitate', 'reuse', if possible 'decommission', rather than 'new
construction' and 'expansion'.
76.    New policy would therefore emphasize a complete freeze on the construction of new health
facilities and would indicate that investments and civil works activities should concentrate through repair
maintenance and adaption, on attaining maximum efficiency and appropriate functional quality in existing
structures.
77.   The organization of the Ministry's civil works administration would reflect this change of
emphasis, and present Ministry officers would have their Descriptions of Employment amended
accordingly.
78.   It is further desirable that the Ministry would establish advisory committees and stakeholder
groups, in order to receive technical support in matters dealing with both civil works activities and the
procurement and maintenance of medical equipment. The stakeholder groups are small communities of
users--both health authorities and local government officials, established at each facility to be
rehabilitated/upgraded. The work of the group will be to provide information as required on functional
problems and requirements, and to approve the proposals made for improvements and alterations. In this
way it is anticipated that the results of the investments will be better appreciated by local staff and users,
and log term aims regarding increased efficiency and quality will more easily be attained.
Needs for Rehabilitation of Facilities
79.    During the June/July 1995 mission a MOH/World Bank team carried out surveys at 27 selected
health facilities. Though mainly concentrating on ambulatory centers and rural hospitals which are agreed
will be the subject of the activities financed by the project, comprehensive data was also collected at
Raion Hospitals, District Hospitals etc., where it was considered that conditions were particularly acute,
and where it was anticipated that considerable savings could be made in recurrent costs requirements
through the consolidation and functional decondition of individual buildings.



157                               ANNEX 3
Results of the Inspections
80.    The result of the inspections is that more detailed information has been obtained on the actual
condition of a reasonable number of health facilities in one Oblast, and the requirements which exist for
rebuilding, renovation and refurbishing in order to provide conditions which are acceptable. Evaluation
of conditions at the different facilities has been based on assessment of two main factors, FUNCTIONAL
QUALITY considered in terms of OPERATIONAL EFFICIENCY.
81.    Observations confirm that in these areas there are a lot of improvements which should be made.
Especially that:
(i)    There are too many facilities (Provision norms,-beds, clinics, staff, are high, utilization and
productivity is low. Departments are over-institutionalized, there is no flexibility)
(ii)    Hygienic conditions are very poor (Wards are very cramped, toilet facilities limited, working
conditions for staff are poor, important equipment (such as sterilizing equipment etc..) is not
functioning, materials for cleaning is in short supply, staff are often badly motivated).
(iii)   Functional conditions are very poor (Shortages of working equipment, physical separation of
relating departments, lack of consunumables, etc.)
(iv)   Equipment and buildings are in bad repair (Equipment and buildings often are too difficult or
too costly to maintain. Resources for maintenance are not available, maintenance is giving low
priority by administrators)
(v)    Running costs are far too high (Over provision of facilities and amenities as mentioned above.
Further heating systems are often extremely expensive to run and maintain. Many district heating
systems have above-ground-none-insulated pipe reticulations which may be contributing to
atmospheric overheating but waste fuel and are not adequately heating the buildings).
82.    The implication of the surveys therefore is that it is easily apparent that refurbishment work is
required at all institutions, possibly with the exception of the FAP's. However it would be very wrong
for this or any other project to merely repair and repaint the facilities in their present form without
making an effort to correct the serious faults which have been observed which seriously detract from the
quality and efficiency with which services are provided, thereby incurring unreasonably high recurrent
costs.
83.    The costings included for the proposals made include for required functional changes. However
at this stage the detail of the provision still must be defined and agreed designs and specifications are
finally issued.
84.    Attached are illustrative drawings and specifications for three representative facilities; a raion
hospital, polyclinic, and health center. A complete set is in the project's working papers.



158                             ANNEX 3
E. Costs for Civil Works Activities
New Works
85.   It has been difficult to obtain reliable figures for the costs of new building construction in the
Kyrgyz Republic. There are two main reasons for this:
(i)    Very little new construction work has been implemented during the last 5 years,
(ii)    With regards the construction projects which were carried out before then, very little
attention seems to have been paid to costs.
86.   During project preparation, quite some time has been spent discussing costs with various clients,
technicians, 'experts', etc., and the following costs levels have been tabled and have generally been
accepted as being not unreasonable.
87.   The rates used do in fact compare generally to costs in other countries with similar conditions
to the Kyrgyz Republic, very low labor costs, some skills available, importation necessary of all but the
most basic building materials, etc.
Square Meter Rates
88.   The rates for construction used in compiling the proposals contained in this report therefore are
as follows:
Type of Facility            new construction
Rural Hospitals             US$ 240
Polyclinics                 US$ 240
Health centers              US$ 180
FAPS                        US$ 180
Elemental Rates for Renovation and Refurbishing Works
89.   In order to calculate the costs of the repair and replacement of various building materials it has
been necessary to apportion the above square meter rates to the different building elements used in
construction. This again is based at this stage on previous experience with other projects carried out in
other countries, adjusted for anticipated conditions in the Kyrgyz Republic.

175                                   ANNEX 4
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 4: MEDICAL CARE PROVIDER PAYMENT COMPONENT
A. Background
1.     The Kyrgyz Republic's health financing and delivery system reflects -- to a relatively large extent
-- the old regime of rational planning and hierarchic structure found throughout the former Soviet Union
health system.' Historically, the system has been financed in a centralized "top-down" bureaucratic
allocation process, based upon national budgets formulated and passed by the central legislative and
policy-making bodies.
2.      The health financing system has been beset by two significant issues, however, related to sources
of funds and uses of funds. The first is that allocated budget levels historically have tended toward
chronic underfunding. The old Soviet system allocated funds for health protection on a "residual" basis
from general tax revenues; levels of funds were determnined only after budgets were allocated for other
"productive" sectors such as industry, manufacturing, and defense. Over the last several years, national
independence and the general economic decline has exacerbated the issue of underfunding. Independence
from the Soviet Union has forced self-sufficiency for the Republic which historically enjoyed a positive
net transfer of resources from Moscow through the tax system (World Bank, 1992). The economic
decline that the Kyrgyz Republic (and most other parts of the NIS) has suffered over the past several
years has resulted in reduced tax revenues being generated locally. An emerging funding crisis in health
services has been the result.
3.      The share of the region's GDP devoted to health historically has been low, and also erratic since
the 1980s--falling from 6%  of GDP to just over 3%  for the New Independent States (NIS) as a whole.
In the Kyrgyz Republic, spending as a percent of GDP was 4.1 % in 1990, but has fallen for 3 of the last
four years. Schieber (World Bank, 1995) reports that the most complete information available is for
public spending including spending from the consolidated budget (including all Ministries not just the
MOH), extra-budgetary public expenditures on health, and enterprise spending on health. The ratio of
public health expenditures to GDP declined from 4.2% of GDP in 1990 to 2.6% in 1993, with an
increase to 3.6% in 1994. The GDP in 1994 is only 50% of the 1990 level. Similarly, 1994 real (GDP
deflator adjusted) health expenditures are only 46 % of the 1990 level, up from 40% of the 1990 level
in 1993. In short, there has been a 50% reduction in real output in the Kyrgyz Republic since 1990,
accompanied by a 54% reduction in real public health spending.2
4.      Schieber (World Bank, 1995) further found the picture even more bleak when per person
spending is converted into U.S. dollars using purchasing power parities (i.e., exchange rates which adjust
for cost of living differences across countries). Public budget health spending in the Kyrgyz Republic
in 1993 dropped to only one-fourth the 1990 level.
I Each individual in urban areas is assigned to a polyclinic and has a primary care physician (not a general practitioner, but a therapist,
pediatrician, or obstetrician/gynecologist); in rural areas, there is a basic health unit, called a FAP, of one or more physician-extenders (e.g.,
feldshers or nurses), and rural ambulatory centers.
2 According to Abel-Smith (1994), there has been a fall in output of the industrial sector of 50 per cent, and a drop in export revenue by
65 per cent between 1991 and the end of 1993. Between 1992 and 1993, there was a decline in real GDP by 16 per cent and tax revenue fell
from 14 per cent of GDP to 8 per cent. The economy has further suffered from hyperinflation. Retail prices have been rising by 15-25 per
cent a month.



176                                ANNEX 4
5.     A second problem with the former Soviet approach to health care delivery is that it does not
encourage the efficient use of resources by providers or between them. This was due in part to a system
which allocated resources based on traditional central planning production input measures, such as
occupancy and numbers of staff and beds, rather than services performed, relative complexity of services,
or changes in health status. Schieber (World Bank, 1995) reports that recent trends are somewhat
encouraging, but overall numbers suggest a system that remains bloated. The number of beds per 1,000
population has declined from 12 in 1981 to 9.5 in 1994, but is still some 15 percent above the OECD
average. Similarly, while hospital admissions rates have declined from 25 percent of the population being
admitted in 1987 to 18 percent in 1994, the Kyrgyz Republic is still 20 percent above the OECD average.
The average length of hospital stay has declined by about one day over the past 15 years to 15.6 days
in 1994, but remains slightly above the 14 day OECD average. The average number of inpatient days
of care per year per person has declined from 3.6 days in 1978 to 2.8 in 1994, but is still some 15
percent above the OECD average. The hospital occupancy has declined from 90 percent in 1978 to 81
percent in 1994, compared to an OECD average of 75 percent.
6.     The number of physicians per 1,000 population has increased by some 30 percent over the past
15 years to reach 3.1 in 1994 (of which 0.45 are dentists), somewhat above the OECD average of 2.2.
Only 35 percent are generalists (defined to include therapists, pediatricians, and obstetricians), whereas
the percent share in many OECD countries (excluding the U.S.) is closer to 50 (e.g., United Kingdom)
to 70 percent (e.g., Canada). The number of physician contacts per year is comparable to the high
utilization rates in the OECD countries.
7.     There are other health financing sector problems as well including an acute shortage of
pharmaceuticals, equipment, and supplies.3 These shortages have occurred partly at the expense of an
overemphasis of resources placed in staff and beds. Facility budgets based on production input measures
such as beds provide incentives to increase capacity. A high level of facility capacity within the system
results in high fixed costs. This problem has worsened over the last few years with the deregulation of
energy prices, exacerbated by inefficient heating systems. For example, in Issyk-kul Oblast utilities
consume approximately 30% of total costs. For small rural hospitals the problem is even worse, utilities
consume approximately 67 % of total costs. The decrease in financing and an increase in the proportion
of expenses allocated to fixed costs have combined to produce critical shortages and depletion of
inventory levels in pharmaceuticals, equipment, and supplies.
B. Current Flow of Funds for Health Care
8.     The health sector is financed (at least formally) from general taxation revenues. In general, the
responsibility of collecting general taxes has devolved over the last few years to the oblasts and rayons,
not the central government. At the same time, the scope and authority to tax remains inflexible and is
largely determined by the central govermnent. Once collected, money is either passed on to the central
Ministry of Finance or kept at the individual oblast level depending upon the central allocation formulas.
In some cases, the oblast receives additional subsidies beyond the general taxation base. Currently,
Bishkek and Chui pass along some of their revenues to the central goverrnent, while two oblasts -- Osh
and Djal Abad -- keep what they have collected. The remaining oblasts -- Naryn, Talas, and Issyk-Kul -
- receive central government subsidies in addition to what they collect. A few taxes (e.g., the road tax
' Many repons, including Abel-Smith (1994), a comprehensive review by Welsby (1994) and most recently Haycock (1995), report that
supplics of drags, equipment, asd consumables remain inadequate. Shortages mentioned specifically include anesthetics, X-ray plates, surgical
supplies, food for patients, gasoline for ambulances, fuel for heating water and even sheets. There is often no replacement or repair of equipment
needing spare parts. Equipment includes ambulances, incubators, respiratory equipment, autoclaves and washing machines. Schieber (1995)
nicely summarizes the pharmaceuticals problem.



177                                     ANNEX 4
and Presidential tax) remain strictly federal taxes.4
9.      The allocation or disbursement of funds works in a parallel fashion, with the central government
developing allocation norms across and within sectors, while the actual budget process has generally
devolved to the oblast and rayon levels.
10.    A  recent Bank study (May  1995) by Helga Muller found that in  1994 oblast and local
governments collected 84 percent of the health expenditure. The report suggests that while it makes sense
to delegate social programs to a subnational level, that there is increasing inequities across oblasts in
terms of revenues raised versus needs for social programs. Per capita public health spending varied by
1.7:1 across oblasts in the Kyrgyz Republic in 1994, but the report implies increased variations could
occur over time. The study recommends that the central government should mandate minimum service
levels and develop allocation formulas for critical expenditures in health in order to better equalize fiscal
capacity with actual collections as well as to better match collections versus actual needs and demands
for services.
11.      Within the health sector itself, there are four levels of disbursement of funds for facilities. The
first level is comprised of the Republican institutions, designed to serve the whole population and
generally highly specialized. At the second level the six oblasts and Bishkek city operate institutions of
a mixed primary and specialized nature, open to all citizens within the oblast. Each oblast is further
subdivided into rayons which operate primary care facilities, and local and district hospitals -- largely for
rural areas. After fourth sentence, add sentence: Finally, within the oblast, municipalities also operate
primary care facilities and hospitals, largely for urban residents. The central Kyrgyz Ministry of Finance
does allocate funds directly towards budgets for the national specialty facilities or the Republican
institutions. The central government also allocate budgets directly for medical education and research,
and more traditional public health activities.5 About six percent of the federal budget is earmarked for
certain pre-determined health care activities such as public health.
12.     The Ministry of Finance also sets expenditure norms for oblast and rayon level administrations.
With these norms, individual facilities at the oblast and rayon levels develop proposed budgets. For
inpatient facilities, the norms include nominal numbers of beds and senior medical personnel. For
outpatient facilities, the norms are based on capacity as measured by numbers of physicians and
"potential" numbers of visits. Budgets are reviewed and approved by local oblast and rayon finance
administrations, after consultation with facilities, the local ministries of health, and the local exchequer
office. The budgets are handed down in 18 restrictive budget categories such as salary, utilities, and
pharmaceuticals. Traditionally, funds could not be spent outside of categories, though there are reports
 A good example is the recent Presidential Decree on the Budget for the First Quarter of 1995 (dated January 25, 1995) which states that
'the size of allocations to the local budgets of provinces and Bishkek city in the following amounts:
o       from the VAT tax on Osh, Issyk-Rul, Naryn, Talas, Jalal-Abad provinces - 100 percent; on Chui province 42 percent, on Bishkek
city 22 percent;
o       from the excise tax on Osb, Issyk-Kul, Naryn, Talas, Jalal-Abad provinces -- 100 percent, on Chui province 42 percent, on Bishkek
city 23 percent;
o       from the profit tax on enterprises, incorporation, and organizations on all forms of ownership on Osh, Issyk-Kul, Naryn, Talas
provinces -- 100 percent; on Jalal-Abad province, 31.8 percent. Chui province 42 percent, on Bishkek city 23 percent;
o       from the tax on tumover in retail trade and service to the population on Osh, Issyk-Kul, Naryn, Talas, Jalal-Abad provinces -- 100
percent; on Chui province 42 percent, on Bishkek city 23 percent."
'These traditional public health categories include orphan homes, treatment of AlDs, support of infectious disease hospitals such as TB
sanitoria, basic sanitation and epidemiologic activities, emergency hospitals and transport vehicles, blood transfusion centers, cancer treatment,
narcotic addiction centers, and medical supply storage.



178                                         ANNEX 4
of some increased flexibility (Haycock, 1995). However, there is also anecdotal evidence that the
implementation of the Treasury system has acted to further restrict the flexibility of facilities to allocate
funds outside the budget categories.
13.      Local and oblast budgets are aggregated and returned to the central Ministry of Finance which
uses them to develop a tax plan to match expected revenue and spending.6 The central Ministry of
Finance then informs the oblast administrations of the final approval for public spending levels with a
recommendation (but not a necessary condition) for allocation between the sectors (Haycock, 1995). This
means that spending patterns between local sectors can be altered (for fiscal, political or other reasons),
though discussions with oblast and rayon leaders in other parts of the NIS suggest the shifts are minor
and infrequent (see Schieber, Back-to-Office Report, February 1995).
C. Recent Reforms
14.      The Kyrgyz Government proposed  a series of health sector reforms, including  the Health
Protection Act which was passed and signed into law in July 1992. The Health Protection Act calls for
a shift in priorities toward health promotion and disease prevention policies and a new emphasis on
primary and family-based care. It calls for changes in the form of property - conversion of pharmacies
and pharmaceutical institutions into private and joint-stock companies; conversion of medical institutions
into joint-stock companies; incentives to establish private hospitals, polyclinics, and physician practice;
and private pay and leasing arrangements within public facilities.
15.      The law provides for diversification and decentralization of sources of revenue as well. Financing
of health care can be moved "off-budget", and no longer be solely reliant on general government
revenues. At least one public health insurance fund ("Territorial Health Insurance Fund' -- or "THIF")
can be created in each oblast, to be financed through multiple sources of revenue.' Additional sources
of revenue include private or "voluntary" health insurance coverage, local user fees for pharnaceuticals
and outpatient services,8 and donor funding.
16.      More importantly, the recent legislation separates financing and payment for services from  the
I For example, the Prsidential Decree on the Budget for the First Quarter of 1995 (dated January 25, 1995) stated that the income tax 'levied
from the citizens by enterprises, institudons, and organizations in Chui province in the size of 58 percent and in Bishkek city 78 percent.'
' Sources of revenue include:
o        funds from the current 34.5% Social Insurance and Pension Fund payroll tax, which are currently used for payment of temporary
and permanent disability, social security, other forms of cash assistance, and health. (Approximately 86% of the payroll tax is used
for sick leave, pregnancy, adoption of children, child care up to 3 years of age, care of disabled children up to 14 years, sanitoria
and resort smy entitlements of from 18-24 days per year per worker. The remaining 14% is used for "other purposes".)
o        a new, minimum 6% payroll contribution paid by employers;
o        a capitated rate paid by the oblast govermment into the fund to cover non-working and exempted populations not contribudng through
the employer payroll - emnployees of "public budget organizations" (e.g., schools and hospitals), elderly, unemployed, disabled,
women and children. While the payments would continue to be made by the oblast government, actual source of funding would be
split about 75%/25% between revenues raised by the oblast and central government.
The oblast government would also continue to allocate funds directly to hospitals and clinics for construction/capital improvements, expensive
equipment, and education/training programs. The central government would continue support of medical education and research, and more
traditional public health activities.
While this funding approach decentralizes decision making, it also raises concems about perpetuating or exacerbating inequities in funding across
individual oblasts (see Haycock, 1995).
' See, for example, Haycock (World Bank, 1995) and Abt Associates (July, 1994) for more complete discussions of user fees and user

179                                      ANNEX 4
direct provision of care. This means that payers of health care services can contract with providers,
determine the method of payment, and the payment amounts or rates to providers. Third-party payers
of services act as a catalyst for the prudent purchase of care on behalf of individual consumers, by
changing the incentives faced by hospitals, polyclinics, and individual providers.9
17.     Likewise, administrators could receive payments based on the number of cases, the severity of
the case, and the performance of treatment. These payment changes would create incentives that
encourage more efficient organization and management of service delivery and improved quality of the
care. The focus of care could shift toward primary and outpatient care. Polyclinic and hospital
administrators would have increased flexibility to utilize resources as they see fit to produce services
efficiently. Public sector providers now provide the bulk of services, though private sector providers
could compete to provide services.
18.     Payment changes are consistent with donor organization recommendations over the last few years.
Bobodilla and Ellis (World Bank, 1992), for example, called for incentive-based payment systems to
replace the current budgeting approaches. Abel-Smith (1993) and Holmes (June 1994 Manas Conference)
have called for capitation-based physician payments and bulk-billing arrangements for inpatient care.
19.     The insurance reform  law was originally written to be implemented in 1993. Concerns about its
design and rapid implementation schedule, as well as problems with reforms in other sectors (e.g., the
banking system) and general lack of technical expertise have delayed the implementation date several
times since."' Other health sector changes have occurred. Most notably, direct payments for outpatient
services and for outpatient pharmaceuticals have been introduced. Haycock (1995) notes that current law
prohibits users fees for inpatient services. Nevertheless, there is a sizable base of anecdotal evidence
(e.g., discussions with hospital administrators) indicating user fees are applied to inpatient services.
Patients also frequently must provide their own food, drugs and consumables for inpatient stays." The
recent United Nations Development Program (UNDP) report (1995) found that user fees account for an
increasing and sometimes majority portion of revenues for some facilities. More troubling is the recent
ODA survey showing that user fees are both increasing rapidly but also affecting economically vulnerable
Importantly. the reform law generally follows an approach familiar to NIS countries that stems from initiates strting in 1989 when the
so-clled New Economic Mechanisms (NEM) were announced as approved in Moscow under the former Soviet structure. The NEM provided
for greater local autonomy and a number of demonstration sites in each of the republics. For example, Kemnerovo, Kaluga. Samira, and St.
Petersburg were demonstration sites in the Russian Federation.
Three areas of change were focused on:
o        restructuring of financing -- health budgets would be developed differendy. A formula of 18 production input categories was replaced
by a stndard per capita payment based on a mix of measures of resource use and historic trends;
o       organization and management restructuring - through greater autonomy and management systems;
o        improved internal efficiency in the delivery of care - through improved payments for services within nd across facilities.
The NEM demonstration sites were cancelled or superseded by subsequent health insurance laws in Russia and other NIS countries, but the
general principles of NEM took root in terms of the recognition of the need for greater flexibility of resource aflocation, payment to personnel
and some limited management restructuring approaches.

recendy, Abel-Smith (1994) noted some progress has probably been made in assuring a reasonably efficient and safe system of banking
transactions.
" Haycock (1995) has found that inpatient facilities also accept 'informal payments' for pharmaceuticals, food, consumables, even acess

groups and negatively affecting quality and access to care."2 Finally, a small number of employer groups
negotiate contracts with hospitals for services to their employees, public sector physicians accept private
pay patients through special leasing arrangements, and at least 4 new private or "voluntary" insurance
companies have emerged in Bishkek and elsewhere in the country.'3"4
20.      These activities point to a health sector in transition in the Kyrgyz Republic, moving from  a
publicly-based central planning scheme to a more privatized, public/private mix in the organization and
financing of care. At the same time, these activities are incremental and may have unintended
consequences for efficiency and access to care. As a result, the government decided to test its legislated
financing and delivery system reform model as a way of implementing a more comprehensive strategy
for change. While implementation of its 1992 law is still delayed, it has chosen to pilot test the model
in at least one geographic area, Issyk-kul Oblast.
21.      Initially, the demonstration did not cover the entire oblast; rather, it was designated to begin in
a "Free Economic Zone" (or FEZ) of the town of Karakol, and three surrounding raions within the
oblast. 5 The site was chosen because of the FEZ designation, the relatively strong industrial base and
potential mining resources, and the relatively high per capita level of spending on health care in this
oblast relative to the rest of the country (the Kyrgyz Republic MOH, 1994). The Issyk-kul pilot
requested that the pilot be extended to the entire oblast, the Ministry of Health approved this request.
Implementation of a comprehensive demonstration in a portion of the oblast creates political and technical
problems including cross-subsidies and competitive disadvantages across rayons, and the need to
determine how to reimburse for referrals across boundaries.
22.      In Spring 1994, the Ministry of Health requested technical assistance from  USAID/Abt Associates
in implementing the Issyk-Kul health reform experiment, as well as assistance in training personnel who
would help implement it. Many of the details of the demonstration follow from the principles of the
health reform legislation. At the same time, the legislation, left many specific implementation issues to
be addressed during the transition period. This was particularly true in the areas of organizational and
delivery system structure, payment methods, quality assurance, information systems design and cost
accounting methods.

181                             ANNEX 4
23.   The pilot demonstration provides the first opportunity to implement the new law in a systematic
way for a specified geographic area, and to use lessons from it for a longer-term national implementation.
An initial proposal was developed by USAID/Abt Associates (see report of July 15, 1994) for the
demonstration design and implementation; the proposal was subsequently finalized and agreed upon with
the oblast and central governments. A number of activities and initiatives started over the latter part of
1994 relative to implementation of the demonstration.
24.   The medical care provider payment component would extend the Issyk-kul pilot to Bishkek City
and Chui Oblast. For full implementation of the Issyk-kul pilot to occur, the government must ensure
that all legal and regulatory obstacles are removed. It is a condition of negotiation for the project that
a Presidential decree and Government edict are issued which remove any barriers to the implementation
of new provider payment systems in the Issyk-kul pilot. Meeting this condition requires that at least the
following three points be addressed:
(i)    Savings from rationalization of facilities or implementation of new provider payment
methods will be retained and reinvested.
(ii)    Establishment of a mechanism to enforce the pooling of funds at the national, oblast,
municipal and rayon level.
(iii)   Authority to establish new payment methods including at least the following:
(a)    The line-item budget system will not be used to reimburse providers.
(b)    Determination and resolution of conflicts with the Treasury System created by
new provider payment methods.
(c)    Capability to institute performance-based pay.
(d)    Facility management autonomy including hiring and firing.
25.   The USAID-funded Issyk-kul pilot program expects implementation to move forward rapidly after
these legal and regulatory issues are finalized. The program has experienced some problems including
turnover of local counterparts and difficulty in obtaining necessary commodities such as equipment for
Family Group Practices and computers. Most of these commodities were not included in the USAID-
funded program, so grants were secured from other donor agencies. The World Bank project should
provide enough commodities to facilitate the desired changes in behavior and produce visible change to
positively impact the attitude of the population toward health reforms.
26.   The project files contain an operational plan, accomplishments, next steps and responsible party
for the Issyk-kul demonstration as of October, 1995 (in November, 1995, progress continued on many
of the next steps).
27.    The most favorable geographic areas for extending the Issyk-kul demonstration would be Bishkek
City and Chui Oblast. These sites are preferable for several reasons: 1.) relatively dense, urban
population; 2.) most affluent geo-political units (in terms of per capita income and tax revenues paid);
3.) relative sophistication of medical institutions and trained personnel; 4.) relative excess of medical
providers, especially physicians per capita in Bishkek; 5.) contiguous geographically, effectively sharing
market areas for medical services; 6.) proximity to health sector leadership for observation and
evaluation; and, 7.) proximity to technical coordinating staff for implementation purposes under the
credit. Discussions with leadership (e.g., central and oblast-level MOH, MANAS tearn) indicated interest
and agreement in using these geographic areas as demonstration sites.



182                              ANNEX 4
28.   The conceptual plan for the World Bank project outlined below closely resembles the Issyk-kul
demonstration plan, except there is no additional source of financing from an employer-based health tax.
The Kyrgyz Republic can use the base of experience from the Issyk-kul demonstration to extend the
reforms to Bishkek City and Chui Oblast. Specifics aspects of each element may not be replicated
exactly, the demographic, epidemiologic, and economic features of a different geographic area may
dictate design modifications; local preferences and values will be important as well. These differences
will be identified in the technical assistance provided to refine the Issyk-kul products to fit Bishkek City
and Chui Oblast.
29.    Problem: The health care system in the Kyrgyz Republic is dominated by the hospital sector,
which consumes more than 70% of resources. One reason for this inefficient allocation of resources is
the financial incentives for facilities contained in the current line item budgeting system. The budgeting
system is based on input measures such as number of beds and staff, and rewards increases in capacity
rather than the efficient provision of high quality health services.
30.   Purpose and Benefits: The medical care provider payment component would create financial
incentives for all providers of health services to manage care provided to patients more efficiently and
cost-effectively while maintaining or improving the quality of health services provided to the population.
Decision-making on the allocation of resources would be decentralized so that both providers of health
services and the population have more responsibility and accountability for the efficient provision of high
quality health services.
31.   The methods used to pay hospitals, polyclinics, and physicians would be changed to foster
efficiency in the health care delivery system and promote the use of primary care. Payment for care
would no longer be made on the basis of production input measures but rather on the basis of
performance as measured by services provided, or episodes of care completed. The new provider
payment methods would strengthen the connection between the quantity and quality of services provided,
and the financial reimbursement received for those services. Incentives would encourage treatment of
patients on an outpatient as opposed to inpatient basis, reduce length-of-stay for those patients who do
require hospitalization, and reduce unnecessary referrals to specialists and for diagnostic tests.
32.   Benefits resulting from achieving the goal of increasing the efficiency of the health care sector
include the following:
(i)    Ability to provide more health services with current funding levels due to more efficient
allocation of existing health sector resources.
(ii)   Increased confidence in the health sector by businesses and the population leading to
greater willingness to invest in health.
(iii)  Increased responsibility and accountability of health providers for the outcome of health
services provided to the population.
(iv)   Increased consumer participation in decisions about their health care.
(v)    Improvements in the quality of health services.
(vi)   Increased access to health services for the population.



183                            ANNEX 4
(vii)   Greater satisfaction and prestige for medical professionals, including attracting and
keeping quality professionals.
33.   Provider payment reforns would change the financing and the organization of care in two
important ways. Payment for care would be separated from delivery of care. The payer would focus
on paying for the best care at the lowest cost -- it would purchase care rather than maintain facilities.
The provider would have more flexibility to focus on delivering the most optimal mix of services with
the best outcome. Secondly, providers would be "at risk" in that they would now be responsible for
differences between payments received and costs incurred. Providers would be accountable for more
efficient and higher quality of care.
34.   This provider payment component does not directly address the institution of new forms of
financing for the health sector, such as an employer tax, or the creation or organization of insurance.
General reforms already occurring in the Kyrgyz Republic exhibit a broad range of activity related to new
sources of financing and insurance. The German donor fund GTZ also is providing support and technical
assistance related to insurance. The Bank can provide only marginal benefit in including this component.
35.   Implementation: Although the Government is concerned about the level of foreign technical
assistance, by definition, the institution of new provider payment methods requires substantial technical
expertise in hospital payment, physician payment, clinical practice, cost accounting, clinical information
systems, management, finance and internal auditing, computer systems, accreditation, and quality
improvement. Although most of the products would have been developed in the Issyk-kul pilot, they will
need to be adjusted to fit Bishkek and Chui Oblast because the population composition is different.
36.   The implementation strategy of this component is to use foreign experts selectively to produce
defined products. Each product, for example hospital payment, would have a dedicated foreign expert
or module leader. Module leaders would have three goals in their work: 1.) Refine the Issyk-kul
products to fit Bishkek and Chui; 2.) provide initial training to a broad audience on the systems/products
through workshops, and intensively train a local expert in the specialty area; and 3.) work with the local
expert to implement this aspect of the project.
37.   The local expert for each programmatic area would be responsible for imnplementation of the
product and continued training of health personnel. Qualified computer specialists would support them.
They would both work for the fiscal intermediary or one of three associations formed to facilitate the
implementation of the provider payment reforms. The three associations are a Primary Care Physicians
Association, a Hospital Association, and an Accreditation Commission.  Establishing these three
organizations should contribute to the sustainability of the reforms. The Accreditation Commission needs
to be independent to objectively determine whether a facility meets the minimum standards required to
operate and receive payment for services. In the medium to long-term, the Primary Care Physicians
Association and Hospital Association will serve as representative bodies in a more decentralized, market-
oriented health sector. While the local experts serving as their employees are initially supported by the
project, they are developing marketable skills which will be in demand in the future.
38.   Description: The medical care provider payment component requires US$4.3 million in Bank
funds to implement over a four year period. The Government of the Kyrgyz Republic would be required
to provide contributions in terms of staff time and expertise. Implementation of this component requires
structural change in the health services delivery system as well as development of new provider payment
systems and the corresponding fmnancial infrastructure to support them.  There are four major
programmatic elements of the medical care provider payments component encompassing both structural
change in the delivery system and new provider payment systems.



184                               ANNEX 4
39.    The firt element is comprehensive restructuring of the primary care sector through the
establishment of new primary care group practices (called Family Group Practices in the Issyk-kul
pilot). Currently, there are very few general or family practitioners. A group consisting of a therapist
(internist), pediatrician, and obstetrician/gynecologist is required to provide comprehensive primary care
services. In order to reimburse providers a capitated rate for the provision of all primary health services
to an individual, a entity capable of providing the entire range of primary health services must exist.
40.    The primary care groups would be the focal point of the new provider payment methods: they
would enroll people and receive a capitated rate for providing a specified set of health services.
The financial incentive for the groups is to provide services rather than referring patients to other parts
of the health delivery system. Different models of primary care should be encouraged, and over time
clinical retraining and new medical institute curriculum will lead to the development of general and family
practitioners. The primary care groups should be independent, form voluntarily, and be organized as a
business. They can initiate the development of non-profit entities within the health sector and also
become private practitioners.
41.    Approximately 300 Family Practice Groups would be needed to serve the population of Bishkek
City and Chui Oblast, estimated at 1.6 million people. Each group would be capable of enrolling and
serving approximately 5,300 people. A significant portion of the provider payment component budget
will be directed at developing the capacity of these entities to respond to the financial incentives of the
new payment methods.
42.    The project activities included in this element are as follows:"6
(i)    Formation of a Primary Care Physicians Association to coordinate physician formation
of groups, and provide continuing education and representation for primary care
physicians. In addition, the Association would employ the local experts to implement this
element including practice managers, fundholding specialists, and computer specialists.
(ii)   Identification of potential locations for Family Practice Groups (FPGs), determination of
policies on leasing publicly owned health premises to FPGs, and initiation of process of
establishing FPGs as non-profit entities.
(iii)   Form approximately 150 FPGs in the first year of the project, 100 in the second year and
50 in the third year. Formation of the groups consists of physicians voluntarily choosing
their partners; funding minor renovations of new practice locations - allowing FPGs to
establish a separate identity and be dispersed throughout the community; funding
equipment to enable the groups to treat patients rather than refer them; funding clinical
training to increase clinical capability and begin development of a cadre of general
practitioners; and establishment of financial systems, including procurement of computers
and development of practice managers providing business skills for the groups. The
groups should be established in both urban and rural areas, the Issyk-kul experience
shows that the FPGs integrate well into the current rural health delivery system.
(iv)   Retraining of 1200 primary care physicians would occur over the first three years to
coincide with the establishment of primary care groups. Internists, pediatricians, and
obstetricians/gynecologists would receive both classroom and on-site training to improve
their capability to manage care on an outpatient basis and begin the conversion to general
16see the Gantt Chart for detailed implementation timeframe



185                            ANNEX 4
and family practitioners. This process would be enhanced by changes in the curriculum
for medical students. Training for specialists would be offered as they begin to redefine
their role in the new health care delivery system. To develop the business skills
necessary for the FPGs, one doctor from each group as well as practice managers, at a
ratio of one for each 20 FPGs, would receive training on FPG financial and information
systems. This in-country training would be enhanced by study tours for practice managers
and primary care physicians.
43.   A clinical training center would be established and provided with equipment and materials at the
Republican Department of Continuous Medical Education (DCME) to provide clinical training to
physicians. The development and provision of clinical training materials, books, and curriculum materials
will magnify and sustain the impact of training activities.
44.   The second element is free choice of primary care provider by the population and enrollment
of the entire population into primary care groups. Participation by the population in decisions about
their health care gives providers an incentive to provide high quality services. If patients are not
satisfied, they can change providers. Information would be provided to the population to help them make
informed choices about their medical provider.
45.   The project activities included in this element are as follows:
(i)    A public awareness and marketing campaign to inform the population about FPGs and
their right to choose their physician.
(ii)   Actual enrollment of the population in FPGs and establishment of a enrollment database
for the new provider payment methods.
(iii)   Production of information comparing the performance of FPGs across various indicators,
allowing the population to evaluate providers and make educated choices.
46.   The third element is new incentive-based provider payment systems for primary care
services, outpatient specialty and ancillary servlces, and hospital services. These payment systemns
replace line item budgets for facilities with payment for health services at the patient level. Although the
experience of the Issyk-kul pilot will determine the exact form of these systems, the main concepts are
a capitated rate and case-based hospital payment. A capitated rate is the payment of a set fee to a
provider for the provision of certain health services to an individual. A case-base hospital payment
system reimburses hospitals a prospectively set fee depending on the type and severity of the treated case.
47.   Currently, national, oblast, municipal, and rayon governments finance and control their own
facilities using a budgetary system which allocates funds through predetermined functional categories such
as salaries and medical supplies. The health reforms envision the population choosing providers and
providers receiving payment for specific services provided to patients. The focus of payment for health
