services changes from the facility level to the patient level. A fiscal intermediary is needed to pool the
funds from the national, oblast, municipal, and rayon sources and reimburse providers for health services
they provide to the population. Funds would no longer be delineated by government level and separately
allocated to facilities; services would be purchased by the fiscal intermediary from one pool of funds.
48.   The fiscal intermediary requires institutional capacity to pool the funds and manage the new



186                               ANNEX 4
provider payment systems. The development and installation of new computer systems is especially
important. Provider payment on a treated case basis increases the base unit for reimbursement many
times. For example, in Issyk-kul, the base unit for hospital payment will increase from budgets for about
50 facilities to payment for about 50,000 treated hospital cases. This very large increase in the number
of transactions requires the development of automated systems to manage the payment process.
49.    Current accounting systems provide a good starting point; however, they could benefit from the
introduction of accrual accounting and more sophisticated financial reporting to present available
information in a more useful form. Internal auditing and control procedures are necessary. Relationships
between the fiscal intermediary and the treasury and banking system need to be clearly defined. The
transition to a stable market economy may require the banking system to gradually shift its focus toward
handling financial transactions efficiently, away from serving as a control mechanism.
50.    The project activities included in this element are as follows:
(i)    Formation of a Accreditation Commission to license and accredit providers to operate and
receive reimbursement. In addition, the Commission would employ the local experts to
implement this element including accreditation specialists, facility surveyors, quality
assurance specialists, and computer specialists.
(ii)    Formation of a Hospital Association to represent facilities. In addition, the Association
would employ the local experts to implement this element including hospital payment
specialists, cost accounting and clinical information specialists, management specialists
and computer specialists.
(iii)   Define organizational structure, build institutional capacity, create banking relationships,
and develop regulations for the fiscal intermediary required to pool funds from different
budget sources.
(iv)   Procure computer hardware, and refine computer and accounting systems for the fiscal
intermediary. Computer systems would handle enrollment of the population, construction
of rates for the payment systems, recording of clinical information from facility bills,
payment of providers for services, operation of a quality assurance system, and analysis
of health statistics. An accounting system would record all financial transactions from
defined source payment documents, interact with the banking system and provide
financial reports for the fiscal intermediary.
(v)    Development and implementation of a rationalization plan to close or consolidate some
facilities. Rationalization should result in initial capacity reduction, alignment of facilities
to improve competition, and savings which can be invested in more equitable payment
rates for providers.
(vi)   Refine and extend the provider payment methods developed in the Issyk-kul pilot (again,
the specific design will be adapted to the environment in Bishkek City and Chui Oblast
througlWa process of evaluating options). The provider payment systems include:
(a)    A fundholding system with FPGs as fundholders receiving a capitated rate
(predetermined fee per individual for provision of a defined set of health
services) for each person enrolled. They would provide all health services
required by their enrollees either directly or by purchasing them from specialists,



187                             ANNEX 4
diagnostic facilities, or hospitals. The capitated rate paid to FPGs would be
initially be adjusted for age and sex. FPGs would have the right to refer to any
accredited specialist or facility. To accomplish the objective of moving health
resources to the primary care sector, the FPGs would be the clinical and fmnancial
center of the health delivery system.
(b)    Payment methods for outpatient specialists and diagnostic tests. The Issyk-kul
pilot is developing a schedule of fees for different services which will serve as
the maximum amount the FPG would pay. However, these services can also be
based on rates negotiated between FPGs and specialists and providers of
diagnostic tests.
(c)    A case-based hospital payment system which could either reimburse hospitals on
a per case basis or establish a global budget based on the type and severity of
treated cases. The Medical Economic Standards system developed in Russia
should not be used. Payment on a per case basis requires a patient classification
system to group cases into categories based on clinical cohesiveness and
homogeneity of resource use. Costs are then estimated for the categories and a
relative weight calculated which reflects how much greater or less than average
the resource use is for this particular diagnosis. The relative weight for the
classified case is then used to reimburse hospitals a predetermined fee for that
particular hospital. Technical issues needing resolution include how to maintain
budget neutrality, how to avoid placing FPGs at too much risk, and
determination of other payment adjustments.
(d)    The payment systems are for operating costs only. Capital investments will
continue to be centrally allocated and can be used to enhance competition among
providers.
(e)    Determination of whether special payment approaches should be tailored to
specific categories of care under the loan program, such as tuberculosis,
ARI/CDD, or whether adjustments are needed to general payment policies for
high priority services such as preventive care.
(f)    Development of a health price index which can be used to update the payment
rates for providers.
(vii)   Approximately 100 facility managers and government officials would receive training on
the new payment methods. Intensive training would be provided for the staff of the fiscal
intermediary. This in-country training would be enhanced by a study tour on provider
payment methods.
(viii)  The Health Finance and Management Department of the Republican Department of
Continuous Medical Education (DCME) would be established as at training center for
provider payment and financial and management infrastructure. It will allow training to
be provided efficiently and build education capacity to provide the finance and
management skills needed within the health sector to sustain reforms.
51.   The fourth element is development of health sector infrastructure to support the
implementation of new provider payment systems. This infrastructure consists of three areas: 1.)



188                              ANNEX 4
integrated management information systems (MIS), such as cost accounting and clinical information
systems, to track resource use and utilization patterns and provide managers tools to improve their
decision-making ability; 2.) increased management autonomy, flexibility, and accountability for health
care providers; and, 3.) quality assurance systems to monitor care and prevent "gamning" or abuse under
different financial incentives of new provider payment systems.
52.    The project activities included in this element are as follows:
(i)    Procurement and installation of computer hardware and software for health facilities.
(ii)    Refinement and extension of the cost accounting and clinical information systems
developed in the Issyk-kul pilot. Cost accounting and clinical information systems are
needed to provide facility managers with information to make good decisions about the
type and mix of services produced by the facility. In addition, this informnation is
required by the government to determine payment rates for providers.  Finally,
improvement in financial accounting methods would allow more efficient management
of financial transactions and produce better financial reports. Training on these systems
would be provided for approximately 100 facility accountants. This in-country training
would be enhanced by a study tour on management and financial systems.
(iii)   Creation of a legal and regulatory framework to provide facilities with autonomy to
efficiently allocate payment received for health services, including the capability to hire
and fire staff and institute performance-based pay for employees. Training will be
provided for approximately 100 facility managers to help redefine organizational
structure, including the institution of some facility boards; improve resource management,
including planning and budgeting; and introduce new personnel policies.
(iv)   A licensing and accreditation program to determine the capability of a facility to provide
high quality services. The USAID-funded program is providing technical assistance to
implement a national licensing and accreditation program. The World Bank project will
fund refinement of the accreditation program, an independent Accreditation Commission,
and staff capacity to survey facilities.
(v)    Refinement and implementation of the quality assurance program developed in the Issyk-
kul pilot. Quality assurance is needed to counteract perverse financial incentives
potentially encountered under the new provider payment methods. Medical criteria and
sampling techniques would be used to ensure that referrals, hospital admissions and
discharges are appropriate. In addition, techniques for continuous quality improvement
would be implemented at the facility level.
53.    The goal of the provider payment component is to provide health services more efficiently,
producing savings which can be reinvested to improve the quality of health services and the access of the
population to health services. Determination of whether the component has achieved the desired impact
and savings in the health sector can be measured by the following indicators:
(i)    Reduction of hospital admission rate and length of stay.
(ii)    Reduction in referral rate of primary care physicians.
(iii)  Increase in proportion of health sector resources allocated to the primary care sector.
(iv)   Reduction of both number of beds and number of facilities, resulting in reduction of fixed
costs, such as utilities, in the health sector.



189                             ANNEX 4
(v)    Number of Family Group Practices formed.
(vi)   Percentage of the population enrolled in Family Group Practices.
54.   The provider payment component needs to establish an evaluation and monitoring element to
determine the status of program implementation and measure progress toward target indicators. A
baseline study of physician referrals is necessary, as this data is not currently collected. Most of the other
baseline data is available, although a lot of effort is required to collect and compile it into a form suitable
for analysis.
55.   The central Ministry of Health (MOH) would have overall responsibility for the component with
implementation managed by the Technical Coordinating Committee (TCC). The TCC would have one
full-time advisor to coordinate all provider payment activities. Close coordination is required with several
other critical organizational components -- the central Ministry of Finance (MOF) and the oblast (city in
the case of Bishkek) level administration, Department of Health, and Department of Finance. To
facilitate collaboration between the central, oblast and city level governments, Ministry/Department of
Health, and Ministry/Department of Finance, a task force would be formed with representatives of the
various stakeholders. The World Bank, TCC, and task force would continue its close collaboration with
other relevant donors -- notably USAID, ODA ant GTZ.
56.   A smooth transition of the provider payment reforms from the Issyk-kul pilot to Bishkek City and
Chui Oblast requires continuous coordination with the USAID-funded pilot over the next year. The TCC
and task force should meet regularly with the USAID-funded team, begin work on designing the program
and adapting the Issyk-kul products, communicate with and educate officials about the design,
implementation and results of the Issyk-kul pilot, and monitor the progress of the pilot program. The
three parties should continue to work to facilitate the rapid implementation of the Issyk-kul pilot by
identifying, communicating and helping to resolve any issues which arise.
57.   The conditions of negotiation for legal and regulatory requirements to implement the Issyk-kul
pilot were discussed above. In addition, two conditions of effectiveness will help ensure collaboration
and a smooth transition from the Issyk-kul pilot program. First, the TCC will evaluate the Issyk-kul
provider payments pilot program. Second, any additional legislation or regulation blocking the way to
provider payment reforms will be identified and specific recommendations made for changes in such
legislation and regulation submitted to appropriate decision-makers.
58.   The following covenants address critical issues which will improve the potential for successful
implementation of the provider payment component:
(i)    The Borrower shall keep its level of total executed budgetary financing of the health
sector, from all sources and by all means at a level not less, in real inflation-adjusted
terms, than such total executed financing in 1994.
(ii)   The Borrower shall, not later than December 31, 1996, abolish the restrictive legislation
identified under the conditions of effectiveness.
(iii)   The Borrower shall not carry out the provider payments component before reaching
agreement with the Association on the modalities, taking into account its evaluation of
the Issyk-kul pilot project.
(iv)   The Borrower shall, not later than December 31, 1997, establish and thereafter maintain
a Health Fund into which national, oblast, municipal, and rayon level health and finance



190                             ANNEX 4
authorities would contribute and out of which medical providers will be paid, together
with regularly updated guidelines on the manner and level of contribution by these
authorities.
(v)    Not later than December 31, 1997, the Borrower shall design and implement a national
health accounts system to enable the Borrower to track the allocation of resources and the
flow of funds in the health sector.
(vi)   The Borrower shall, not later than December 31, 1996, issue guidelines, acceptable to
the Association, to health authorities at all levels on leasing of publicly owned health
premises to family group practices.
(vii) The Borrower shall, not later than December 31, 1997, pass legislation allowing
establishment of primary care groups as non-profit entities and opening of bank accounts
by such groups.
(viii) The Borrower shall, not later than December 31, 1996, carry out a study of the levels
of patient referrals from primary care physicians to specialists and for diagnostic tests in
Bishkek City and Chui Oblasts.



191                            ANNEX 5
KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
HEALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
ANNEX 5: PHARMACEUTICAL MANAGEMENT AND REFORM COMPONENT
A. Introduction
1.    The availability of drugs in the Kyrgyz Republic has fallen precipitously since the breakup of the
Soviet Union in 1991, caused primarily by the collapse of public finances. According to rough estimates
by Kyrgyz Pharmacia, the partly Government-owned and still Government-controlled company that
dominates the drug wholesale market in the republic, total sales in 1995 for the country as a whole should
be approximately US$ 10 million, or a little more than US$ two dollars per capita. Annual sales
reportedly totaled 80 million Russian rubles in the late 1980s, i.e. approximately US$ 120 million at the
official exchange rate, or slightly more than US$ 26 per capita.
2.    The poor availability of medicines is significantly aggravated, however, by wasteful and cost-
ineffective pharmaceutical management. There are two major sources of waste and ineffectiveness in the
current management of pharmaceuticals in the Kyrgyz Republic. One is the inadequate regulation of
drugs. When the Kyrgyz Republic was part of the Soviet Union, all drug regulatory institutions and
functions were located in Moscow; since independence, the Republic has lacked an institutional capability
to assure the safety and efficacy of medicines produced and/or distributed locally. Moreover, the danger
of ineffective, or even unsafe, medicines being supplied may be rising, in light of the worsening
economic conditions, the growing shortage of drugs, and the unscrupulousness of low-quality
manufacturers. A more serious source of waste is undisciplined, cost-ineffective public spending. Despite
cuts in government funds for drugs, the funds that are available still are being used to buy an excessively
large number of drugs, many of which have never been proven to be effective. Expensive branded drugs
are sometimes purchased instead of less expensive, therapeutically equivalent generic products. And
because a large number of different drugs are purchased in relatively small quantities, the demand for
many important, widely used medicines often is not met, while the price that must be paid to obtain these
drugs is higher than would be the case if larger quantities of fewer drugs were purchased through
competitive bidding.
3.    The pharmaceutical management component of the project is designed to improve the availability
of essential drugs in the Kyrgyz Republic both (a) directly, by financing the procurement and distribution
of a large quantity of essential drugs, and (b) indirectly, by supporting the implementation of far-reaching
reforms designed to strengthen the Government's drug regulatory capabilities and to increase the
discipline and cost-effectiveness of public spending.  Financial support for the procurement and
distribution of essential drugs is contingent on the Government's continued determination to implement
these reforms. These reforms, taken together, will lead to a significant improvement in the availability
of essential drugs in the republic and to a permanent increase in the cost effectiveness of public and
private spending on drugs--that is, the amount of health provided by any given amount of public and/or
private resources.
4.    The financing of approximately US$ 6.0 million of essential drugs, besides helping to improve
directly the availability of needed medicines, also is intended to help improve the overall efficiency of
drug distribution, by supporting, albeit indirectly, the continued restructuring and privatization of the
government monopoly previously in charge of the drug trade in the republic. In view of the large size
of the contemplated procurement, the complex procedures that will be used to implement the procurement
and the subsequent distribution of drugs need to be described and explained in detail and that is the
primary purpose of this Annex.



192                              ANNEX 5
B. Pharmaceutical Management Reforms
5.     National Drug Regulatory Organization.  The national drug regulatory authority will be
responsible for:
(a)    the registration of all drugs and vaccines manufactured and/or marketed in the Kyrgyz
Republic, including pre-registration review of all technical information on safety and
effectiveness, the organization of clinical trials if necessary, and post-registration
monitoring of drug utilization and possible adverse drug reactions;
(b)    quality testing and quality assurance of all drugs and vaccines manufactured, imported
into, marketed, and/or otherwise distributed in the Kyrgyz Republic.
6.     A national drug regulatory organization was established in 1993 by decree of the Prime Minister.
Located within the MOH, the organization is known officially as the Administration for Standardization
and Oversight of the Quality of Medicines and Medical Technology. The authority and responsibilities
of this organization are reaffirmed in a proposed "Law on Medicines" that has been drafted. The
Administration for Standardization includes, among its subordinate departments, a national drug testing
laboratory which is responsible for (a) providing technical support of the Administration's registration
decisions and (b) ascertaining the quality of drugs manufactures, imported, and/or otherwise distributed
in the Kyrgyz Republic.
7.     The Drug Regulatory Organization and its subordinate testing laboratory, which are co-located,
have rented 340 meters of space at the Institute of Cardiology and are negotiating with the Institute to
rent an additional 160 meters of adjoining space. With a total of approximately 500 square meters of
space, the organization will have sufficient room to perform its functions. Renovation of the premises
has begun, and a small amount of testing equipment has been procured; it will be installed when
additional renovation is completed and the necessary laboratory furniture has been acquired. Current staff
of the organization numbers 15 of a planned 35. The project will provide extensive support to enable
the organization and laboratory to become fully operational as quickly as possible. The project will
provide US$97,000 for renovation of the premises and US$357,000 for the purchase of: office furniture
and equipment; laboratory testing equipment and furniture; reagents and chemical glassware; scientific
literature, computer software, and databases; and other necessary supplies.
8.     The project will finance a substantial amount of training for the staff of the organization and
laboratory, including activities designed to foster close professional relations between officials of the
organization and counterparts in drug regulatory agencies of other countries. The project will finance
in-country training in the use of computers and databases, the use of new laboratory equipment, and cost-
effectiveness analysis for approximately 75 people during the first two years of the project. The project
also will finance approximately 50 person-weeks of training and study tours abroad, spread over four
years. In order to facilitate widespread dissemination of this information within the Kyrgyz Republic and
to support a limited amount of training for persons outside the organization and laboratory, e.g. local
health care officials, the project will finance an annual two-day national conference on drug regulatory
affairs, including participation of foreign experts.
9.    The Drug Regulatory Organization presently operates as a self-financing body, relying on incoii.-
from registration and testing fees. As a result, drug regulatory officials may have a direct economic
interest in their regulatory decisions, and this may increase the risk that ineffective, or even unsafe, drugs
will be made available to patients. Part of the costs of the drug regulatory organization can and should
continue to be financed out of registration and testing fees. The labor costs of the staff that process



193                            ANNEX 5
registration application, the paper and other consumables used, and the labor and travel expenses of
inspectors should be financed out of registration fees. The costs of reagents and other consumables used
in laboratory analyses, as weli as the labor costs of technicians, likewise should be charges to the vendors
whose products are being tested. However, in order to avoid potential conflicts of interest, the
compensation of senior drug officials and other staff with a direct voice in regulatory decisions should
be financed solely and directly from the state budget. The adoption of revised procedures for financing
the Administration for Standardization, which provide that the salaries of senior officials and regulatory
decision-makers are financed by the Government and not from fees, was a condition of negotiatons.
10.   The TCC  component coordinator for pharmaceutical management will supervise the
implementation of the various activities. In cooperation with the Chief of the Drug Regulatory
Organization, other MOH officials, and the Project Coordination Unit, the TCC component coordinator
will prepare terms-of-reference for all procurement contracts, including adequately specified lists of
equipment, furniture, supplies, and other goods to be purchased with project funds. The TCC component
coordinator will prepare, in concert with the Chief of the Drug Regulatory Organization, an annual
training plan, including the dates, location, purpose, and names of participants for all in-country training
courses and foreign study tours; the TCC component coordinator will exercise personal responsibility for
assuring that all technical and logistical requirements of the training are met. The TCC component
coordinator will maintain continual communications with other donors, in particular the WHO, in order
to minimize duplication and to maximize coordination in all drug regulatory technical assistance and
training activities. Finally, the Coordinator will, each year, convene and chair a temporary organizing
committee to prepare for and to implement the annual national conference on drug regulatory affairs.
11.   Essential Drugs Program: An Essential Drugs List (EDL), numbering 300 different products,
has been adopted as a first step toward the creation of an Essential Drugs Program (EDP). Part 1 of this
list, which has received the WHO's endorsement, consists of the 195 "most widely distributed basic
medicines" that are expected to be consistently available at, or by prescription from, all primary and
secondary health care facilities. Part 2 consists of 105 "basic medicines used in specialized institutions."
Formal adoption and publication of the EDL by the MOH, was a condition of negotiations.
12.   The draft EDL was compiled by a large ad hoc committee in the MOH and is the product of a
lengthy dialogue between the MOH and experts from the WHO and the World Bank. The process for
establishing and maintaining the EDL must now be institutionalized. The "Law on Medicines" delegates
to the MOH formal authority to compile the EDL. Final passage of this law, with this authority included,
and the adoption of formal rules and procedures for compiling and maintaining the EDL, including
provisions for its periodic review and, if necessary, revision in the future, are to be accomplished prior
to December 31, 1996.
13.   In order to become an effective means of influence over the procurement and utilization of drugs,
the EDL must be made widely available, and the rationale for an EDL must be understood clearly and
supported by physicians, pharmacists, other health care professionals, and health care financing decision-
makers. The project will support development and publication of a brochure, containing the EDL and
a lengthy commentary explaining how the List was compiled and its purpose. The project will finance
the printing and distribution of several thousand copies of the brochure, which will be made available to
all pharmacists and health facilities prior to the start of the sizable procurement of essential drugs under
the project. The project will help to finance a workshop in each oblast prior to effectiveness, in order
to introduce the EDL to local health leaders and to build professional support for an Essential Drugs
Program. In the second and fourth years of the project, revised editions of the EDL will be published
and distributed.



194                            ANNEX 5
14.   Equally necessary for effective implementation of the EDL is the development and dissemination
of new, standard medical treatment guidelines that are compatible and consistent with the limited range
of drugs included on the List. The MOH already has begun to draft new treatment guidelines, and the
project will support a study tour prior to effectiveness, designed to further this effort.
15.   Maximum benefit of the EDL will be achieved when the List becomes a principal mechanism for
regulating public spending on drugs. However, a requirement that public spending for drugs be limited
to the EDL is, for the time being, impracticable. Two years will be needed in order to train health care
professionals and to win their support and cooperation, but also in order to gain experience with the List
and to revise the List in light of this experience, as will inevitably be required. From the start of the
third year of the project, public spending on drugs will be limited to the EDL: from that date, state-
owned health facilities will be permitted to buy, and specially "entitled" ambulatory patients will be
permitted to receive free of charge or at subsidized prices, only drugs included on the Kyrgyz Republic's
Essential Drugs List. Duning negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government that it will
formally adopt and effectively implement this rule by the beginning of 1998. At the samne time, no
restrictions will be placed on the privately-financed purchase, distribution, and sale of medicines which
are not included on the EDL.
16.   The establishment and operations of the Kyryyz Republic's Essential Drugs Program will be
undertaken and supervised by a standing commission or committee appointed by the Minister of Health,
consistent with authority provided by the proposed "Law on Medicines" and as provided by the rules to
be adopted on compiling and maintaining the EDL. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that
the formation of this committee will be done by December 31, 1996. The TCC component coordinator
for pharmaceutical management will be a member of this body and will coordinate the various project
activities intended to support implementation of the EDP. In particular, the TCC component coordinator
will: (a) convene and chair a small, informal working group to oversee production and distribution of the
EDL; (b) maintain close communications with the WHO, UNICEF, and other donors in order to ensure
that foreign assistance in the development of new standard treatment guidelines is used with minimum
duplication and maximum effectiveness; and (c) convene and chair an informal working group to design
and implement a monitoring program which will include regular surveys of pharmacies and physicians
in order to measure the availability and utilization of essential drugs.
C. Drug Distribution in the Kyrgyz Republic
17.   The Old System: Until recently, the procurement and distribution of pharmaceuticals in the
Kyrgyz Republic was the exclusive responsibility of Pharmacia, a centralized, vertically integrated, semi-
commercial government corporation headquartered in Moscow that, during the Soviet period,
monopolized the drug trade throughout the former' Soviet Union. As in every other union republic,
Kyrgyz Pharmacia consisted of a central office and warehouse in the republican capital, Bishkek (then
known as Frunze); regional offices and warehouses at the oblast level; and a large network of hospital,
inter-hospital, and community (retail) pharmacies. Although concentrated disproportionately in the largest
cities, pharmacies were numerous and widely dispersed, so that medicines were generally accessible to
patients and health facilities alike, even in rural areas. The system was administered by a large cadre of
trained professional pharrnacists and pharmacy technicians.
18.   There being almost no domestic pharmaceutical industry in the Kyrgyz Republic, approximately
96-98% of the drugs distributed locally were--and are still--imported. Most shipments were delivered
to Kyrgyz Pharmacia's central warehouse in Bishkek, where they were divided into lots and onward
shipped to the oblast Pharmacias which, in turn, acted as distributors for the pharmacies and health care
facilities located in their respective regions. In Chui Oblast, the regional Pharmacia recently was



195                             ANNEX 5
disbanded; the national office of Kyrgyz Pharmacia now supplies pharmnacies and health care facilities
located in Chui Oblast, as well as those in Bishkek city, out of its central warehouse.
19.   At the point of distribution to patients, the pharmacy system functioned--and continues to
function--like that in most countries. In hospitals, physicians ordered or prescribed drugs which were
supplied by the hospital pharmacy or, if the hospital was too small to warrant its own pharmacy, by the
inter-hospital pharmacy designated as the hospital's regular supplier. [It should be remembered that these
pharmacies, although physically located in the hospitals, belonged to the Pharmacia system, not the
Ministry of Health or local health department.] In similar fashion, out-patients could have their
prescriptions filled at any retail pharmacy, although some pharmacies specialized in particular illnesses
or patient groups.
20.   Relatively few patients paid for their medicines. By law, all drugs dispensed in hospitals were
(are) supposed to be provided to patients free of charge; and there was (is) a long list of ambulatory
patients similarly entitled to receive medicines free of charge or at heavily subsidized prices. The
entitlement list has been frequently amended but usually has consisted of approximately 20 categories of
illness--e.g. dysentery, bronchial asthma, AIDS--and anywhere from 15 to 30 population groups-e.g.
World War n veterans, Chernobyl victims, children up to the age of three. Overall, before the breakup
of the Soviet Union, probably 80-90% of all drugs consumed in the republic were dispensed to patients
at no charge.
21.   However, while mostly free to patients, drugs were not free--at least not on paper--to the
pharmacies and health care facilities that dispensed them. The hospital "bought" drugs from its pharmacy
at controlled prices equal to the pharmacy's acquisition cost plus a standard, fixed markup to cover
operating expenses. The hospital generally "paid" the pharmacy out of its own budget, although
sometimes the pharmacy was paid directly by the city or oblast health department. Funds needed to cover
these costs, i.e. the fiscal cost of drugs supplied to in-patients, have long been appropriated separately
under Article 10 of the republic and oblast health budgets. A similar payment mechanism functioned in
the case of exempted ambulatory patients. Every month, retail pharmacies billed the city or oblast health
department for the cost of drugs dispensed to exempted out-patients, again based on prices equal to
acquisition cost plus a standard markup. The anticipated fiscal cost of treating these patients was included
in Article 10 budgets as well. [Pharmacies also sold drugs for cash to patients who were not exempt
from payment, although these receipts reportedly constituted a relatively small portion of total revenues.]
22.   The financing of drug procurement was based largely on this system of inter-organizational
payments. Hospital and retail pharmacies bought drugs from their oblast Pharmacia, paying out of
revenues received from their local health department (for the treatment of exempted patients) and from
cash-paying customers. Oblast Pharmacias, in turn, botight their drugs from Kyrgyz Pharmacia's republic
warehouse and paid out of the revenues they received from their pharmacies. The Kyrgyz Republic
Pharmacia bought drugs from the national Pharmacia in Moscow. In this fashion, the procurement and
distribution of drugs were financed indirectly by: (1) the republic's consolidated health budget line
appropriations for drugs, i.e. the sum of all Article 10 appropriations; and (2) a comparatively much
smaller amount of private spending on drugs.
23.   In addition, given the controls on prices, finances at each level of the system and in each
institution were expected to be more or less in balance; that is, for any given oblast Pharmacia or
individual pharmacy, total revenues ought to have been approximately equal to total operating (non-
capital) costs. In practice, the system's finances were often not in balance. Some Pharmacias and
pharmacies earned "excess" revenues which, if discovered, generally were confiscated by the tax
authorities; others accumulated arrears, caused by various contingencies, including late or non-payment



196                             ANNEX 5
by local health authorities. Although the managers of loss-making enterprises might be disciplined, or
even fired on occasion, the arrears apparently had no financial consequences nor any effect on the supply
of drugs. At the end of the year, financial imbalances were invariably cleared and debts written off;
excess earners had their surplus taken away, those in debt were given added funds, and the cycle began
again.
24.   Post Independence: When the Soviet Union broke up and Kyrgyzstan became an independent
republic, this accounting system suddenly began to work as a financing system as well. The republic
Pharmacia's debts no longer were covered automatically by Moscow, and the republic's traditional
suppliers--mostly Russian and Ukrainian manufacturers--no longer would ship drugs to the Bishkek
warehouse without strong assurances they would be paid. Increasingly, pre-payment in a convertible
currency has been required. In order to maintain the same volume of supply as before, either the
republican company (now known as Kyrgyz Pharmacia) needs to receive sufficient income from its
subordinate companies and to be able to convert the revenue into a useable currency; or the Kyrgyz
Govermnent needs to make up the shortfall directly, either from its budget or by means of a credit. In
fact, economic depression and the Government's strong fiscal stabilization policy have, in combination,
prevented the direct subsidization of Kyrgyz Pharmacia and have led, in addition, to a significant decline
in public spending overall, including consolidated Article 10 appropriations. As a result, receipts to
Kyrgyz Pharmacia and to each oblast Pharmacia, financed out of public spending and measured in
constant prices, have fallen substantially.
25.   As public spending on drugs has fallen, private spending has grown. Informal sources claim that
as much as 50 percent or more of the drugs consumed in the republic's hospitals are paid for out of
pocket by the patients or their families. In a 1994 survey sponsored by the British foreign assistance
agency, ODA, nearly three-fifths of 285 respondents who reported having been hospitalized in the
previous year claimed to have spent at least US$ five dollars to obtain drugs prescribed during their
hospital stay. Nevertheless, total spending on drugs has, by all available evidence, declined dramatically.
Kyrgyz Pharmacia and the oblast Pharmacias are still the only significant distributors in the republic, and
their current budgets are but a fraction of what they were previously, before independence.
26.   At the level of health care facilities and patients, the availability of drugs has worsened
commensurately. Although most hospitals, especially in major urban centers, reportedly have been able
to maintain minimum supplies of essential drugs, it supposedly has become a common practice, especially
in "elective" procedures, to present the patient a list of necessary supplies, including drugs, which the
patient him or herself must supply before care is provided. Previously, it was thought that the policy of
free in-patient drugs was a cause of excessive hospitalization rates and long average lengths of stay; now,
the diminishing supply of drugs probably is a cause of the decline observed in the use of hospital services.
Exempted ambulatory patients have experienced a similar decline in availability. Some physicians
reportedly have reduced their prescribing, not wanting to add to the frustrations of their patients or to
their facilities' debts; and some pharmacies reportedly have refused to dispense drugs to exempted
patients, often claiming stock outages. Supplies nominally reserved for exempt patients reportedly have
been diverted from state to the new private pharmacies where they are sold at market-clearing prices.
27.   Kyrgyz Government Policy:  In response to the worsening drug situation, the Kyrgyz
Government is making what appears to be a serious effort to break up the Pharmacia system. The plan
is to make each level of the system independent, to fully commercialize the various successor companies,
and gradually to privatize them. The central office and warehouse, Kyrgyz Pharmacia, and the five
oblast Pharnnacias have each been made into an independent, joint-stock company. Most hospital
pharmacies have been formally transferred to the facilities in which they are located or to the
corresponding oblast health department. At the retail level, some pharmacies have been auctioned off



197                             ANNEX 5
for cash, others have been privatized "administratively," i.e. given to their employees, and still others
remain the property of their parent Pharmacias. Kyrgyz Pharmacia, for example, operates 33 pharmacies
in the city of Bishkek. In addition, a number of new, private pharmacies have appeared, mainly in
Bishkek where, according to Kyrgyz Pharmacia officials, new entrants may account for as much as 30
percent of local sales.
28.   The restructuring of Pharmacia is far from complete.  Kyrgyz Pharmacia and the oblast
companies still are the only significant local distributors active in the republic. Also, the financial
condition of most of the system reportedly is weak, aggravated in particular by the Govermnent's
insistence that all of the inherited debt be fully apportioned to the successor companies. Especially
vulnerable are the oblast Pharmacias which reportedly owe Kyrgyz Pharmacia approximately US$5
million for previous deliveries, and whose warehouses are described as nearly empty for lack of working
capital. Moreover, despite decentralization, the influence of government in Pharmacia operations remains
very strong. For examnple, on paper only 35 percent of the stock of Kyrgyz Pharmacia belongs to the
national Government; another 35 percent has been assigned to the company's employees; and the
remaining 30 percent supposedly has been sold to private investors, including several foreign firms. Yet,
the shares assigned to employees have not yet been distributed, leaving management solidly in control
of those votes; neither management nor the Government will identify the private investors who have
bought shares; and it is abundantly clear to any casual observer that informal relations between senior
managers at Kyrgyz Pharmacia and senior officials in the Ministry of Health and elsewhere in government
remain extremely close and cooperative. At the oblast level, where the level of government ownership
is said to be as high as 70 percent, relations must be even tightel
29.   On the other hand, the drug distribution system obviously is in transition, and the changes made
so far are significant and clearly in the right direction, for there can be no doubt that a privately-owned,
competitive system of drug wholesalers and retailers is likely to operate far more efficiently and cost
effectively than one run by the Government. At the same time, the effects of continued strong
government control over the Pharmacia companies should not be misconstrued or overstated. The oblast
governments in Naryn and Osh, for example, may exercise a decisive voice in the operations of "their"
Pharmacias, as strong or stronger than the national Government's voice in Kyrgyz Pharmacia. But these
three governments are today much less unified and in sync with one another than they were previously
under Communism. More important, the Pharmacia monopoly has been broken, and competition at all
levels is rising. The oblast Pharmacias no longer buy exclusively from Kyrgyz Pharmacia, but
increasingly are engaging in direct negotiations with traditional Pharmacia suppliers (chiefly Russian
manufacturers) and with the old and new distributors that have proliferated throughout the NIS. Kyrgyz
Pharnacia, for its part, has begun to compete with its former oblast subordinates over supply contracts
with pharmacies and health care facilities. Probably it is only a matter of time before the oblast
Pharmacias reach out across oblast borders and begin to compete directly with one another.
30.   Apart from organizational questions, the Government's response to the worsening drug situation
has been much more tepid. Particularly disappointing has been the marked lack of interest shown in
reviewing and revising the exemptions policy that promises free in-patient drugs to all and free out-patient
drugs to as much as two-thirds of the population. The effects of this exemptions policy and the prospects
of change will be discussed in more detail below. Here, the following should be said. First, although
very generous on paper, the Government's drug entitlement policy has become seriously under-funded
in practice, with the result that fewer patients than before are getting free or subsidized drugs, while
many formally exempt patients are having to pay for them. However, the cuts in supply have been
implemented in an ad hoc, and therefore haphazard, f-shion. As a result, some relatively affluent
patients who could--and would--pay for their drugs still are getting them free of charge, while many very
poor patients are having to pay or to do without. Meanwhile, free drugs still are being dispensed in cases



198                             ANNEX 5
of non-urgent care, when in cases requiring immediate attention the necessary drugs are unavailable.
Such outcomes are neither equitable nor cost-effective in terms of health policy.
31.   Nor is the system any more rational in terms of budget and finance policy, for there is virtually
no connection between the cost of the exemptions and actual spending decisions. The Ministry of Health
continues to calculate annual drug needs of the population--i.e., what is required to satisfy the
Government's obligations in this area--and to convey this information to the Parliament. But the Ministry
has made no effort to rank in order of priority the exemptions or associated demand for drugs, and
because resources are wholly inadequate to fully fund the Ministry's requests, the national Parliament's
Article 10 appropriations have been based mainly on the previous year's budget and the current year's
anticipated revenues. At the same time, decisions by the national Parliament are mainly indicative, not
prescriptive, for most health spending is budgeted and financed locally where the cost of exemptions is
not calculated and where the connection between these costs and spending decisions is virtually non-
existent.  In theory, the substance of an entitlements policy and its financing should be closely
coordinated. In practice, the Kyrgyz Government's present approach to drug entitlements precludes such
coordination, virtually assuring that the cost of patient exemptions and the financing of those exemptions
will be permanently out of sync.
32.   Summary: There exists in the Kyrgyz Republic a well-developed drug distribution system that
is professionally run and easily accessible to health facilities and most of the population. This system is
in transition, and while much ground remains to be covered, the pace of change is impressive and its
direction is decidedly progressive. The weaknesses in the current system, apart from its incomplete
restructuring, should not be minimized. There probably are too many retail pharmacies in operation, and
some will likely have to be closed as uneconomical. The system's technological level is generally low;
accounting, pricing, anti inventory operations especially need to be computerized. The professional
training of most pharmacists is similarly inadequate. The growing emphasis on primary and preventive
care, combined with recent advances in drug therapy, demand that pharmacists play an increasingly active
role in treatment decisions, but this will require that local pharmacists receive much more preparation in,
for example, clinical pharmacology. Perhaps most serious for the immediate future is the near-total
absence of professionals trained in the economics, finances, and management of drug procurement and
distribution.
33.   However, on balance the strengths of the existing system seem more impressive than its
weaknesses, and this has decided implications for the project. Clearly it is unnecessary, and probably
it would be counterproductive, to encourage the formation of a new mechanism to handle the procurement
and distribution of essential drugs financed under the project. Indeed, care should be taken not to impede
or undermine the generally progressive changes now taking place in the Pharmacia system. Particularly
undesirable, from the standpoint of thefuture effectiveness of the Kyrgyz Republic's health system, would
be the inadvertent reconstitution of a government monopoly in charge of the drug trade. In addition,
there are embedded in this system well-established principles and mechanisms of accounting and
financing, including means of cost recovery. Cost recovery in practice is limited by the Government's
exemption policy and will improve only when that policy is changed and fewer patients are entitled to
receive free drugs. Sufficient mechanisms for cost recovery already exist, however, and do not need to
be created or encouraged.



199                             ANNEX 5
D. The Procurement and Distribution of Essential Drugs Under the Project
34.   The project will make available approximately $US 6.9 million (including contingencies) to
finance the purchase and distribution of essential drugs. The purpose is twofold: to help ensure minimum
health standards during the most wrenching phase of the country's transition to democracy and a market
economy, when social and economic dislocations are the most severe; and to reinforce needed reforms
specifically in the health sector, aimed at raising the efficiency and cost-effectiveness with which public
funds are used.
35.   There exist, in principle, a variety of ways by which essential drugs might be procured and
distributed. The Association and the Government have agreed that several key principles must be
observed, regardless of the method chosen:
(i)    Only the most essential, cost-effective drugs should be purchased in
order to maximize the health effects of the available funds;
(ii)    Drugs procured under the project should be sold to distributors, and then
to the public, at near-market prices in order to prevent their diversion to
neighboring countries or the black market;
(iii)   Prices to health care facilities should be limited in order to maximize availability;
(iv)   Final retail prices to ambulatory patients, except in the case of exempted
patients, should be unrestricted in order to limit market distortions and
to raise the maximum amount of additional private spending;
(v)    Drugs procured under the project should be made equally available to all
regions in the republic;
(vi)   Drugs procured under the project should be made equally available to all
onward distributors and retailers, regardless of their form of ownership.
36.   With these principles in mind, and taking into account the strengths, weaknesses, and ongoing
changes in the existing drug distribution system, the Association and the Government have agreed
tentatively to procure and distribute drugs financed under the project in the following manner. All final
arrangements must be reviewed and approved by the Association prior to effectiveness. In addition, these
rules and procedures will be reviewed by the Association at the end of each procurement cycle and
revised, if necessary, by mutual agreement with the Kyrgyz Government, so as to ensure that new,
financially viable, privately-owned drug wholesalers and retailers are in no way disadvantages, compared
to remaining state-owned companies.
Procurement
37.   (1) In order to minimize supply shocks, the $US 6.9 million to be provided under the project
(including contingencies) will be spent in four annual tranches of approximately $US 2.25 million in each
of the first two project years and $US 1.2 million annually in the third and fourth years. (2) Drugs will
- be procured on the basis of International Competitive Bidding and in accordance with World Bank rules
on pharmaceutical procurement and standard World Bank documentation. (3) Procurement will be
executed by the Project Coordinating Unit (PCU). (4) Drugs procured under the project will be selected
exclusively from Part 1 of the Essential Drug List of the Kyrgyz Republic, "A List of the Most Widely



200                              ANNEX 5
Distributed Basic Medicines.' (5) A list of drugs to be purchased under each tranche will be approved
by the Association and published in Development Business prior to preparation of the bidding documents.
(6) To be eligible for consideration, drugs must be registered in the republic before the evaluation of
proposals. (7) In order to give potential vendors sufficient time to register their drugs, bidding documents
for the initial tranche will be prepared prior to effectiveness or six months after the initial procurement
list is published, whichever comes later; in the case of subsequent tranches, there will be at least a 4-
month period between publication of the initial procurement list and preparation of the bidding
documents. (8) Procurements will be divided into six lots, according to a formula approved by the
Association prior to effectiveness; winning vendors will deliver shipments, in accordance with this
formula, to Naryn Oblast Pharmacia, Issyk-kul Oblast Pharmacia, Osh Oblast Pharmacia, Talas Oblast
Pharmacia, Dzhalal-Abad Oblast Pharmacia, and Kyrgyz Pharmacia (for onward distribution in Chui
Oblast and Bishkek city)--hereafter, these recipients are called the "Distributors"; bidders' prices will
include the cost of transportation and delivery to these destinations. (9) Drugs will be sold to the
Distributors on credit, based on an agreement to be included in the bidding documents and approved by
the Association ptior to effectiveness. (10) All drugs must be labeled in Russian; all packages must
include a standard insert in Russian; all individual packs, packages, and shipping containers must be
labeled; and all labels must indicate the product is an "Essential Drug of the Kyrgyz Republic."
38.   (11) Distributors will divide the shipments further and sell individual lots to hospitals, other health
care facilities, and community pharmacies at a price no higher than the acquisition cost plus a 5-percent
markup for storage and handling; Distributors will be permitted to refuse to sell or ship to any institution
or organization unable to assure full payment or reimbursement. (12) Kyrgyz Pharmacia will onward
ship drugs to health care facilities and pharmacies in Chui oblast and Bishkek city, according to rules
agreed to by the PCU; these rules will be reviewed by the Association prior to effectiveness. (13) Retail
pharmacies will be permitted to sell drugs procured under the project to non-exempt ambulatory patients
at unrestricted prices. (14) Revenues received by Distributors from the sale of drugs procured under the
project will be taxed in accordance with a special written formula agreed to by the Ministry of Health
and the Ministry of Finance; this formula will reserve fixed percentages of gross revenues for (a) the
retirement of the Distributors' outstanding debts and (b) additions to the Distributors' working capital;
the formula must be approved by the Association prior to effectiveness.
Alternative Methods of Distribution
39.   As noted, there are other ways that essential drugs might be procured and distributed under the
project; when examined closely, however, these turned out to be either significantly more complicated
and expensive administratively, at odds with one or more of the goals or principles of the project as
described above, or simply impractical. For example, one proposal considered at length was to establish
a Drug Revolving Fund (DRF), a quasi-public corporation that would attempt to sell as many of the drugs
as possible in order to maximize cost recovery. The idea ultimnately was rejected for several reasons.
Since there already exists in the Kyrgyz Republic a well-developed, accessible, and professionally
operated drug distribution system, there would seem to be little advantage in creating a new structure,
particularly one that would require considerable oversight by the Government and the Association.
Moreover, in view of the restructuring and ongoing privatization of the existing system, it surely would
be counterproductive to at this point in time encourage the Government to get back into the drug trade.
Finally, as previously discussed, there already exist well-established principles and mechanisms of cost
recovery. What limits cost recovery in practice is the Government's exemption policy, and the creation
of a DRF would not, by itself, alter this fact.
40.   Another possibility would be for the Government to procure drugs through the PCU, as currently
envisioned; and then to sell the drugs at cost to Kyrgyz Pharmacia and to let Kyrgyz Pharmacia distribute



201                             ANNEX 5
them according to general guidelines and/or specific tasks, as agreed to in a contract with the
Government. The chief difficulty with this idea is that Kyrgyz Pharmacia very likely would choose not
to participate, i.e. would not buy the drugs at a price equal to the procurement cost, because it probably
would not be profitable to do so. [If it were profitable, then Kyrgyz Pharmacia would not need the
Association to help finance the procurement.] One reason is that even generic products bought under the
project are likely to be significantly more expensive than many of the drugs Kyrgyz Pharmacia presently
buys from Russian manufacturers--drugs which, in most cases, will not be eligible for procurement under
the project because the factories in question do not meet the standards of Good Manufacturing Practices
(GMPs). Another reason is that because the drugs purchased under the project all will be Essential
Drugs, many will end up being dispensed to exempted in- or out-patients. [Even if there was sufficient
demand from non-exempt patients, it would be wholly impractical to try to sell project-financed drugs
to cash-paying customers, while distributing free of charge to exempt patients the very same drugs
procured with budget funds.] The ability of distributors and pharmacies to make money from the sale
of project-financed drugs will depend significantly on the ability and willingness of local health authorities
to use their Article 10 funds to buy these products.
41.   A third possibility, closely related to the second, would be for the Government to onward sell
the drugs to Kyrgyz Pharmacia at subsidized prices, e.g. through a concessionary on-lending arrangement
similar to what is presently envisioned vis-a-vis the oblast Pharnacias. The main objection here is
political, namely: why undermine the progressive restructuring of the Pharmacia system that is now
taking place, by giving the entire subsidy to Kyrgyz Pharmacia which already dominates the wholesale
market and which appears to be doing reasonably well financially, particularly when compared to the
now-independent oblast Pharmacias? If a subsidy is to be provided-as probably it must, if project-
financed drugs are to be onward distributed--surely it would be more sensible to try to reinforce the
progressive changes occurring in the republic's pharmaceutical market by giving most of the subsidy to
Kyrgyz Pharmacia's prospective, but financially weaker, competitors.
42.   A fourth possibility would be for the Government to procure drugs through the PCU, perhaps
hiring Kyrgyz Pharmacia physically to receive shipments; and then to auction the drugs to any and all
bidders, subject only to the provision that they subsequently distribute the drugs exclusively within the
Kyrgyz Republic. For reasons already discussed, it is likely that the drugs distributed in this manner
would be sold at prices significantly below their procurement cost, so that the buyers would in effect
receive a subsidy. However, in this case the Government would not be able to choose the recipients of
the subsidy (Kyrgyz Phannacia would likely gamer a sizable percentage), nor would it be able to prevent
the subsequent distribution of the drugs disproportionately in the republic's richest markets (i.e. Bishkek)
to the disadvantage of other, less wealthy regions.
E. The Kyrgyz Government's Drug Exemptions Policy
43.   It has been noted several times previously that potentially adequate mechanisms of cost recovery
for drugs bought with public funds already exist, and that cost recovery has been limited severely in
practice by the Government's generous policy of exempting large numbers of patients from paying the
full cost of their drugs. Although this policy is now seriously under-funded, which has led to rationing
in practice, the cuts in funding and supplies have been distributed in a largely ad hoc, haphazard fashion
that has been both inequitable and cost-ineffective in its effects. The rationalization of this policy, i.e.
the reduction of exemptions and/or their cost to what the Government's consolidated health budget can
presently afford, would very likely have a net positive impact on the population's health status, by
increasing the cost-effectiveness of public spending on drugs. Rationalization of the present exemptions
policy would, in addition: (a) remove a significant barrier to entry into the drug trade by private
entrepreneurs who fear becoming saddled with obligations that could threaten their profitability; (b)



202                              ANNEX 5
eliminate a major cause of the financial weakness of existing pharmacies and drug wholesalers; and (c)
lead to an increase in private spending on drugs by patients and institutions that may be able and willing
to buy drugs on the open market "out of pocket," but that are presently able to satisfy their drug
requirements at less than full cost by taking advantage of the Government's subsidy. [The need to re-
connect the substance of the exemptions policy with the financing of this policy, as discussed earlier, is
a separate issue from the need to reduce the fiscal cost of the exemptions policy, although changes in
either one will affect the other, and so both issues will have to be considered together.] Given these
prospective benefits, the question needs to be asked, to what extent can or should changes in the
Governments's exemptions policy be encouraged under the project?
44.    Analytically, the fiscal cost of this policy might be reduced in one or both of two ways: either
the list of exemptions (exempted patients) can be cut; or the cost of those exemptions can be reduced,
e.g by introducing co-financing by recipients (co-payments, deductibles) and/or by restricting eligibility
to the poor through means-testing. Either approach is very likely to run into difficult political and
technical obstacles in practice. Thus, there are strong public health reasons for keeping the treatment of
communicable diseases free; there are equally strong political reasons for continuing to exempt war
veterans; and there are good economic and ethical reasons for exempting high-cost cancer drugs that
could quickly bankrupt even the most affluent of patients. As evidenced already by the long struggle to
compile the Essential Drugs List, every exemption category will have its defenders, on professional and
political grounds, and paring the list in more than a symbolic way will be no mean feat. Nor will it be
much easier to reduce the cost of the policy. For example, a very nominal, standard dispensing fee could
be introduced, payable by all recipients; but it would be difficult politically to institute a fee large enough
to raise a significant amount of money. Anything more elaborate--e. g. a variable co-payment (deductible)
based on the price of the drug or the income of the patient--could lead to undesirable prescribing and/or
dispensing behavior, or would become enmeshed in the same problems involved in efforts to limit
eligibility (see below).
45.    The most commonly advocated--and, at first glance, seemingly the simplest and most powerful-
way to rationalize exemptions policy would be to limit exemptions to the poor and to require all others
to pay for their drugs. [However, even at the level of general principles, there will be opponents arguing
that health care, including the provision of essential drugs, has always been and should remain a "right,"
not a commodity.] Ordinarily, this would be a goal for the future, not a task for the present, because
it usually is extremely complicated administratively and expensive to implement means-testing in a
country like the Kyrgyz Republic, where a very high percentage of personal income normally is
concealed, corruption is rife, and cooperation between different agencies of government is minimal.
Nevertheless, in the course of the "social safety net" project, also supported by the World Bank, two
broad classes of families have been identified, based on their income and need for supplementary public
assistance; this same classification system, some have suggested, could be used to limit eligibility for drug
benefits, and perhaps other social benefits as well. Thus, families receiving aid from the Social Security
Ministry also could be designated as eligible to receive free or subsidized drugs, while all others could
be required to pay for their drugs out of pocket.
46.   In fact, instituting such a policy would be difficult. For one thing, it is unlikely that non-exempt
patients would be made to pay for the treatment of serious communicable diseases, and the continued full
exemption of these categories of patients will reduce the fiscal savings of such a change in policy. Much
more important, however, is the fact that the cost of drugs for individuals and families--like the cost of
other medical services, but unlike the cost of food, shelter, clothing, and transportation--is highly variable
and unpredictable. In any given year, a few individuals and families will need no drugs; many will need
drugs costing a small amount; some will need drugs costing more; a few will need drugs costing a lot;
and a very few will need drugs costing an enormous amount.  Accordingly, there will be strong



203                              ANNEX 5
pressures: (a) to cap the financial risk of any individual or family, by establishing a ceiling on monthly
out-of-pocket expenditures for drugs covered by whatever exemption categories remain; and (b) to grade
an individual's or family's financial risk according to income or cost of treatment, by introducing a
variable co-payment required of non-exempt patients, applicable until the ceiling on total out-of-pocket
expenditures has been reached, and based on the patients' income. Instituting even a simple cap on
financial risk, although a simple concept in theory, would be a huge administrative headache and
expensive; the complexity and cost of more elaborate schemes would rise geometrically.
47.   In the present context it might still make sense to press for introduction of the simple, two-class
system for regulating exemptions, if it could be shown that the financial savings would be significant.
Unfortunately, this information does not presently exist. Indeed, not even crude estimates of the potential
savings can be calculated until: (a) reliable data are collected on drug utilization at the patient level; and
(b) a decision is made concerning which exemption categories will be maintained even for the [non-
exempt" (i.e. families not receiving public assistance).
48.   There is within the Government and among health professionals widespread awareness that the
current exemptions policy is far more expensive than the budget presently can afford and that it needs
to be changed. However, there is no consensus yet as to how this should be done, nor does the issue
appear to be a high priority on the Government's agenda. Whether there is sufficient political will to
tackle this issue now is, therefore, open to question.
F. Expected Impact of the Project
49.   Ironically, the fiscal cost of the Government's present exemptions policy should decline under
the project regardless, as a consequence of the introduction of the Essential Drugs Programn, and
particularly at the start of the third year of tl . project when public spending will be limited exclusively
to those drugs contained on the Essential Drugs List. As discussed previously, a significant, sustained
improvement in the cost-effectiveness of public spending on drugs is the most important expected benefit
of the pharmaceutical management component. In addition, the procurement and distribution of $US 6.9
million of essential drugs will have an impact on the physical availability of drugs during the project, on
the composition of available supply, and on the financial viability of the distribution system; and these
impacts need to be considered separately.
50.   Total Physical Supply. Because the drugs are to be sold--either for cash or for reimbursement
from Article 10 funds--there is likely to be only a modest increase in the total physical supply of drugs
in the republic as a result of the project. Consider the following: unless drugs are given away, total
physical supply will be a function of aggregated public and private spending. As a condition of the
project, the Government will assure that aggregated public spending will fall no lower than 1994 levels
measured in constant prices. There already is a sizable amount of private spending; and the project will
almost certainly have both positive and negative first-order effects on that spending. Specifically, the
impact will be negative, insofar as some cash-paying patients will be able to get the same drugs they are
buying now more cheaply; and it will be positive, insofar as other cash-paying patients are likely to spend
more in order to obtain needed drugs that should be more available and more attractively priced than
before. Sellers, in the meantime, will want to maximize their profits; if they are unsure about the
prospects of reimbursement by local governments, they will likely try to divert as much as possible of
their stock to cash-paying customers and to price their drugs at market-clearing levels. Intuitively, it
seems likely that the net impact of the project will be (1) to increase total private spending, and (2) to
depress prices somewhat (which will raise the buying power of both public and private spending); the
total physical supply of drugs should rise as a result.



204                               ANNEX 5
51.    Composition of Total Supply. Because drugs procured under the project will be Essential
Drugs, a significant percentage is likely to be dispensed to exempted patients and, in turn, financed out
of local health budgets. Thus, if total public spending for drugs remains constant, a higher percentage
of that spending will be used to buy Essential Drugs--specifically those drugs procured under the project-
even prior to the third year of the project when the Government is required to limit public spending
exclusively to the drugs contained on the republic's Essential Drugs List. By the same token, a smaller
percentage of aggregated public spending will be available to support the purchase of drugs NOT supplied
under the project. In addition, because the oblast Pharmacias will be receiving drugs at very low cost,
it may become more difficult for other distributors to sell the same drugs profitably, i.e. at a price high
enough to recover their acquisition costs. Thus, it is possible that alternative sourcing of the drugs
procured under the project could decline, particularly if quantities procured under the project represent
a substantial portion of annual demand for the drugs in question. These uncertainties and risks with
respect to the project's impact on the composition of the republic's drug supply--because the magnitude
of these effects are nearly impossible to predict in advance, and because supplies of widely used drugs
could be disrupted--are reasons why the lists of drugs to be procured under the project need to be chosen
carefully and why disbursement is best spread out over multiple tranches.
52.    Distribution System. The project's most immediate impact on the drug distribution system will
be the re-capitalization of that system's weakest link: the oblast Pharmacias. Although still majority
government-owned, these companies are now nominally independent and are supposed to be operating
as commercial entities; eventually they are to be fully privatized. For now, however, the oblast
Pharmacias appear to lack both financing and drugs: their warehouses reportedly are empty; they are
deeply in debt; and they have no access to short-term credit which they might use to rebuild their
inventories. By providing these regional distributors with a substantial supply of low-cost drugs, the
project will, in effect, be giving these companies a new lease on life. If the oblast Pharmacias are
permitted to retain a significant portion of their earnings from the onward sale of these drugs, it is
possible than one or more of the five will survive and develop into a financially viable distributor with
improved prospects of escaping government control and becoming a genuinely private competitor of
Kyrgyz Pharmacia. There is, of course, no certainty in this result, although the potential gain surely
warrants the effort. For by supporting the establishment of a drug distribution system based on privately-
owned, competitive wholesalers and retailers, the project can effect a sustained improvement in the
efficiency of drug distribution in the republic and, in turn, a sustained increase in the availability of
drugs.
